    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] Vertex shaders transform geometry positions - [x] The coordinate space chain: model -> world -> view -> clip -> screen - [x] <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span> is the required output (clip-space position) - [x] Other outputs are interpolated by the rasterizer for the fragment shader - [x] Uniform matrices (model, view, projection) control the camera - [x] The full-screen triangle trick avoids vertex buffers for 2D effects - [x] Vertex shaders can animate geometry (wobble, explode, bounce)

    The foundation

    • Vertex shader = runs once per corner point and decides where that corner lands on screen.
    • Vertex vs fragment = vertex places geometry, fragment colors pixels; the rasterizer fills the gap between them.
    • Coordinate spaces = model to world to view to clip to screen — the staged journey from an object's own space to pixels.
    • Matrix = a move/rotate/scale machine; multiplying a point by one transforms it between spaces.
    • <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span> output = the required clip-space result; everything else you output gets interpolated for the fragment shader.
    • Vertex descriptor = the Swift-side map telling Metal how your vertex data (position, uv, normal) is laid out in memory.
    • Geometry animation = move the corners with sin, normals, or springs to wobble, explode, or bounce a mesh.
    • Full-screen triangle = the shortcut for 2D effects: no real geometry, just one big triangle covering the screen.
    Prev← 06 — Fragment Shaders Deep DiveNext08 — Compute Shaders (Kernels) →

    08 — Compute Shaders (Kernels)

    Start here — the plain idea

    A fragment shader is locked to the drawing pipeline — its job is to color pixels for triangles on screen. A compute shader throws that constraint away. Think of it as "renting the GPU's thousands of tiny cores to run any small function, on every item of a big pile of data, all at once."

    The mental model: you have a huge grid of work — say every pixel of an image, or every particle in a simulation. The GPU spawns one thread per cell of that grid, and each thread runs your kernel function. Each thread's only superpower is knowing its own coordinate (thread_position_in_grid) — "I am the thread for pixel (412, 88)." From that it reads input, does its math, and writes output. A million threads, each doing one tiny job, finishing in a blink.

    Because you control everything, compute shaders can read and write the same texture, share scratch memory between nearby threads for speed, and crunch plain arrays that have nothing to do with images. It's the GPU as a general parallel calculator.

    Compute shaders are the most flexible shader type. They're not tied to the rendering pipeline — they're general-purpose GPU programs. Apple uses them for image processing, blur effects, physics simulation, and machine learning.

    Compute vs Render Shaders

    RENDER PIPELINE:
  Vertex Shader -> Rasterizer -> Fragment Shader -> Framebuffer
  Fixed flow, designed for drawing triangles/pixels

COMPUTE PIPELINE:
  Kernel Function -> Read/Write Textures/Buffers
  Free-form, you control everything

    When to use compute shaders: - Image processing (blur, filters, effects) - When you need to READ and WRITE to the same texture - When you need to coordinate between threads (threadgroup memory) - Data processing that doesn't map to rendering (physics, sorting)

    Basic Compute Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

// kernel = compute shader entry point
kernel void brighten(
    texture2d<float, access::read>  inTexture  <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,   // Read-only input
    texture2d<float, access::write> outTexture <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(1)</span>,   // Write-only output
    uint2 gid <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>)                        // Which pixel am I?
{
    // Bounds check — don't write outside the texture
    if (gid.x >= outTexture.get_width() || gid.y >= outTexture.get_height()) {
        return;
    }

    // Read pixel at this position
    float4 color = inTexture.read(gid);

    // Process it
    color.rgb *= 1.5;  // 50% brighter
    color.rgb = saturate(color.rgb);  // Clamp to [0, 1]

    // Write result
    outTexture.write(color, gid);
}

    Thread Model

    The GPU organizes work into a grid of threads. You control the grid dimensions.

    GRID (total work)
┌───────────────────────┐
│ ┌───┐ ┌───┐ ┌───┐    │
│ │TG │ │TG │ │TG │    │  TG = Threadgroup
│ │0,0│ │1,0│ │2,0│... │
│ └───┘ └───┘ └───┘    │
│ ┌───┐ ┌───┐ ┌───┐    │
│ │TG │ │TG │ │TG │    │
│ │0,1│ │1,1│ │2,1│... │
│ └───┘ └───┘ └───┘    │
│ ...                    │
└───────────────────────┘

Each Threadgroup:
┌──────────────┐
│ t0  t1  t2 ..│  Each t = one thread = one invocation of your kernel
│ t16 t17 t18..│  Threads within a group can share memory
│ ...          │
└──────────────┘

    Thread ID Attributes

    kernel void myKernel(
    uint2 gid   <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>,          // Global thread ID (which pixel)
    uint2 tid   <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_threadgroup</span>,    // Local ID within group
    uint2 tgid  <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">threadgroup_position_in_grid</span>,      // Which threadgroup
    uint2 tgSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">threads_per_threadgroup</span>,          // Threadgroup dimensions
    uint  lid   <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_index_in_threadgroup</span>        // Linear index within group
) {
    // gid is what you use most — it tells you which pixel to process
}

    Swift Side: Dispatching Compute Work

    class ComputeProcessor {
    let device: MTLDevice
    let commandQueue: MTLCommandQueue
    let pipeline: MTLComputePipelineState

    init() {
        device = MTLCreateSystemDefaultDevice()!
        commandQueue = device.makeCommandQueue()!

        let library = device.makeDefaultLibrary()!
        let function = library.makeFunction(name: "brighten")!
        pipeline = try! device.makeComputePipelineState(function: function)
    }

    func process(input: MTLTexture, output: MTLTexture) {
        let commandBuffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!
        let encoder = commandBuffer.makeComputeCommandEncoder()!

        encoder.setComputePipelineState(pipeline)
        encoder.setTexture(input, index: 0)
        encoder.setTexture(output, index: 1)

        // Thread group size (typically 8x8 or 16x16)
        let threadGroupSize = MTLSize(width: 16, height: 16, depth: 1)

        // How many thread groups needed to cover the texture
        let threadGroups = MTLSize(
            width:  (input.width  + threadGroupSize.width  - 1) / threadGroupSize.width,
            height: (input.height + threadGroupSize.height - 1) / threadGroupSize.height,
            depth: 1
        )

        encoder.dispatchThreadgroups(threadGroups, threadsPerThreadgroup: threadGroupSize)
        encoder.endEncoding()

        commandBuffer.commit()
        commandBuffer.waitUntilCompleted()  // Synchronous — or use addCompletedHandler for async
    }
}

    Choosing Thread Group Size

    // Option 1: Fixed size (simple, works everywhere)
let threadGroupSize = MTLSize(width: 16, height: 16, depth: 1)

// Option 2: Optimal size from pipeline (Apple recommended)
let w = pipeline.threadExecutionWidth  // Usually 32 on Apple GPUs
let h = pipeline.maxTotalThreadsPerThreadgroup / w
let threadGroupSize = MTLSize(width: w, height: h, depth: 1)

// Option 3: dispatchThreads (automatic, iOS 11+)
// Let Metal figure out the optimal grouping
encoder.dispatchThreads(
    MTLSize(width: texture.width, height: texture.height, depth: 1),
    threadsPerThreadgroup: threadGroupSize
)
// With this, you STILL need the bounds check in the kernel

    Threadgroup Memory (Shared Memory)

    Threads within a threadgroup can share data via threadgroup memory. This is much faster than reading from textures or device memory.

    kernel void boxBlur(
    texture2d<float, access::read>  inTex  <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    texture2d<float, access::write> outTex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(1)</span>,
    uint2 gid <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>,
    uint2 tid <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_threadgroup</span>)
{
    // Shared tile: 18x18 for a 16x16 group with 1-pixel border
    threadgroup float4 tile[18][18];

    // Each thread loads its pixel into shared memory
    int2 loadPos = int2(gid) - 1 + int2(tid);
    loadPos = clamp(loadPos, int2(0), int2(inTex.get_width()-1, inTex.get_height()-1));
    tile[tid.y + 1][tid.x + 1] = inTex.read(uint2(loadPos));

    // Border threads load extra pixels
    if (tid.x == 0)  tile[tid.y + 1][0] = inTex.read(uint2(max(int(gid.x)-1, 0), gid.y));
    if (tid.x == 15) tile[tid.y + 1][17] = inTex.read(uint2(min(gid.x+1, inTex.get_width()-1), gid.y));
    if (tid.y == 0)  tile[0][tid.x + 1] = inTex.read(uint2(gid.x, max(int(gid.y)-1, 0)));
    if (tid.y == 15) tile[17][tid.x + 1] = inTex.read(uint2(gid.x, min(gid.y+1, inTex.get_height()-1)));

    // BARRIER: wait for all threads to finish loading
    threadgroup_barrier(mem_flags::mem_threadgroup);

    // Now compute 3x3 box blur from shared memory (fast!)
    float4 sum = float4(0);
    for (int y = 0; y < 3; y++) {
        for (int x = 0; x < 3; x++) {
            sum += tile[tid.y + y][tid.x + x];
        }
    }
    sum /= 9.0;

    outTex.write(sum, gid);
}

    threadgroup_barrier

    This is critical: it ensures ALL threads in the group have reached this point before any thread continues. Without it, you might read data that another thread hasn't written yet.

    // All threads load data
tile[tid.y][tid.x] = someData;

// Wait for everyone
threadgroup_barrier(mem_flags::mem_threadgroup);

// Now safe to read any position in tile
float4 neighborData = tile[tid.y + 1][tid.x];

    Read-Write Textures

    Sometimes you need to read AND write the same texture:

    kernel void inPlaceInvert(
    texture2d<float, access::read_write> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    uint2 gid <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>)
{
    float4 color = tex.read(gid);
    color.rgb = 1.0 - color.rgb;
    tex.write(color, gid);
}

    Swift side — texture must be created with .shaderRead and .shaderWrite usage:

    let descriptor = MTLTextureDescriptor.texture2DDescriptor(
    pixelFormat: .rgba8Unorm,
    width: width,
    height: height,
    mipmapped: false
)
descriptor.usage = [.shaderRead, .shaderWrite]
let texture = device.makeTexture(descriptor: descriptor)!

    Buffer Processing (Non-Image Data)

    Compute shaders can process any data, not just images:

    struct Particle {
    float2 position;
    float2 velocity;
    float life;
};

kernel void updateParticles(
    device Particle *particles <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant float &deltaTime <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>,
    uint id <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>)
{
    Particle p = particles[id];

    // Physics update
    p.velocity.y += 9.8 * deltaTime;    // Gravity
    p.position += p.velocity * deltaTime; // Move
    p.life -= deltaTime;                  // Age

    // Bounce off ground
    if (p.position.y > 1.0) {
        p.position.y = 1.0;
        p.velocity.y *= -0.6;  // Bounce with energy loss
    }

    particles[id] = p;
}

    Exercises

    1. Grayscale kernel: Write a compute shader that converts an image to grayscale using luminance weights.

    2. Pixelation: Write a compute shader that pixelates an image. Hint: round the read position to a grid: uint2 pixelated = (gid / blockSize) * blockSize.

    3. Edge detection: Implement a Sobel edge detection kernel using threadgroup memory for the 3x3 neighborhood.

    The foundation

    • kernel = the entry-point function of a compute shader; it runs once per thread, off the drawing pipeline.
    • thread = one invocation of your kernel, responsible for one item of work (usually one pixel).
    • grid = the full set of work items; threadgroup = a small block of threads that can share fast scratch memory.
    • thread_position_in_grid = the global coordinate that tells each thread which pixel or item it owns.
    • threadgroup memory = a shared scratchpad for one group of threads — far faster than re-reading the texture.
    • threadgroup_barrier = a "everyone wait here" line, so no thread reads shared data before all threads have written it.
    • dispatch = the Swift-side act of launching the grid: set textures/buffers, compute how many groups cover the data, and fire.
    • access::read / write / read_write = how a kernel declares it will read, write, or do both to a texture.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] Compute shaders (kernel) are general-purpose GPU programs - [x] <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span> tells each thread which data to process - [x] Thread groups organize threads; threads within a group can share memory - [x] threadgroup_barrier synchronizes threads within a group - [x] Dispatch from Swift: set textures/buffers, calculate thread groups, dispatch - [x] access::read, access::write, access::read_write for textures - [x] Compute shaders can process buffers (arrays), not just images

    Prev← 07 — Vertex ShadersNext09 — Textures and Sampling →

    09 — Textures and Sampling

    Start here — the plain idea

    A texture is just an image the GPU can read — usually a photo or a pattern you want to show or process. Sampling is the act of asking that image "what color is at this spot?"

    The catch is that you don't ask in pixels, you ask in fractions. UV coordinates run 0 to 1 across the image regardless of its actual size — (0,0) is one corner, (1,1) the opposite — so your code doesn't care whether the picture is 256 or 4000 pixels wide. Think of it like asking "give me the color 30% across and 70% down" instead of naming an exact pixel.

    When you ask for a spot that falls between real pixels, the sampler decides how to answer: nearest snaps to the closest pixel (crisp, blocky — good for pixel art), linear blends the neighbors (smooth — good for photos). And when you ask for a spot outside the image, the address mode decides what to do: clamp the edge, tile it, or mirror it. A sampler is basically that little rulebook for "how do I answer in-between and out-of-bounds questions."

    Textures are how images get into your shaders. Sampling is how you read pixels from them — with filtering, wrapping, and interpolation.

    Loading Textures from Swift

    import MetalKit

// From UIImage
let textureLoader = MTKTextureLoader(device: device)
let options: [MTKTextureLoader.Option: Any] = [
    .textureUsage: MTLTextureUsage.shaderRead.rawValue,
    .textureStorageMode: MTLStorageMode.shared.rawValue,
    .SRGB: false  // Set true if image is sRGB
]
let texture = try textureLoader.newTexture(cgImage: uiImage.cgImage!, options: options)

// From file in bundle
let texture = try textureLoader.newTexture(name: "myImage", scaleFactor: 1.0,
                                            bundle: nil, options: options)

// From URL
let texture = try textureLoader.newTexture(URL: imageURL, options: options)

    Creating Empty Textures

    let descriptor = MTLTextureDescriptor.texture2DDescriptor(
    pixelFormat: .rgba8Unorm,  // 8 bits per channel, normalized [0,1]
    width: 1024,
    height: 1024,
    mipmapped: false
)
descriptor.usage = [.shaderRead, .shaderWrite]
descriptor.storageMode = .shared  // CPU and GPU accessible

let texture = device.makeTexture(descriptor: descriptor)!

    Common Pixel Formats

    Format Bits Range Use
    .rgba8Unorm 8/ch [0, 1] Standard images
    .rgba16Float 16/ch [-65504, 65504] HDR, intermediate buffers
    .rgba32Float 32/ch full float range Scientific, max precision
    .r8Unorm 8 total [0, 1] Masks, grayscale
    .rg16Float 16/ch, 2 ch float range Normal maps, motion vectors
    .bgra8Unorm 8/ch [0, 1] Screen output (default drawable)

    Sampling in Shaders

    Sampler Object

    // Inline sampler (defined in shader — most common)
constexpr sampler texSampler(
    coord::normalized,          // UVs in [0, 1]
    address::clamp_to_edge,     // Out-of-bounds handling
    filter::linear              // Interpolation
);

float4 color = myTexture.sample(texSampler, uv);

    Coordinate Modes

    coord::normalized   // UVs range [0, 1] — resolution independent
coord::pixel        // UVs in actual pixel coordinates [0, width]

    Address Modes (What Happens Outside [0,1])

    clamp_to_edge:        repeat:              mirrored_repeat:
┌──────┐              ┌──────┬──────┐      ┌──────┬──────┐
│AAAAAB│              │ABCABC│ABCABC│      │ABCCBA│ABCCBA│
│AAAAAB│              │DEFDEF│DEFDEF│      │DEFFED│DEFFED│
│CCCCCD│              │ABCABC│ABCABC│      │ABCCBA│ABCCBA│
└──────┘              └──────┴──────┘      └──────┴──────┘
Stretches last pixel   Tiles the image      Tiles with mirror flip

clamp_to_zero:
┌──────┐
│ ABCD │   Returns 0 (transparent black) outside bounds
│ EFGH │
│      │
└──────┘

    Filter Modes

    filter::nearest:                filter::linear:
┌──┬──┬──┐                     ┌──┬──┬──┐
│██│  │██│  Sharp, pixelated   │▓▒│░▒│▒▓│  Smooth, blended
│  │██│  │  Each pixel is a    │░▓│▓▒│▓░│  Pixels are interpolated
│██│  │██│  solid block        │▓▒│░▒│▒▓│  with neighbors
└──┴──┴──┘                     └──┴──┴──┘

Use nearest for: pixel art, data textures
Use linear for: photos, smooth effects

    Reading Textures Without a Sampler

    // Direct read — integer coordinates, no filtering
float4 color = texture.read(uint2(x, y));
// No interpolation, no address mode
// Fastest, but must use exact pixel coordinates

// Get texture dimensions
uint width = texture.get_width();
uint height = texture.get_height();

    Use .read() in compute shaders. Use .sample() in fragment shaders.

    Multi-Texture Effects

    fragment half4 blendTextures(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> texA <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    texture2d<half> texB <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(1)</span>,
    constant float &blendFactor <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);

    half4 colorA = texA.sample(s, in.uv);
    half4 colorB = texB.sample(s, in.uv);

    return mix(colorA, colorB, half(blendFactor));
}

    Using a Texture as a Lookup Table (LUT)

    Color grading in Photos, Instagram filters — many use LUT textures:

    fragment half4 lutFilter(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> photo <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    texture3d<half> lut <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(1)</span>)  // 3D LUT texture
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);
    constexpr sampler lutSampler(coord::normalized, filter::linear);

    half4 color = photo.sample(s, in.uv);

    // Use RGB as coordinates into the 3D LUT
    half4 graded = lut.sample(lutSampler, float3(color.rgb));

    return graded;
}

    Texture Coordinates Tricks

    Flip

    float2 flippedH = float2(1.0 - uv.x, uv.y);  // Horizontal flip
float2 flippedV = float2(uv.x, 1.0 - uv.y);  // Vertical flip

    Tile

    float2 tiled = fract(uv * float2(3.0, 3.0));  // 3x3 tiling

    Zoom Into Center

    float zoom = 2.0;
float2 zoomed = (uv - 0.5) / zoom + 0.5;  // 2x zoom into center

    Offset / Pan

    float2 panned = uv + float2(time * 0.1, 0.0);  // Scroll right over time

    Aspect-Correct Fit

    // Fit texture into view preserving aspect ratio (like .aspectFit)
float2 aspectFit(float2 uv, float2 viewSize, float2 textureSize) {
    float viewAspect = viewSize.x / viewSize.y;
    float texAspect = textureSize.x / textureSize.y;

    float2 scale;
    if (texAspect > viewAspect) {
        scale = float2(1.0, viewAspect / texAspect);
    } else {
        scale = float2(texAspect / viewAspect, 1.0);
    }

    return (uv - 0.5) / scale + 0.5;
}

    Exercises

    1. Texture zoom: Load an image and implement pinch-to-zoom by passing a zoom level from Swift.

    2. Scrolling texture: Make a texture scroll horizontally over time using UV offset.

    3. Texture mask: Use a second grayscale texture as a mask — show texture A where mask is white, texture B where mask is black.

    4. Kaleidoscope: Create a kaleidoscope effect by mirroring and rotating UV coordinates before sampling.

    The foundation

    • texture = an image the GPU can read from (and sometimes write to).
    • UV coordinates = fractional positions from 0 to 1 across the image, so your code is resolution-independent.
    • sampling = reading a color at a UV; .sample() interpolates and obeys the sampler, .read() grabs an exact pixel with no filtering.
    • sampler = the rulebook for in-between and out-of-bounds reads (coordinate mode, address mode, filter).
    • filter mode = how to answer between pixels: nearest snaps (crisp), linear blends (smooth).
    • address mode = what to do outside [0,1]: clamp the edge, repeat, mirror, or return transparent.
    • pixel format = how each pixel's bits are stored (e.g. rgba8Unorm for normal images, rgba16Float for HDR/intermediate).
    • LUT = a lookup-table texture you index by a pixel's own color to remap it — how many photo filters work.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] Loading textures from Swift via MTKTextureLoader - [x] Creating empty textures with MTLTextureDescriptor - [x] Sampler configuration: coord, address, filter modes - [x] .sample() (with sampler, interpolated) vs .read() (direct pixel access) - [x] Common pixel formats and when to use each - [x] UV manipulation: flip, tile, zoom, pan, aspect-correct - [x] Multi-texture effects and LUT-based color grading

    Prev← 08 — Compute Shaders (Kernels)Next10 — Buffers and Uniforms: Passing Data from Swift to Metal →

    10 — Buffers and Uniforms: Passing Data from Swift to Metal

    Start here — the plain idea

    A shader runs on the GPU and can't reach into your app to grab the current time or where the user's finger is. So you have to hand it that data. This lesson is about the delivery channels.

    Think of it like passing notes into a sealed room. For a tiny note — a single number, a touch point, a small struct — you slide it under the door (setBytes). For a fat envelope — thousands of particles, a big array — you set up a dedicated mail slot once and keep refilling it (MTLBuffer). A uniform is just a value that's the same for every pixel this frame (time, intensity, resolution), as opposed to per-pixel data the shader computes itself.

    The one gotcha that bites everyone: Swift and Metal must agree byte for byte on how a struct is laid out in memory, and they pad fields to alignment boundaries. The classic trap is float3, which secretly takes 16 bytes, not 12. Get the layout wrong and your shader reads garbage — so the alignment rules here matter more than they look.

    Every interactive shader needs data from your app — time, touch position, slider values, arrays of data. This lesson covers all the ways to get data into your shaders.

    Method 1: setBytes (Small Data, Simplest)

    For data under 4KB. No buffer object needed.

    Swift

    // Single value
var time: Float = 2.5
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&time, length: MemoryLayout<Float>.size, index: 0)

// Vector
var resolution = SIMD2<Float>(Float(width), Float(height))
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&resolution, length: MemoryLayout<SIMD2<Float>>.size, index: 1)

// Struct
struct Params {
    var time: Float
    var resolution: SIMD2<Float>
    var touchPoint: SIMD2<Float>
    var intensity: Float
    var padding: Float = 0  // Align to 16 bytes
}
var params = Params(time: 1.0, resolution: .init(400, 800), touchPoint: .init(200, 400), intensity: 0.5)
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&params, length: MemoryLayout<Params>.size, index: 0)

    Metal

    struct Params {
    float time;
    float2 resolution;
    float2 touchPoint;
    float intensity;
    float padding;
};

fragment float4 myShader(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    constant float &time <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,           // Single value
    constant float2 &resolution <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>,    // Vector
    constant Params &params <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>         // Or struct
) { ... }

    Struct Alignment Rules

    Metal and Swift must agree on memory layout. Key rules:

    float    = 4 bytes, align to 4
float2   = 8 bytes, align to 8
float3   = 16 bytes, align to 16 (NOT 12!)
float4   = 16 bytes, align to 16
float4x4 = 64 bytes, align to 16
bool     = avoid (use int or float instead)

    The float3 trap: float3 takes 16 bytes (same as float4), not 12. This catches everyone:

    // WRONG — misaligned!
struct Bad {
    var position: SIMD3<Float>  // 16 bytes (not 12!)
    var color: SIMD4<Float>     // Offset is wrong if you assumed 12
}

// RIGHT — use SIMD4 or add explicit padding
struct Good {
    var position: SIMD4<Float>  // 16 bytes, clear
    var color: SIMD4<Float>     // 16 bytes
}

// Or match Metal's packed_float3:
// Metal: packed_float3 = 12 bytes, but this requires careful Swift handling

    Method 2: MTLBuffer (Large Data, Reusable)

    For data over 4KB, or data you update frequently.

    Swift

    // Create buffer once
let bufferSize = MemoryLayout<Params>.size
let buffer = device.makeBuffer(length: bufferSize, options: .storageModeShared)!

// Update every frame
let params = buffer.contents().bindMemory(to: Params.self, capacity: 1)
params.pointee.time = elapsedTime
params.pointee.resolution = SIMD2<Float>(Float(width), Float(height))

// Bind to shader
encoder.setFragmentBuffer(buffer, offset: 0, index: 0)

    Array Data

    struct Particle {
    var position: SIMD2<Float>
    var velocity: SIMD2<Float>
    var color: SIMD4<Float>
}

let particleCount = 10000
let bufferSize = MemoryLayout<Particle>.stride * particleCount
let particleBuffer = device.makeBuffer(length: bufferSize, options: .storageModeShared)!

// Fill with data
let particles = particleBuffer.contents().bindMemory(to: Particle.self, capacity: particleCount)
for i in 0..<particleCount {
    particles[i] = Particle(
        position: .init(Float.random(in: 0...1), Float.random(in: 0...1)),
        velocity: .init(Float.random(in: -1...1), Float.random(in: -1...1)),
        color: .init(1, 0, 0, 1)
    )
}

encoder.setFragmentBuffer(particleBuffer, offset: 0, index: 0)

    Metal

    struct Particle {
    float2 position;
    float2 velocity;
    float4 color;
};

fragment float4 drawParticles(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    constant Particle *particles <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant int &particleCount <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>)
{
    float4 color = float4(0);

    for (int i = 0; i < particleCount; i++) {
        float dist = distance(in.uv, particles[i].position);
        float glow = 0.001 / (dist * dist + 0.001);
        color += particles[i].color * glow;
    }

    return saturate(color);
}

    Method 3: Triple Buffering (Smooth Animation)

    For data that updates every frame, use 3 buffers to avoid GPU/CPU conflicts:

    class TripleBufferedRenderer {
    let buffers: [MTLBuffer]
    var currentBuffer = 0
    let semaphore = DispatchSemaphore(value: 3)  // Allow 3 frames in flight

    init(device: MTLDevice, size: Int) {
        buffers = (0..<3).map { _ in
            device.makeBuffer(length: size, options: .storageModeShared)!
        }
    }

    func updateAndDraw(encoder: MTLRenderCommandEncoder, params: Params) {
        semaphore.wait()  // Wait if 3 frames are already in flight

        // Write to current buffer (GPU is reading from the other 2)
        let buffer = buffers[currentBuffer]
        buffer.contents().storeBytes(of: params, as: Params.self)

        encoder.setFragmentBuffer(buffer, offset: 0, index: 0)

        // After GPU finishes, signal the semaphore
        let sem = semaphore
        encoder.parentCommandBuffer?.addCompletedHandler { _ in
            sem.signal()
        }

        currentBuffer = (currentBuffer + 1) % 3
    }
}

    Common Data Patterns

    Time + Resolution (Minimum for Animated Shaders)

    struct AnimationUniforms {
    var time: Float
    var deltaTime: Float
    var resolution: SIMD2<Float>
    var frameCount: UInt32
    var padding: SIMD3<Float> = .zero  // Pad to 16-byte boundary
}

    Interactive (Touch/Mouse)

    struct InteractiveUniforms {
    var time: Float
    var resolution: SIMD2<Float>
    var mousePosition: SIMD2<Float>    // Normalized [0,1]
    var mouseDown: Float               // 1.0 or 0.0 (avoid Bool)
    var mouseDragStart: SIMD2<Float>
}

    Multi-Pass (Blur needs two passes)

    struct BlurUniforms {
    var direction: SIMD2<Float>   // (1,0) for horizontal, (0,1) for vertical
    var radius: Float
    var resolution: SIMD2<Float>
}

    Exercises

    1. Color picker: Pass an RGB color from Swift (3 sliders) and fill the screen with that color.

    2. Touch glow: Pass a touch position and draw a glowing circle that follows the finger.

    3. Array visualization: Pass an array of 20 float values and draw them as a bar chart in the shader.

    The foundation

    • uniform = a value you pass from Swift into the shader, the same for every pixel this frame (time, resolution, a slider value).
    • setBytes = the quick way to slip small data (<4KB) into the shader with no buffer object needed.
    • MTLBuffer = a reusable chunk of GPU memory for large or frequently-updated data, like a refillable mail slot.
    • struct alignment = Swift and Metal must lay out a struct identically, byte for byte, including padding.
    • the float3 trap = float3 occupies 16 bytes, not 12; assume 12 and every field after it is misaligned.
    • constant vs device = constant marks read-only data; device marks data the shader may also write.
    • triple buffering = rotating among three buffers so the CPU can write next frame's data while the GPU still reads this frame's.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] setBytes for small data (<4KB), MTLBuffer for large/reusable data - [x] Struct alignment: float3 = 16 bytes, match layouts between Swift and Metal - [x] constant qualifier for read-only data, device for read-write - [x] Triple buffering for smooth frame-by-frame updates - [x] Avoid Bool in shader structs; use Float or Int32 instead

    Prev← 09 — Textures and SamplingNext11 — Structs and Attributes →

    11 — Structs and Attributes

    Start here — the plain idea

    When you draw real geometry (not just full-screen effects), each shape is a list of vertices — points that carry data like position, a texture coordinate, a normal, a color. This lesson is about getting that per-vertex data to flow cleanly from Swift, through the vertex shader, into the fragment shader.

    Picture an assembly line. Swift hands over a buffer of raw bytes. The vertex shader receives one vertex at a time as a tidy struct (<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>), transforms it, and outputs a VertexOut. Then the rasterizer does something almost magical: for every pixel inside a triangle, it blends the three corner vertices' values automatically. A pixel halfway between a red corner and a blue corner arrives at the fragment shader already purple. That free blending — interpolation — is why you only specify data at corners but get smooth gradients across the whole face.

    The fiddly part is the handshake: a vertex descriptor tells Metal exactly where each field sits in your Swift struct (which byte offset, which <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(n)</span> slot). Same alignment rules as the last lesson — get an offset wrong and the GPU reads the wrong field.

    How to define custom data structures for the vertex pipeline and ensure correct data flow between Swift, vertex shaders, and fragment shaders.

    The Data Flow

    Swift (CPU)                    Vertex Shader (GPU)         Fragment Shader (GPU)
===========                    ==================         ===================
Vertex Buffer  ──────────>     VertexIn <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>
(raw bytes)                         |
                                    | (your transform code)
                                    v
                               VertexOut  ──────────>      VertexOut <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>
                               (position, uv, etc.)        (interpolated by rasterizer)
                                                                 |
Uniform Buffer ──────────>     <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(n)</span>          ──>  <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(n)</span>
(params, matrices)                                              |
                                                                v
Textures       ──────────>     <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(n)</span>         ──>  <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(n)</span>
                                                                |
                                                           return float4 (color)

    Defining Vertex Structs

    Swift Side

    struct Vertex {
    var position: SIMD3<Float>      // 12 bytes (padded to 16)
    var texCoord: SIMD2<Float>      // 8 bytes
    var normal: SIMD3<Float>        // 12 bytes (padded to 16)
    var color: SIMD4<Float>         // 16 bytes
}
// Total stride: use MemoryLayout<Vertex>.stride (accounts for padding)

    Metal Side

    struct VertexIn {
    float3 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(0)</span>;
    float2 texCoord <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(1)</span>;
    float3 normal   <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(2)</span>;
    float4 color    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(3)</span>;
};

    Vertex Descriptor (Connects Them)

    let descriptor = MTLVertexDescriptor()

// Position: float3 at attribute 0
descriptor.attributes[0].format = .float3
descriptor.attributes[0].offset = 0
descriptor.attributes[0].bufferIndex = 0

// TexCoord: float2 at attribute 1
descriptor.attributes[1].format = .float2
descriptor.attributes[1].offset = MemoryLayout<SIMD3<Float>>.stride  // 16 (padded)
descriptor.attributes[1].bufferIndex = 0

// Normal: float3 at attribute 2
descriptor.attributes[2].format = .float3
descriptor.attributes[2].offset = MemoryLayout<SIMD3<Float>>.stride + MemoryLayout<SIMD2<Float>>.stride
descriptor.attributes[2].bufferIndex = 0

// Color: float4 at attribute 3
descriptor.attributes[3].format = .float4
descriptor.attributes[3].offset = MemoryLayout<SIMD3<Float>>.stride * 2 + MemoryLayout<SIMD2<Float>>.stride
descriptor.attributes[3].bufferIndex = 0

// Layout stride
descriptor.layouts[0].stride = MemoryLayout<Vertex>.stride
descriptor.layouts[0].stepFunction = .perVertex

pipelineDescriptor.vertexDescriptor = descriptor

    Vertex-to-Fragment Structs

    The VertexOut struct is the bridge between vertex and fragment shaders:

    struct VertexOut {
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>;   // REQUIRED: screen position
    float2 texCoord;                // Texture UV (interpolated)
    float3 worldNormal;             // World-space normal (interpolated)
    float3 worldPosition;           // World-space position (interpolated)
    float4 color;                   // Vertex color (interpolated)
    float  pointSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">point_size</span>; // For point rendering
};

    The rasterizer automatically interpolates all fields (except <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>) across the triangle face. A pixel at the center of a triangle gets the average of the three vertices' values.

    Flat Interpolation (No Interpolation)

    struct VertexOut {
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>;
    float4 color <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">flat</span>;  // NOT interpolated — uses the "provoking vertex" value
    int materialID <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">flat</span>; // Useful for per-face IDs
};

    Multiple Vertex Buffers

    You can split vertex data across multiple buffers for flexibility:

    // Buffer 0: positions only
// Buffer 1: normals + UVs
// Buffer 2: per-instance data

descriptor.attributes[0].bufferIndex = 0  // Position from buffer 0
descriptor.attributes[1].bufferIndex = 1  // TexCoord from buffer 1
descriptor.attributes[2].bufferIndex = 1  // Normal from buffer 1

descriptor.layouts[0].stride = MemoryLayout<SIMD3<Float>>.stride  // Buffer 0 stride
descriptor.layouts[1].stride = MemoryLayout<SIMD2<Float>>.stride + MemoryLayout<SIMD3<Float>>.stride  // Buffer 1

// Per-instance data
descriptor.attributes[3].bufferIndex = 2
descriptor.layouts[2].stride = MemoryLayout<SIMD4x4<Float>>.stride
descriptor.layouts[2].stepFunction = .perInstance  // Once per instance, not per vertex
descriptor.layouts[2].stepRate = 1

    Instanced Rendering

    Draw the same geometry many times with different transforms:

    struct InstanceData {
    float4x4 modelMatrix;
    float4 color;
};

vertex VertexOut instancedVertex(
    VertexIn in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    constant InstanceData *instances <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(2)</span>,
    uint instanceID <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">instance_id</span>)
{
    VertexOut out;
    InstanceData instance = instances[instanceID];

    float4 worldPos = instance.modelMatrix * float4(in.position, 1.0);
    out.position = viewProjection * worldPos;
    out.color = instance.color;
    out.texCoord = in.texCoord;

    return out;
}

    // Draw 1000 instances of the same mesh
encoder.drawIndexedPrimitives(
    type: .triangle,
    indexCount: mesh.indexCount,
    indexType: .uint16,
    indexBuffer: mesh.indexBuffer,
    indexBufferOffset: 0,
    instanceCount: 1000
)

    Exercises

    1. Colored mesh: Create a simple quad with per-vertex colors and see the interpolation.

    2. Instanced particles: Draw 500 small quads using instanced rendering, each at a random position with a random color.

    The foundation

    • vertex = one point of geometry carrying data (position, UV, normal, color).
    • VertexIn / <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span> = the struct of per-vertex data the vertex shader receives, one vertex per run.
    • VertexOut = the struct the vertex shader emits; its <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span> field is required, the rest get interpolated.
    • interpolation = the rasterizer auto-blending corner values across a triangle, so a mid-face pixel gets the weighted average.
    • <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">flat</span> = opt out of interpolation; the pixel just takes one vertex's value (useful for per-face IDs).
    • <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(n)</span> = the slot number that ties a struct field to a vertex-descriptor entry.
    • vertex descriptor = the map telling Metal each field's format, byte offset, and buffer, so it can unpack your Swift bytes.
    • instanced rendering = drawing the same geometry many times with per-instance data (e.g. a different matrix and color each time).

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Vertex data flows: Swift buffer -> <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span> -> VertexOut -> interpolated -> fragment
    • [x] MTLVertexDescriptor maps Swift struct layout to Metal <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">attribute(n)</span>
    • [x] Alignment matters: float3 = 16 bytes, match strides exactly
    • [x] <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">flat</span> disables interpolation for per-face data
    • [x] Multiple buffers and instanced rendering for complex scenes
    Prev← 10 — Buffers and Uniforms: Passing Data from Swift to MetalNext12 — Color Effects →

    12 — Color Effects

    Start here — the plain idea

    These are the friendliest shaders to write, because each pixel only looks at itself. No neighbors, no geometry — the shader gets one color in and returns one color out. That's the whole job. Brightness multiplies it, invert flips it, sepia tints it. This is exactly the machinery behind Instagram filters and the adjustments in Photos.

    A few patterns repeat. Luminance — the dot product of RGB with weights like (0.21, 0.72, 0.07) — collapses a color to a single perceived-brightness number; grayscale, threshold, and saturation all lean on it (the green weight is highest because our eyes are most sensitive to green). And mix(a, b, amount) is the universal dimmer: it slides between the original and the adjusted version, so almost every effect gets a strength knob for free.

    Because each effect is just color-in, color-out, you can stack them — contrast, then saturation, then a vignette — like running a photo through a chain of filters. Think of it as a pixel passing down a conveyor belt of small tweaks.

    The simplest and most useful shader effects — transform pixel colors without reading neighbors. These are what Instagram filters, photo editors, and Apple's Photos app use.

    Grayscale

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 grayscale(float2 pos, half4 color) {
    // Perceptual luminance weights (ITU-R BT.709)
    half luma = dot(color.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    return half4(luma, luma, luma, color.a);
}

    Brightness

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 brightness(float2 pos, half4 color, float amount) {
    // amount: 0 = black, 1 = original, 2 = double bright
    return half4(color.rgb * half(amount), color.a);
}

    Contrast

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 contrast(float2 pos, half4 color, float amount) {
    // amount: 0 = flat gray, 1 = original, 2 = high contrast
    half3 adjusted = (color.rgb - 0.5h) * half(amount) + 0.5h;
    return half4(saturate(adjusted), color.a);
}

    Saturation

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 saturation(float2 pos, half4 color, float amount) {
    half luma = dot(color.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    half3 adjusted = mix(half3(luma), color.rgb, half(amount));
    return half4(adjusted, color.a);
}

    Hue Rotation

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 hueRotate(float2 pos, half4 color, float angle) {
    float cosA = cos(angle);
    float sinA = sin(angle);

    half3x3 hueMatrix = half3x3(
        half3(0.213h + cosA * 0.787h - sinA * 0.213h,
              0.213h - cosA * 0.213h + sinA * 0.143h,
              0.213h - cosA * 0.213h - sinA * 0.787h),
        half3(0.715h - cosA * 0.715h - sinA * 0.715h,
              0.715h + cosA * 0.285h + sinA * 0.140h,
              0.715h - cosA * 0.715h + sinA * 0.715h),
        half3(0.072h - cosA * 0.072h + sinA * 0.928h,
              0.072h - cosA * 0.072h - sinA * 0.283h,
              0.072h + cosA * 0.928h + sinA * 0.072h)
    );

    return half4(hueMatrix * color.rgb, color.a);
}

    Sepia

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 sepia(float2 pos, half4 color, float intensity) {
    half3 sepiaColor = half3(
        dot(color.rgb, half3(0.393, 0.769, 0.189)),
        dot(color.rgb, half3(0.349, 0.686, 0.168)),
        dot(color.rgb, half3(0.272, 0.534, 0.131))
    );
    return half4(mix(color.rgb, sepiaColor, half(intensity)), color.a);
}

    Invert

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 invert(float2 pos, half4 color) {
    return half4(1.0h - color.rgb, color.a);
}

    Posterize (Reduce Color Levels)

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 posterize(float2 pos, half4 color, float levels) {
    half3 posterized = floor(color.rgb * half(levels)) / half(levels - 1.0);
    return half4(posterized, color.a);
}

    Threshold (Black & White)

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 threshold(float2 pos, half4 color, float cutoff) {
    half luma = dot(color.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    half bw = step(half(cutoff), luma);
    return half4(bw, bw, bw, color.a);
}

    Color Temperature (Warm/Cool)

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 temperature(float2 pos, half4 color, float temp) {
    // temp: -1 = cool (blue), 0 = neutral, 1 = warm (orange)
    half3 adjusted = color.rgb;
    adjusted.r += half(temp * 0.1);
    adjusted.b -= half(temp * 0.1);
    return half4(saturate(adjusted), color.a);
}

    Vignette

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 vignette(float2 pos, half4 color, float2 size, float intensity) {
    float2 uv = pos / size;
    float2 center = uv - 0.5;
    float dist = length(center) * 1.4;
    float vig = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.4, 1.0, dist) * intensity;
    return half4(color.rgb * half(vig), color.a);
}

    Combining Effects

    Image("photo")
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.contrast(.float(1.2)))
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.saturation(.float(1.3)))
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.temperature(.float(0.15)))
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.vignette(.float2(size), .float(0.6)))

    Exercises

    1. Color mixer: Combine brightness, contrast, and saturation with 3 sliders.
    2. Duotone: Map luminance to a gradient between two custom colors.
    3. Channel swap: Let the user rearrange RGB channels (show all 6 permutations).

    The foundation

    • color effect = a shader that maps one pixel's color to a new color, never reading neighbors.
    • luminance = a single brightness number from dot(rgb, weights); the basis for grayscale, threshold, and saturation.
    • mix(a, b, amount) = the universal blend/strength knob, sliding between original and adjusted.
    • brightness / contrast = multiply the color; contrast does it around 0.5 so midtones stay put while extremes spread.
    • saturation = mix between the gray (luma) version and the full-color version.
    • hue rotation = spin colors around the wheel with a 3x3 color matrix.
    • preserve alpha = always pass color.a through unchanged, so you only touch RGB.
    • chaining = stacking multiple colorEffects in SwiftUI, each one feeding the next.

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Grayscale via luminance dot product
    • [x] Brightness (multiply), contrast (around midpoint), saturation (mix with luma)
    • [x] Hue rotation with 3x3 color matrix
    • [x] All effects preserve alpha channel
    • [x] Effects can be chained in SwiftUI
    Prev← 11 — Structs and AttributesNext13 — Blur Effects →

    13 — Blur Effects

    Start here — the plain idea

    A blur is just averaging each pixel with its neighbors; a bigger radius averages more neighbors, so detail smears away. That's the entire idea. Everything in this lesson is about doing that averaging fast enough to run live on every glass surface in iOS.

    Here's why speed is the whole story. If every output pixel honestly averaged a 20-pixel-radius square, that's ~1,700 reads per pixel, billions per frame — your phone would melt. So the lesson is a ladder of clever shortcuts that get the same soft look for a fraction of the work: split the 2D blur into a horizontal pass then a vertical pass (a Gaussian conveniently allows this), sample between pixels so one read counts as two, or do several tiny blurs at growing distances instead of one giant one.

    Think of it like blending a smoothie. The naive way is hand-mashing every berry against every other berry. The smart ways are coarser strokes that reach the same smooth result far cheaper. Apple even ships a one-liner (MPS) that does the whole thing for you.

    Blur is the most important visual effect in iOS — used in navigation bars, sheets, Control Center, notifications, and every glass material. This lesson builds from the simplest blur to Apple-quality implementations.

    Why Blur is Hard

    A naive blur reads many neighboring pixels for every output pixel. For a 20px radius, that's (2*20+1)^2 = 1681 texture reads per pixel. At 2.7 million pixels, that's 4.5 billion reads per frame. We need smart algorithms to make this fast.

    Level 1: Box Blur (Simplest)

    Average all pixels in a square region. Simple but produces boxy artifacts.

    fragment half4 boxBlur(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float &radius <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant float2 &texSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    int r = int(radius);
    float count = 0;

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float2 offset = float2(x, y) / texSize;
            sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + offset);
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(count);
}

    Cost: O(radius^2) per pixel. Unusable for large radii.

    Level 2: Separable Blur (2-Pass)

    Key insight: a 2D Gaussian can be decomposed into two 1D passes (horizontal then vertical). This reduces O(r^2) to O(2r).

    // Pass 1: Horizontal blur
fragment half4 blurH(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float &radius <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant float2 &texSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(radius);
    float sigma = radius * 0.5;

    for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
        float weight = exp(-float(x * x) / (2.0 * sigma * sigma));
        float2 offset = float2(float(x) / texSize.x, 0.0);
        sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + offset) * half(weight);
        totalWeight += weight;
    }

    return sum / half(totalWeight);
}

// Pass 2: Vertical blur (same shader, different direction)
fragment half4 blurV(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float &radius <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant float2 &texSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(radius);
    float sigma = radius * 0.5;

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        float weight = exp(-float(y * y) / (2.0 * sigma * sigma));
        float2 offset = float2(0.0, float(y) / texSize.y);
        sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + offset) * half(weight);
        totalWeight += weight;
    }

    return sum / half(totalWeight);
}

    Swift side — two render passes:

    // Pass 1: source -> intermediate (horizontal blur)
encoder1.setFragmentTexture(sourceTexture, index: 0)
// Render to intermediateTexture

// Pass 2: intermediate -> output (vertical blur)
encoder2.setFragmentTexture(intermediateTexture, index: 0)
// Render to outputTexture

    Level 3: Optimized Gaussian (Linear Sampling Trick)

    By sampling between two pixels (filter::linear), you get the interpolated value of both — effectively 2 samples for the price of 1. This halves the number of texture reads.

    // Precomputed for 9-tap kernel (radius ~4)
// These offsets sample BETWEEN pixels to get two taps in one read
constant float offsets[3] = { 0.0, 1.3846153846, 3.2307692308 };
constant float weights[3] = { 0.2270270270, 0.3162162162, 0.0702702703 };

fragment half4 optimizedBlurH(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float2 &texSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);
    float texelSize = 1.0 / texSize.x;

    half4 color = tex.sample(s, in.uv) * half(weights[0]);

    for (int i = 1; i < 3; i++) {
        float offset = offsets[i] * texelSize;
        color += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(offset, 0)) * half(weights[i]);
        color += tex.sample(s, in.uv - float2(offset, 0)) * half(weights[i]);
    }

    return color;
}

    5 texture reads instead of 9 for the same quality. At larger radii, the savings compound.

    Level 4: Kawase Blur (Best Quality/Performance Ratio)

    Used by many game engines and real-time renderers. Instead of one large-radius blur, apply multiple small blurs at increasing sample distances. Each pass is just 5 taps.

    fragment half4 kawaseBlur(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float &iteration <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,  // 0, 1, 2, 3...
    constant float2 &texSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);

    float2 texelSize = 1.0 / texSize;
    float offset = float(iteration) + 0.5;

    half4 color = tex.sample(s, in.uv) * 4.0h;
    color += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(-offset, -offset) * texelSize);
    color += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2( offset, -offset) * texelSize);
    color += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(-offset,  offset) * texelSize);
    color += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2( offset,  offset) * texelSize);

    return color / 8.0h;
}

    Swift: Run 4-6 passes, ping-ponging between two textures:

    for i in 0..<6 {
    var iteration = Float(i)
    encoder.setFragmentBytes(&iteration, length: 4, index: 0)
    encoder.setFragmentTexture(i % 2 == 0 ? texA : texB, index: 0)
    // Render to the OTHER texture
}

    5 texture reads per pass x 6 passes = 30 reads for a large blur. Compare to 1681 reads for naive box blur.

    Level 5: Dual Kawase Blur (Apple-Quality)

    The technique used by many production renderers. Downscale with blur, then upscale with blur. Very fast, very smooth.

    // Downsample pass (halves resolution + blurs)
fragment half4 dualKawaseDown(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float2 &texelSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);
    float2 halfTexel = texelSize * 0.5;

    half4 sum = tex.sample(s, in.uv) * 4.0h;
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv - halfTexel);
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + halfTexel);
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(halfTexel.x, -halfTexel.y));
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv - float2(halfTexel.x, -halfTexel.y));

    return sum / 8.0h;
}

// Upsample pass (doubles resolution + blurs)
fragment half4 dualKawaseUp(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    constant float2 &texelSize <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear, address::clamp_to_edge);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(-texelSize.x * 2.0, 0)) ;
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(-texelSize.x, texelSize.y)) * 2.0h;
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(0, texelSize.y * 2.0));
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(texelSize.x, texelSize.y)) * 2.0h;
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(texelSize.x * 2.0, 0));
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(texelSize.x, -texelSize.y)) * 2.0h;
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(0, -texelSize.y * 2.0));
    sum += tex.sample(s, in.uv + float2(-texelSize.x, -texelSize.y)) * 2.0h;

    return sum / 12.0h;
}

    Pipeline: Source -> Down1 (1/2) -> Down2 (1/4) -> Down3 (1/8) -> Up3 (1/4) -> Up2 (1/2) -> Up1 (full)

    Level 6: MPS Gaussian Blur (Built-In, Zero Shader Code)

    Apple provides production-quality blur in Metal Performance Shaders:

    import MetalPerformanceShaders

let blur = MPSImageGaussianBlur(device: device, sigma: 10.0)

let commandBuffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!
blur.encode(commandBuffer: commandBuffer,
            sourceTexture: inputTexture,
            destinationTexture: outputTexture)
commandBuffer.commit()

    This uses Apple's optimized implementation — the same quality as UIVisualEffectView. Use it when you don't need custom behavior.

    Comparison

    Method Reads/Pixel Radius 20 Quality Complexity
    Box blur r^2 1681 Low (boxy) Simple
    Separable Gaussian 2r 40 High Medium
    Optimized Gaussian r 20 High Medium
    Kawase (6 pass) 30 30 Good Medium
    Dual Kawase ~40 40 Very good Complex
    MPS optimized optimized Best One line

    Exercises

    1. Box vs Gaussian: Implement both and compare the visual quality side by side (split screen).

    2. Animated blur: Blur an image with a radius that animates from 0 to 30 over time.

    3. Selective blur: Blur only the bottom half of an image (simulate depth of field).

    4. Kawase implementation: Implement the full Kawase multi-pass blur in Swift + Metal.

    The foundation

    • blur = averaging each pixel with its neighbors; bigger radius = more neighbors = more smearing.
    • box blur = the naive square average; simple but boxy and O(radius²), too slow for big radii.
    • Gaussian = a weighted average where nearby pixels count more, giving a soft, natural falloff.
    • separable blur = splitting a 2D blur into a horizontal pass then a vertical pass, turning O(r²) into O(2r).
    • linear-sampling trick = sampling between two pixels so one read returns both, halving texture reads.
    • Kawase / dual Kawase = stacking several tiny blurs (and down/up-scaling) to fake a huge blur for cheap.
    • multi-pass / ping-pong = rendering into one texture, then back, repeatedly, to chain blur passes.
    • MPS = Apple's built-in MPSImageGaussianBlur, production-quality blur in one line of Swift.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] Why naive blur is too slow (O(r^2) texture reads) - [x] Separable blur: 2D blur = horizontal 1D + vertical 1D (O(2r)) - [x] Linear sampling trick: sample between pixels to halve reads - [x] Kawase blur: multiple small passes = large blur - [x] Dual Kawase: downscale + upscale for maximum efficiency - [x] MPS blur: Apple's built-in, use when custom behavior isn't needed - [x] Multi-pass rendering: ping-pong between textures

    Prev← 12 — Color EffectsNext14 — Distortion Effects →

    14 — Distortion Effects

    Start here — the plain idea

    Color effects change what color a pixel is. Distortion effects change where a pixel reads its color from. The pixel keeps its color — it just grabs it from a shifted spot. That one move gives you ripples, swirls, lens bulges, and shockwaves.

    Picture the screen as a printed image under a sheet of clear, bendy plastic. You don't repaint anything; you just warp the plastic, and the picture appears to stretch and wobble. In a shader the "warp" is a little math function that says, for each pixel, "read from here instead." A sin of distance makes a ripple; a rotation that fades with distance makes a swirl; pushing pixels outward from a center makes a magnifying bulge.

    Two habits show up everywhere: use distance and normalize to work radially (out from a center point), and use smoothstep to fade the effect gently at its edges so it doesn't end in a hard seam. And always handle "this pixel is outside the effect" by leaving its position alone.

    Distortion shaders move pixels around without changing their color. They remap where each pixel reads its source data from.

    The Concept

    Normal:     Read pixel at position (x, y)
Distorted:  Read pixel at position (x + offset_x, y + offset_y)

    The fragment shader calculates the offset and samples the original texture at the shifted position.

    Ripple

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 ripple(float2 position, float2 origin, float time) {
    float dist = distance(position, origin);
    float wave = sin(dist * 0.1 - time * 8.0) * 8.0;
    float falloff = exp(-dist * 0.01) * exp(-time * 1.5);
    float2 dir = normalize(position - origin + 0.001);
    return position + dir * wave * falloff;
}

    Wave

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 wave(float2 position, float time, float amplitude, float frequency) {
    float offsetY = sin(position.x * frequency * 0.01 + time * 3.0) * amplitude;
    float offsetX = cos(position.y * frequency * 0.01 + time * 2.0) * amplitude * 0.5;
    return position + float2(offsetX, offsetY);
}

    Swirl

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 swirl(float2 position, float2 center, float radius, float angle) {
    float2 diff = position - center;
    float dist = length(diff);

    if (dist > radius) return position;

    float percent = (radius - dist) / radius;
    float theta = percent * percent * angle;

    float s = sin(theta);
    float c = cos(theta);
    float2 rotated = float2(
        diff.x * c - diff.y * s,
        diff.x * s + diff.y * c
    );

    return center + rotated;
}

    Barrel Distortion (Lens Effect)

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 barrel(float2 position, float2 center, float2 size, float strength) {
    float2 uv = (position - center) / (size * 0.5);
    float r2 = dot(uv, uv);
    float distortion = 1.0 + r2 * strength;
    return center + uv * distortion * (size * 0.5);
}

    Pixelation

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 pixelate(float2 position, float blockSize) {
    return floor(position / blockSize) * blockSize + blockSize * 0.5;
}

    Magnifying Glass

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 magnify(float2 position, float2 center, float radius, float zoom) {
    float dist = distance(position, center);
    if (dist > radius) return position;

    float2 diff = position - center;
    float smoothEdge = smoothstep(radius * 0.8, radius, dist);
    float2 zoomed = center + diff / mix(zoom, 1.0, smoothEdge);
    return zoomed;
}

    Shockwave

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 shockwave(float2 position, float2 center, float time,
                                  float waveWidth, float amplitude) {
    float dist = distance(position, center);
    float radius = time * 300.0;  // Expanding ring

    float diff = abs(dist - radius);
    if (diff > waveWidth) return position;

    float intensity = (1.0 - diff / waveWidth) * amplitude;
    intensity *= 1.0 - smoothstep(0.0, 1.0, time);  // Fade over time

    float2 dir = normalize(position - center);
    return position + dir * sin(diff * 0.5) * intensity;
}

    Exercises

    1. Interactive swirl: Swirl centered on touch point, angle controlled by drag distance.
    2. Underwater: Combine two waves at different frequencies for an underwater wobble.
    3. Glitch: Random horizontal line offsets that change every few frames.

    The foundation

    • distortion = moving where a pixel reads its color from, not changing the color itself.
    • offset = the shift you add to a pixel's read position; the shape of that shift is the effect.
    • radial direction = normalize(position - center) — the "straight out from the center" arrow most effects push along.
    • falloff = fading the effect with distance or time (via exp or smoothstep) so it eases off instead of cutting hard.
    • swirl = rotating pixels around a center by an angle that shrinks with distance.
    • barrel / magnify = scaling pixels' distance from a center to bulge or zoom, like a lens.
    • guard the radius = return the original position for pixels outside the effect, so nothing leaks.

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Distortion = remapping read position, not changing color
    • [x] normalize(position - origin) gives radial direction
    • [x] smoothstep for soft falloff at effect edges
    • [x] Always handle the "outside effect radius" case (return original position)
    Prev← 13 — Blur EffectsNext15 — Noise and Procedural Patterns →

    15 — Noise and Procedural Patterns

    Start here — the plain idea

    Nature is rarely perfectly smooth or perfectly random — clouds, fire, marble, and water all have a structured messiness. Noise functions are how you manufacture that on the GPU, with no textures, just math. They're the secret ingredient behind almost everything organic-looking.

    Start with pure randomness: a hash function that turns any position into a repeatable random number (same input, same output, but neighbors look totally unrelated). That raw static is too jagged to use, so you smooth it — pick random values on a grid and blend between them (value noise), or blend random directions for an even silkier result (Perlin/gradient noise). The killer move is FBM: layer several copies of noise, each one finer and fainter than the last, like adding big rolling hills, then bumps, then pebbles. That stack of scales is exactly what makes clouds look like clouds.

    Think of it as a recipe for controlled chaos. One more flavor, Voronoi, measures distance to scattered points to make cell-like patterns — stones, scales, cracked earth.

    Noise functions generate organic, natural-looking randomness. They're the foundation of clouds, fire, water, terrain, and countless visual effects.

    Random (Hash) Function

    Pure randomness — different value for every position, no smoothness:

    float random(float2 st) {
    return fract(sin(dot(st, float2(12.9898, 78.233))) * 43758.5453123);
}

// Returns 0.0 to 1.0
// Same input always gives same output (deterministic)
// Adjacent pixels get completely different values (not smooth)

    Value Noise

    Smooth random values — interpolate between random grid points:

    float valueNoise(float2 st) {
    float2 i = floor(st);
    float2 f = fract(st);

    // Smooth interpolation curve
    float2 u = f * f * (3.0 - 2.0 * f);  // smoothstep

    // Random values at 4 grid corners
    float a = random(i);
    float b = random(i + float2(1.0, 0.0));
    float c = random(i + float2(0.0, 1.0));
    float d = random(i + float2(1.0, 1.0));

    // Bilinear interpolation
    return mix(mix(a, b, u.x), mix(c, d, u.x), u.y);
}

    Perlin-Style Gradient Noise

    Smoother than value noise, more organic:

    float2 gradientDir(float2 p) {
    p = fmod(p, 289.0);
    float x = fract(sin(dot(p, float2(127.1, 311.7))) * 43758.5453);
    float y = fract(sin(dot(p, float2(269.5, 183.3))) * 43758.5453);
    return normalize(float2(x, y) * 2.0 - 1.0);
}

float gradientNoise(float2 st) {
    float2 i = floor(st);
    float2 f = fract(st);
    float2 u = f * f * f * (f * (f * 6.0 - 15.0) + 10.0);  // Improved smoothstep

    float2 ga = gradientDir(i);
    float2 gb = gradientDir(i + float2(1.0, 0.0));
    float2 gc = gradientDir(i + float2(0.0, 1.0));
    float2 gd = gradientDir(i + float2(1.0, 1.0));

    float va = dot(ga, f);
    float vb = dot(gb, f - float2(1.0, 0.0));
    float vc = dot(gc, f - float2(0.0, 1.0));
    float vd = dot(gd, f - float2(1.0, 1.0));

    return mix(mix(va, vb, u.x), mix(vc, vd, u.x), u.y) * 0.5 + 0.5;
}

    Fractal Brownian Motion (FBM)

    Layer multiple octaves of noise for detail at multiple scales:

    float fbm(float2 st, int octaves) {
    float value = 0.0;
    float amplitude = 0.5;
    float frequency = 1.0;

    for (int i = 0; i < octaves; i++) {
        value += amplitude * gradientNoise(st * frequency);
        frequency *= 2.0;     // Each octave is 2x frequency
        amplitude *= 0.5;     // Each octave is half amplitude
    }

    return value;
}

// Usage: fbm(uv * 4.0, 6) -> cloud-like pattern

    Voronoi (Cellular Noise)

    Creates cell-like patterns (stone tiles, scales, bubbles):

    float voronoi(float2 st) {
    float2 i = floor(st);
    float2 f = fract(st);

    float minDist = 1.0;

    // Check 3x3 neighborhood
    for (int y = -1; y <= 1; y++) {
        for (int x = -1; x <= 1; x++) {
            float2 neighbor = float2(x, y);
            float2 point = random2(i + neighbor);  // Random point in each cell
            float dist = distance(f, neighbor + point);
            minDist = min(minDist, dist);
        }
    }

    return minDist;
}

float2 random2(float2 st) {
    return float2(
        fract(sin(dot(st, float2(127.1, 311.7))) * 43758.5453),
        fract(sin(dot(st, float2(269.5, 183.3))) * 43758.5453)
    );
}

    Practical Uses

    Cloud/Fog

    fragment half4 clouds(VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>, constant float &time <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>) {
    float2 uv = in.uv * 4.0;
    uv += float2(time * 0.1, time * 0.05);  // Drift

    float n = fbm(uv, 6);
    half3 skyColor = half3(0.4, 0.6, 0.9);
    half3 cloudColor = half3(1.0);

    return half4(mix(skyColor, cloudColor, half(n)), 1.0h);
}

    Film Grain

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 filmGrain(float2 pos, half4 color, float time, float intensity) {
    float grain = random(pos * 0.01 + time) * 2.0 - 1.0;
    return half4(color.rgb + half(grain * intensity), color.a);
}

    Dissolve Transition

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 dissolve(float2 pos, half4 color, float progress) {
    float noise = random(pos * 0.05);
    float threshold = progress * 1.2;  // Slightly over 1.0 for full dissolve

    if (noise < threshold) {
        // Glowing edge
        if (noise > threshold - 0.05) {
            return half4(1.0, 0.5, 0.0, color.a);  // Orange edge
        }
        return half4(0, 0, 0, 0);  // Dissolved
    }
    return color;
}

    Exercises

    1. Animated noise: Scroll noise over time to create flowing clouds.
    2. Noise terrain: Use noise as a heightmap — darker = lower, brighter = higher. Color based on height (green for low, white for peaks).
    3. Voronoi cells: Color each Voronoi cell differently using the cell's grid position as a random seed.

    The foundation

    • hash / random = a function turning a position into a repeatable pseudo-random number; same input, same output, neighbors unrelated.
    • value noise = smoothing that randomness by blending random values placed on a grid.
    • gradient (Perlin) noise = a smoother variant that blends random directions, giving more organic flow.
    • octave = one layer of noise at a given scale; doubling frequency, halving amplitude each step.
    • FBM (fractal Brownian motion) = stacking octaves so you get coarse shapes plus fine detail — the cloud-maker.
    • Voronoi (cellular noise) = distance to the nearest scattered point, producing cell/stone/scale patterns.
    • practical uses = clouds, film grain, dissolve transitions, fire, water — all just noise shaped and colored.

    Checkpoint

    • [x] random() = deterministic hash, not smooth
    • [x] Value noise = smooth interpolation between random grid values
    • [x] Gradient noise = dot product with random gradients (smoother)
    • [x] FBM = layered octaves of noise for multi-scale detail
    • [x] Voronoi = nearest-point distance creates cellular patterns
    • [x] Practical: clouds, grain, dissolve, fire, water
    Prev← 14 — Distortion EffectsNext16 — Blending and Compositing →

    16 — Blending and Compositing

    Start here — the plain idea

    Compositing is the math for stacking layers — deciding what happens where a foreground meets a background. Every time something semi-transparent sits over something else, a tiny formula runs to mix them. This lesson is that formula, in all its flavors.

    The base case is alpha compositing: a pixel that's 70% opaque shows 70% of its own color and lets 30% of the background through. That's "source over," the normal stacking you take for granted. The Porter-Duff family is just every variation on the theme — keep only where they overlap, punch one out of the other, and so on. A key detail is premultiplied alpha: the color is already scaled by its transparency, which makes the blend math clean and is what Metal expects (mismatch it and you get dark halos).

    Then there are the Photoshop-style blend modes — multiply darkens, screen lightens, overlay boosts contrast. These are just per-channel arithmetic on the two colors. Apple's glass highlights lean on plusLighter (additive, clamped), which is why overlapping light looks like it glows.

    How to combine layers — the math behind Photoshop blend modes, alpha compositing, and Apple's private compositingFilter.

    Alpha Compositing (Porter-Duff)

    The foundation. How a source (foreground) combines with a destination (background):

    // Source Over (Normal blending — most common)
float4 sourceOver(float4 src, float4 dst) {
    return src + dst * (1.0 - src.a);
}

// Premultiplied version (what Metal actually uses):
// result = src + dst * (1 - src.a)
// This works directly because src.rgb is already multiplied by src.a

    All 12 Porter-Duff Modes

    float4 pdClear(float4 s, float4 d)      { return float4(0); }
float4 pdCopy(float4 s, float4 d)       { return s; }
float4 pdSourceOver(float4 s, float4 d)  { return s + d * (1-s.a); }
float4 pdSourceIn(float4 s, float4 d)    { return s * d.a; }
float4 pdSourceOut(float4 s, float4 d)   { return s * (1-d.a); }
float4 pdSourceAtop(float4 s, float4 d)  { return s * d.a + d * (1-s.a); }
float4 pdDestOver(float4 s, float4 d)    { return d + s * (1-d.a); }
float4 pdDestIn(float4 s, float4 d)      { return d * s.a; }
float4 pdDestOut(float4 s, float4 d)     { return d * (1-s.a); }
float4 pdDestAtop(float4 s, float4 d)    { return d * s.a + s * (1-s.a); }
float4 pdXor(float4 s, float4 d)         { return s*(1-d.a) + d*(1-s.a); }
float4 pdPlusLighter(float4 s, float4 d) { return min(s + d, 1.0); }

    Apple's plusL compositing filter = pdPlusLighter. This is their go-to for glass highlights.

    Photoshop-Style Blend Modes

    These operate on the RGB channels (alpha handled separately):

    // Multiply (darkens)
float3 multiply(float3 base, float3 blend) { return base * blend; }

// Screen (lightens)
float3 screen(float3 base, float3 blend) { return 1.0 - (1.0-base) * (1.0-blend); }

// Overlay (contrast: darkens darks, lightens lights)
float3 overlay(float3 base, float3 blend) {
    return mix(
        2.0 * base * blend,
        1.0 - 2.0 * (1.0-base) * (1.0-blend),
        step(0.5, base)
    );
}

// Soft Light (subtle overlay)
float3 softLight(float3 base, float3 blend) {
    return mix(
        2.0 * base * blend + base * base * (1.0 - 2.0*blend),
        sqrt(base) * (2.0*blend - 1.0) + 2.0*base * (1.0-blend),
        step(0.5, blend)
    );
}

// Hard Light
float3 hardLight(float3 base, float3 blend) { return overlay(blend, base); }

// Color Dodge (brightens base by blend)
float3 colorDodge(float3 base, float3 blend) {
    return base / max(1.0 - blend, 0.001);
}

// Color Burn (darkens base by blend)
float3 colorBurn(float3 base, float3 blend) {
    return 1.0 - (1.0-base) / max(blend, 0.001);
}

// Difference
float3 difference(float3 base, float3 blend) { return abs(base - blend); }

// Exclusion
float3 exclusion(float3 base, float3 blend) {
    return base + blend - 2.0*base*blend;
}

// Add (Linear Dodge)
float3 add(float3 base, float3 blend) { return min(base + blend, 1.0); }

    Using Blend Modes with Alpha

    float4 blendWithAlpha(float4 base, float4 blend, float3 (*blendFunc)(float3, float3)) {
    float3 blended = blendFunc(base.rgb, blend.rgb);
    float3 result = mix(base.rgb, blended, blend.a);
    return float4(result, base.a);
}

    Apple's Compositing Filters (Private API)

    On CALayer, applied via layer.setValue("plusL", forKey: "compositingFilter"):

    Filter Effect Equivalent
    plusL Add (clamp to 1) min(src + dst, 1.0) — Apple's glass highlights
    plusD Add (allow overflow) src + dst
    multiply Multiply src * dst — shadows, tinting
    sourceOver Normal blend Standard alpha compositing
    sourceIn Mask to dest shape src * dst.a
    destIn Mask to source shape dst * src.a

    SwiftUI Blend Modes

    Image("foreground")
    .blendMode(.multiply)     // Uses GPU blend mode
    .blendMode(.screen)
    .blendMode(.overlay)
    .blendMode(.softLight)
    .blendMode(.difference)
    .blendMode(.plusLighter)   // This IS Apple's "plusL"

    Exercises

    1. Blend mode gallery: Show the same two images composited with each blend mode, labeled.
    2. Light leak: Use screen blending to overlay a gradient "light leak" on a photo.
    3. Shadow casting: Use multiply to add a soft shadow under UI elements.

    The foundation

    • compositing = the math for combining a foreground layer with a background.
