    • alpha / source-over = showing a pixel's color in proportion to its opacity, letting the rest of the background through.
    • premultiplied alpha = color already scaled by its transparency; keeps blend math clean and is what Metal expects.
    • Porter-Duff modes = the 12 ways two layers can overlap (over, in, out, atop, xor, clear...).
    • blend modes = per-channel arithmetic: multiply darkens, screen lightens, overlay boosts contrast, soft light is a gentle version.
    • plusLighter (plusL) = additive blend clamped to 1; Apple's go-to for glowing glass highlights.
    • SwiftUI .blendMode() = the high-level handle that maps straight to these GPU blend operations.

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Porter-Duff compositing: 12 modes for combining layers with alpha
    • [x] Premultiplied alpha: result = src + dst * (1 - src.a)
    • [x] Photoshop blend modes: multiply, screen, overlay, soft light, etc.
    • [x] Apple's plusL = min(src + dst, 1.0) — their glass highlight technique
    • [x] SwiftUI .blendMode() maps to GPU blend operations
    Prev← 15 — Noise and Procedural PatternsNext17 — Metal in UIKit →

    17 — Metal in UIKit

    Start here — the plain idea

    So far your shaders have lived in tidy little previews. Now you want them on a real screen, inside a real app. That's what the "rendering pipeline" is: the plumbing that takes your shader, runs it on the GPU, and shows the result frame after frame.

    Think of it like a kitchen. The GPU is the chef, the device is your line to that kitchen, the commandQueue is the order rail, and a drawable is the empty plate the chef fills and sends out. Every frame you write an order ("run this shader, draw a triangle, present the plate"), drop it on the rail, and a fresh plate appears on screen. MTKView is the easy mode — it sets the table and rings the bell 60 or 120 times a second for you. CAMetalLayer is the same idea with the lid off, for when you need to control the timing yourself.

    The other big idea here is that the GPU's output doesn't have to go straight to the screen — it can go into a texture instead. That's what makes multi-pass effects possible: render once into an off-screen picture, then feed that picture back in as input for a second pass. Blur works exactly this way (blur horizontally, then blur that result vertically).

    Setting up the full Metal rendering pipeline with MTKView, CAMetalLayer, and UIKit integration.

    Option 1: MTKView (Recommended)

    MTKView handles the display link, drawable management, and most boilerplate:

    import UIKit
import MetalKit

class MetalViewController: UIViewController {
    var metalView: MTKView!
    var renderer: Renderer!

    override func viewDidLoad() {
        super.viewDidLoad()

        guard let device = MTLCreateSystemDefaultDevice() else { fatalError("No GPU") }

        metalView = MTKView(frame: view.bounds, device: device)
        metalView.autoresizingMask = [.flexibleWidth, .flexibleHeight]
        metalView.colorPixelFormat = .bgra8Unorm_srgb
        metalView.clearColor = MTLClearColor(red: 0, green: 0, blue: 0, alpha: 1)
        metalView.preferredFramesPerSecond = 120  // ProMotion
        metalView.framebufferOnly = true          // Performance: no read-back
        view.addSubview(metalView)

        renderer = Renderer(metalView: metalView)
    }
}

class Renderer: NSObject, MTKViewDelegate {
    let device: MTLDevice
    let commandQueue: MTLCommandQueue
    let pipelineState: MTLRenderPipelineState
    var time: Float = 0

    init(metalView: MTKView) {
        device = metalView.device!
        commandQueue = device.makeCommandQueue()!

        let library = device.makeDefaultLibrary()!
        let descriptor = MTLRenderPipelineDescriptor()
        descriptor.vertexFunction = library.makeFunction(name: "fullscreenVertex")
        descriptor.fragmentFunction = library.makeFunction(name: "myEffect")
        descriptor.colorAttachments[0].pixelFormat = metalView.colorPixelFormat

        pipelineState = try! device.makeRenderPipelineState(descriptor: descriptor)

        super.init()
        metalView.delegate = self
    }

    func draw(in view: MTKView) {
        time += 1.0 / Float(view.preferredFramesPerSecond)

        guard let drawable = view.currentDrawable,
              let descriptor = view.currentRenderPassDescriptor else { return }

        let buffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!
        let encoder = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: descriptor)!

        encoder.setRenderPipelineState(pipelineState)
        encoder.setFragmentBytes(&time, length: 4, index: 0)
        encoder.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)

        encoder.endEncoding()
        buffer.present(drawable)
        buffer.commit()
    }

    func mtkView(_ view: MTKView, drawableSizeWillChange size: CGSize) {}
}

    Option 2: CAMetalLayer (More Control)

    For when you need Metal rendering in a regular UIView without MTKView:

    class MetalLayerView: UIView {
    override class var layerClass: AnyClass { CAMetalLayer.self }

    var metalLayer: CAMetalLayer { layer as! CAMetalLayer }
    var device: MTLDevice!
    var commandQueue: MTLCommandQueue!
    var displayLink: CADisplayLink!

    func setup() {
        device = MTLCreateSystemDefaultDevice()!
        metalLayer.device = device
        metalLayer.pixelFormat = .bgra8Unorm_srgb
        metalLayer.framebufferOnly = true
        metalLayer.contentsScale = UIScreen.main.scale

        commandQueue = device.makeCommandQueue()!

        displayLink = CADisplayLink(target: self, selector: #selector(render))
        displayLink.preferredFrameRateRange = CAFrameRateRange(minimum: 60, maximum: 120)
        displayLink.add(to: .main, forMode: .common)
    }

    @objc func render() {
        guard let drawable = metalLayer.nextDrawable() else { return }
        // Render to drawable.texture, then present
    }
}

    Multi-Pass Rendering (Blur Pipeline)

    func renderBlur(source: MTLTexture, output: MTLTexture) {
    // Create intermediate texture
    let desc = MTLTextureDescriptor.texture2DDescriptor(
        pixelFormat: source.pixelFormat,
        width: source.width, height: source.height, mipmapped: false)
    desc.usage = [.shaderRead, .renderTarget]
    let intermediate = device.makeTexture(descriptor: desc)!

    let buffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!

    // Pass 1: Horizontal blur (source -> intermediate)
    let pass1Desc = MTLRenderPassDescriptor()
    pass1Desc.colorAttachments[0].texture = intermediate
    pass1Desc.colorAttachments[0].loadAction = .dontCare
    pass1Desc.colorAttachments[0].storeAction = .store

    let enc1 = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: pass1Desc)!
    enc1.setRenderPipelineState(blurHPipeline)
    enc1.setFragmentTexture(source, index: 0)
    var dir = SIMD2<Float>(1, 0)
    enc1.setFragmentBytes(&dir, length: 8, index: 0)
    enc1.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)
    enc1.endEncoding()

    // Pass 2: Vertical blur (intermediate -> output)
    let pass2Desc = MTLRenderPassDescriptor()
    pass2Desc.colorAttachments[0].texture = output
    pass2Desc.colorAttachments[0].loadAction = .dontCare
    pass2Desc.colorAttachments[0].storeAction = .store

    let enc2 = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: pass2Desc)!
    enc2.setRenderPipelineState(blurVPipeline)
    enc2.setFragmentTexture(intermediate, index: 0)
    dir = SIMD2<Float>(0, 1)
    enc2.setFragmentBytes(&dir, length: 8, index: 0)
    enc2.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)
    enc2.endEncoding()

    buffer.commit()
}

    Rendering to Texture (Off-Screen)

    func renderToTexture() -> MTLTexture {
    let desc = MTLTextureDescriptor.texture2DDescriptor(
        pixelFormat: .rgba8Unorm, width: 512, height: 512, mipmapped: false)
    desc.usage = [.shaderRead, .renderTarget]
    let texture = device.makeTexture(descriptor: desc)!

    let passDesc = MTLRenderPassDescriptor()
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].texture = texture
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].loadAction = .clear
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].clearColor = MTLClearColor(red: 0, green: 0, blue: 0, alpha: 0)
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].storeAction = .store

    let buffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!
    let encoder = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: passDesc)!
    // Draw your content...
    encoder.endEncoding()
    buffer.commit()
    buffer.waitUntilCompleted()

    return texture
}

    Capturing Screen Content for Processing

    // Snapshot a UIView to a Metal texture
func captureView(_ view: UIView) -> MTLTexture {
    let renderer = UIGraphicsImageRenderer(bounds: view.bounds)
    let image = renderer.image { ctx in
        view.drawHierarchy(in: view.bounds, afterScreenUpdates: false)
    }

    let loader = MTKTextureLoader(device: device)
    return try! loader.newTexture(cgImage: image.cgImage!, options: [
        .textureUsage: MTLTextureUsage.shaderRead.rawValue,
        .SRGB: false
    ])
}

    Checkpoint

    • [x] MTKView: easiest setup, handles display link and drawables
    • [x] CAMetalLayer: more control, embed Metal in any UIView
    • [x] Multi-pass rendering for effects like blur (ping-pong textures)
    • [x] Off-screen rendering to textures
    • [x] Capturing UIView content into Metal textures

    The foundation

    • MTKView = a ready-made canvas that handles the per-frame heartbeat and hands you a blank plate to draw on.
    • CAMetalLayer = the same canvas with manual controls, so you can drop Metal into any plain UIView and drive the timing yourself.
    • command queue / command buffer = the order rail and the order slip; you fill a slip with GPU instructions each frame and commit it.
    • drawable = the actual on-screen plate the GPU paints into, then presents so the user sees it.
    • render to texture = pointing the GPU's output at an off-screen image instead of the screen, so you can reuse the result later.
    • multi-pass (ping-pong) = running the effect in stages, feeding one pass's texture into the next — how a separable blur (horizontal then vertical) is built.
    • framebufferOnly = a promise that you won't read the pixels back, which lets Metal skip work and run faster.
    Prev← 16 — Blending and CompositingNext18 — Metal Shaders in SwiftUI (iOS 17+) →

    18 — Metal Shaders in SwiftUI (iOS 17+)

    Start here — the plain idea

    In UIKit you had to build the whole kitchen yourself. SwiftUI hands you a shortcut: take any view you already drew — text, an image, a whole scroll view — and run a tiny GPU program over its pixels with one modifier. The <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> keyword is just a label that says "this shader is allowed to be plugged into SwiftUI's own pipeline," the same way throws labels a function.

    There are three flavors, and the difference is simply what your shader is allowed to touch. A colorEffect gets one pixel's color and can change that color — recolor, tint, grayscale — but it can't peek at the neighbors. A distortionEffect can't change colors at all; instead it answers a different question: "for the pixel landing here, where should I go grab its color from?" Move that lookup around and you get ripples, pixelation, fisheye. A layerEffect is the powerful one — it hands you the whole rendered view as something you can sample anywhere, so you can read many neighboring pixels and average them. That's what blur, glow, and edge detection need.

    maxSampleOffset is just you telling SwiftUI "my shader might reach this far for a pixel," so it knows how big a region to keep around. Too small and the edges get clipped; too big and you pay for area you never use.

    iOS 17 introduced <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> shaders — the ability to apply custom Metal shaders directly to SwiftUI views. This is the most practical way to use Metal for visual effects in modern iOS apps.

    Three Effect Types

    // 1. colorEffect — per-pixel color transformation
view.colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.myEffect(.float(time)))

// 2. distortionEffect — per-pixel position displacement
view.distortionEffect(ShaderLibrary.myDistortion(.float(amount)),
                       maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 50, height: 50))

// 3. layerEffect — full texture access (sampling at arbitrary positions)
view.layerEffect(ShaderLibrary.myBlur(.float(radius)),
                  maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 64, height: 64))

    Color Effects

    Receives the current pixel color and position. Returns a new color. Cannot read neighboring pixels.

    Metal Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

// MUST have <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> attribute
// First param: position, second param: current color
// Additional params: your custom data

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 grayscale(float2 position, half4 color) {
    half luma = dot(color.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    return half4(luma, luma, luma, color.a);
}

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 tint(float2 position, half4 color, half4 tintColor) {
    return color * tintColor;
}

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 wave(float2 position, half4 color, float time) {
    float brightness = sin(position.x * 0.05 + time * 3.0) * 0.5 + 0.5;
    return half4(color.rgb * half(brightness), color.a);
}

    SwiftUI Usage

    Text("Hello, Metal!")
    .font(.largeTitle)
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.grayscale())

Image("photo")
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.tint(.color(.red)))

Text("Waves")
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.wave(.float(elapsedTime)))

    Distortion Effects

    Receives a position, returns a new position to sample from. Shifts where each pixel reads its color from. Cannot change colors, only positions.

    Metal Shader

    // Returns a float2 — the new sample position
<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 ripple(float2 position, float2 origin, float time,
                              float amplitude, float frequency, float decay) {
    float dist = distance(position, origin);
    float wave = sin(dist * frequency - time * 10.0);
    float falloff = exp(-dist * decay) * exp(-time * 2.0);
    float2 direction = normalize(position - origin + 0.001);

    return position + direction * wave * amplitude * falloff;
}

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 pixelate(float2 position, float size) {
    return floor(position / size) * size + size * 0.5;
}

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> float2 fisheye(float2 position, float2 center, float radius, float strength) {
    float2 diff = position - center;
    float dist = length(diff);

    if (dist < radius) {
        float percent = dist / radius;
        float distortion = pow(percent, strength);
        return center + diff * distortion / percent;
    }
    return position;
}

    SwiftUI Usage

    Image("photo")
    .distortionEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.ripple(
            .float2(tapLocation),
            .float(elapsedTime),
            .float(12),    // amplitude
            .float(15),    // frequency
            .float(0.02)   // decay
        ),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 30, height: 30)
    )

Text("Pixelated")
    .distortionEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.pixelate(.float(10)),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 10, height: 10)
    )

    maxSampleOffset: Tells SwiftUI the maximum distance (in points) a pixel might read from. Set it to at least your maximum distortion amount. Too small = clipping artifacts. Too large = wasted performance.

    Layer Effects

    The most powerful type. Receives the view as a SwiftUI::Layer that you can sample at any position. This enables blur, glow, edge detection, and any effect that reads neighboring pixels.

    Metal Shader

    // The Layer parameter gives you access to the full rendered view
<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 simpleBlur(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer, float radius) {
    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float count = 0;

    for (float y = -radius; y <= radius; y += 1.0) {
        for (float x = -radius; x <= radius; x += 1.0) {
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y));
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(count);
}

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 glow(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer, float radius, float intensity) {
    half4 original = layer.sample(position);
    half4 blurred = half4(0);
    float count = 0;

    for (float y = -radius; y <= radius; y += 2.0) {
        for (float x = -radius; x <= radius; x += 2.0) {
            blurred += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y));
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }
    blurred /= half(count);

    // Add glow (blurred version on top of original)
    return original + blurred * half(intensity);
}

<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 progressiveBlur(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
                                      float2 size, float maxRadius) {
    // Blur increases from top to bottom
    float progress = position.y / size.y;
    float radius = progress * maxRadius;

    if (radius < 1.0) return layer.sample(position);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(min(radius, 20.0));  // Cap for performance

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float weight = exp(-float(x*x + y*y) / (2.0 * radius * radius));
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y)) * half(weight);
            totalWeight += weight;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(totalWeight);
}

    SwiftUI Usage

    ScrollView {
    VStack { /* content */ }
}
.layerEffect(
    ShaderLibrary.progressiveBlur(
        .float2(CGSize(width: screenWidth, height: screenHeight)),
        .float(15)  // maxRadius
    ),
    maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 20, height: 20)
)

Text("Glowing")
    .font(.system(size: 60, weight: .bold))
    .layerEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.glow(.float(8), .float(0.5)),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 8, height: 8)
    )

    Passing Data Types

    // Float
.float(3.14)

// Float2 (from CGPoint or CGSize)
.float2(CGPoint(x: 100, y: 200))
.float2(CGSize(width: 400, height: 800))

// Color
.color(.red)
.color(Color(uiColor: myColor))

// Image (as texture)
.image(Image("myTexture"))

// Data (raw bytes)
.data(myData)

// FloatArray (for passing multiple values)
// Not directly available — use multiple .float() params

    Animating Shaders

    struct AnimatedShaderView: View {
    let startDate = Date()

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { context in
            let elapsed = context.date.timeIntervalSince(startDate)

            Image("photo")
                .colorEffect(
                    ShaderLibrary.wave(.float(Float(elapsed)))
                )
        }
    }
}

    With Gestures

    struct InteractiveShaderView: View {
    @State private var tapLocation: CGPoint = .zero
    @State private var tapTime: Date = .distantPast

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { context in
            let elapsed = context.date.timeIntervalSince(tapTime)

            Image("photo")
                .distortionEffect(
                    ShaderLibrary.ripple(
                        .float2(tapLocation),
                        .float(Float(elapsed)),
                        .float(12),
                        .float(15),
                        .float(0.02)
                    ),
                    maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 30, height: 30)
                )
                .onTapGesture { location in
                    tapLocation = location
                    tapTime = .now
                }
        }
    }
}

    .visualEffect (iOS 17+) — Position-Based Effects Without Metal

    For simpler effects that don't need custom shaders:

    ScrollView {
    ForEach(items) { item in
        ItemRow(item: item)
            .visualEffect { content, proxy in
                let frame = proxy.frame(in: .scrollView)
                let distance = abs(frame.midY - UIScreen.main.bounds.midY)
                let normalizedDistance = min(distance / 300, 1.0)

                content
                    .blur(radius: normalizedDistance * 10)
                    .opacity(1.0 - normalizedDistance * 0.5)
                    .scaleEffect(1.0 - normalizedDistance * 0.1)
            }
    }
}

    Combining with SwiftUI Transitions

    struct ShaderTransition: View {
    @State private var isShowing = false

    var body: some View {
        if isShowing {
            ContentView()
                .transition(.identity.combined(with:
                    .modifier(
                        active: ShaderModifier(progress: 0),
                        identity: ShaderModifier(progress: 1)
                    )
                ))
        }
    }
}

struct ShaderModifier: ViewModifier {
    var progress: Float

    func body(content: Content) -> some View {
        content
            .layerEffect(
                ShaderLibrary.dissolve(.float(progress)),
                maxSampleOffset: .zero
            )
    }
}

    Performance Tips

    1. Keep maxSampleOffset tight — larger values = more processing
    2. Use half4 return type — 2x faster on Apple GPUs
    3. Cap loop iterations — min(radius, 20.0) prevents GPU timeout
    4. Prefer colorEffect over layerEffect when you don't need neighbor reads
    5. Avoid layerEffect on large views with high sample offsets — it scales with view size x offset area

    Exercises

    1. Chromatic aberration: Write a layerEffect that splits R, G, B channels with slight offsets.

    2. Dissolve transition: Write a colorEffect that uses noise to dissolve a view (pixels randomly become transparent based on a progress value).

    3. Magnifier: Write a layerEffect that magnifies the area under a touch point.

    4. Scroll blur: Apply progressive blur to a ScrollView where items blur as they approach the edges.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> attribute makes shaders usable in SwiftUI - [x] Three types: colorEffect (color only), distortionEffect (position only), layerEffect (full access) - [x] Function signatures: position + color/layer + custom params - [x] Passing data: .float(), .float2(), .color(), .image() - [x] maxSampleOffset controls how far a shader can read from neighboring pixels - [x] TimelineView(.animation) for shader animation - [x] SwiftUI::Layer provides .sample(position) in layer effects

    The foundation

    • <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> = a label saying "this shader may be plugged into SwiftUI," just like throws labels a function.
    • colorEffect = recolors one pixel at a time; sees its own color, never the neighbors.
    • distortionEffect = leaves colors alone and instead moves where each pixel reads from — bend that lookup and you get ripples or fisheye.
    • layerEffect = the full-access one; hands you the whole view to sample anywhere, which is what blur and glow need.
    • maxSampleOffset = how far out your shader is allowed to reach for a pixel — set it to your real reach, no more.
    • SwiftUI::Layer.sample(position) = "give me the color at this spot" — the tool that lets a layer effect read its neighbors.
    • TimelineView(.animation) = a clock that re-renders every frame so you can feed elapsed time into a shader and make it move.
    Prev← 17 — Metal in UIKitNext19 — Metal Performance Shaders (MPS) →

    19 — Metal Performance Shaders (MPS)

    Start here — the plain idea

    You've been hand-writing shaders. MPS is Apple saying: "for the common stuff, don't bother — we already wrote it, and we tuned it for every chip we ship." Think of it as a shelf of pre-built power tools. Need a Gaussian blur, an edge detector, a histogram, an image resize? Grab the tool, point it at a source texture and a destination texture, and let it run.

    What makes MPS fast isn't magic — it's that Apple's engineers rewrote each operation specifically for each GPU generation, picking the exact tricks that hardware likes. Your custom shader is general-purpose; theirs is hand-tuned. So the rule of thumb is simple: if a built-in does what you want, it'll beat your version.

    The pattern is always the same: you encode a filter onto a command buffer, which is just adding it to the GPU's to-do list. And because they all share that list, you can chain them — blur, then sharpen, then scale — each feeding the next, all submitted in one go.

    Apple's built-in, hardware-optimized GPU operations. Use MPS when a built-in operation exists — it's always faster than your custom shader.

    Image Processing

    import MetalPerformanceShaders

// Gaussian Blur
let blur = MPSImageGaussianBlur(device: device, sigma: 10.0)
blur.encode(commandBuffer: cmdBuf, sourceTexture: src, destinationTexture: dst)

// Box Blur
let box = MPSImageBox(device: device, kernelWidth: 15, kernelHeight: 15)

// Tent Blur (triangle/linear)
let tent = MPSImageTent(device: device, kernelWidth: 15, kernelHeight: 15)

// Median Filter (noise reduction)
let median = MPSImageMedian(device: device, kernelDiameter: 5)

// Sobel Edge Detection
let sobel = MPSImageSobel(device: device, linearGrayColorTransform: nil)

// Laplacian Edge Detection
let laplacian = MPSImageLaplacian(device: device)

// Morphology (dilate/erode)
let dilate = MPSImageAreaMax(device: device, kernelWidth: 5, kernelHeight: 5)
let erode = MPSImageAreaMin(device: device, kernelWidth: 5, kernelHeight: 5)

// Threshold
let threshold = MPSImageThresholdBinary(device: device, thresholdValue: 0.5,
                                         maximumValue: 1.0, linearGrayColorTransform: nil)

// Histogram
let histogramInfo = MPSImageHistogramInfo(
    numberOfHistogramEntries: 256,
    histogramForAlpha: false,
    minPixelValue: vector_float4(0,0,0,0),
    maxPixelValue: vector_float4(1,1,1,1))
let histogram = MPSImageHistogram(device: device, histogramInfo: &histogramInfo)

// Scale/Resize
let scale = MPSImageBilinearScale(device: device)
// Or Lanczos for higher quality:
let lanczos = MPSImageLanczosScale(device: device)

    Chaining Operations

    let cmdBuf = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!

// Chain: blur -> sharpen -> scale
let blur = MPSImageGaussianBlur(device: device, sigma: 5.0)
let sharpen = MPSImageSharpen(device: device)
let scale = MPSImageLanczosScale(device: device)

blur.encode(commandBuffer: cmdBuf, sourceTexture: src, destinationTexture: temp1)
sharpen.encode(commandBuffer: cmdBuf, sourceTexture: temp1, destinationTexture: temp2)

var scaleTransform = MPSScaleTransform(scaleX: 0.5, scaleY: 0.5, translateX: 0, translateY: 0)
withUnsafePointer(to: &scaleTransform) { ptr in
    scale.scaleTransform = ptr
    scale.encode(commandBuffer: cmdBuf, sourceTexture: temp2, destinationTexture: dst)
}

cmdBuf.commit()

    In-Place Processing

    Some MPS filters can process in-place (read and write same texture):

    let blur = MPSImageGaussianBlur(device: device, sigma: 5.0)

var texture = sourceTexture  // Will be replaced
var fallback: MTLTexture? = nil

blur.encode(commandBuffer: cmdBuf, inPlaceTexture: &texture, fallbackCopyAllocator: nil)
// texture now points to the blurred result

    When to Use MPS vs Custom Shaders

    Use MPS Use Custom
    Standard blur (Gaussian, box) Variable blur, masked blur
    Edge detection (Sobel) Custom edge stylization
    Histogram equalization Artistic color grading
    Image scaling Custom interpolation
    Neural network inference Custom compute logic
    When performance is critical When MPS doesn't have what you need

    MPS is always faster for operations it supports — Apple optimizes for each GPU generation.

    Checkpoint

    • [x] MPS provides hardware-optimized image processing
    • [x] Common operations: blur, edge detect, morphology, histogram, scale
    • [x] Chain operations on the same command buffer
    • [x] In-place processing saves memory allocations
    • [x] Always prefer MPS over custom shaders when the operation matches

    The foundation

    • MPS = Apple's shelf of pre-built, per-chip-tuned GPU operations — use them instead of writing your own when one fits.
    • encode = adding a filter to the command buffer's to-do list; the GPU runs it when you commit.
    • source / destination texture = the "before" picture going in and the "after" picture coming out.
    • chaining = stacking filters on one command buffer so each one's output feeds the next (blur, sharpen, scale).
    • in-place processing = letting a filter overwrite the same texture it read, saving a memory allocation.
    • why MPS wins = it's hand-optimized for each GPU generation, so it beats a general-purpose custom shader at the same job.
    Prev← 18 — Metal Shaders in SwiftUI (iOS 17+)Next20 — Variable Blur →

    20 — Variable Blur

    Start here — the plain idea

    A normal blur treats every pixel the same — smear everything by the same amount. Variable blur is the small twist that makes it look professional: the blur amount changes across the image. Sharp where it matters, soft everywhere else. That's the scroll-edge fade at the top of a list, the dreamy out-of-focus background in portrait mode, the toy-town tilt-shift look.

    The trick is to give each pixel its own blur radius. Where does that number come from? Either a mask — a grayscale picture where white means "blur me a lot" and black means "leave me sharp" — or a formula based on position, like "the farther you are from the focus line, the blurrier you get." Same blur loop as before; you've just made the radius a variable instead of a constant.

    Behind the scenes the GPU still does the heavy thing — sample a circle of neighbors and average them — but now the size of that circle is decided per pixel. That's also why the performance tips matter: a big radius means a lot of neighbor reads, so you cap it, skip pixels the mask says are sharp, and for really heavy blur you cheat by blurring a shrunk-down copy and scaling back up.

    Different blur radius at different positions — the technique behind Apple's scroll edge blur, depth-of-field, and tilt-shift effects.

    Concept

    Standard blur: every pixel gets the same blur radius. Variable blur: each pixel's blur radius is controlled by a mask or formula.

    Mask image:       Result:
█████░░░░░        [blurry][sharp]
█████░░░░░   ->   [blurry][sharp]
█████░░░░░        [blurry][sharp]

White = full blur, Black = no blur

    Method 1: Mask-Driven (Texture Mask)

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 variableBlur(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float maxRadius, float maskY)
{
    // Linear mask: blur increases toward bottom
    float maskValue = smoothstep(maskY, size.y, position.y);
    float radius = maskValue * maxRadius;

    if (radius < 0.5) return layer.sample(position);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(min(radius, 16.0));

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float dist = length(float2(x, y));
            if (dist > radius) continue;

            float weight = 1.0 - (dist / radius);  // Linear falloff
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y)) * half(weight);
            totalWeight += weight;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(totalWeight);
}

    Method 2: Radial Variable Blur

    // Tilt-shift / miniature effect
<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 tiltShift(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float focusY, float focusRange, float maxBlur)
{
    float dist = abs(position.y - focusY);
    float blur = smoothstep(focusRange * 0.5, focusRange, dist) * maxBlur;

    if (blur < 0.5) return layer.sample(position);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float count = 0;
    int r = int(min(blur, 12.0));

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            if (length(float2(x,y)) > blur) continue;
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y));
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(count);
}

    Method 3: CAFilter variableBlur (Private API)

    The fastest approach — runs in the render server:

    // Create gradient mask image
let maskLayer = CAGradientLayer()
maskLayer.frame = CGRect(x: 0, y: 0, width: 400, height: 800)
maskLayer.colors = [UIColor.white.cgColor, UIColor.clear.cgColor]
maskLayer.startPoint = CGPoint(x: 0.5, y: 0.8)
maskLayer.endPoint = CGPoint(x: 0.5, y: 0.3)

UIGraphicsBeginImageContextWithOptions(maskLayer.bounds.size, false, 1)
maskLayer.render(in: UIGraphicsGetCurrentContext()!)
let maskImage = UIGraphicsGetImageFromCurrentImageContext()!
UIGraphicsEndImageContext()

// Apply variable blur
let filter = CAFilter(name: "variableBlur")
filter.setValue(30.0, forKey: "inputRadius")
filter.setValue(maskImage.cgImage!, forKey: "inputMaskImage")
myView.layer.setValue([filter], forKey: "filters")

    Performance Tips

    1. Cap the max radius — min(radius, 16.0) prevents GPU timeout
    2. Use separable passes for large radii — horizontal + vertical
    3. Downscale first for very large blur — blur at 1/4 resolution, upscale
    4. Skip zero-blur pixels — early return when mask is black

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Variable blur = different radius per pixel
    • [x] Controlled by mask texture, distance formula, or both
    • [x] CAFilter variableBlur is the fastest (private API, render server)
    • [x] Always cap max radius for performance
    • [x] Use for scroll edges, depth of field, tilt-shift, focus effects

    The foundation

    • variable blur = a blur whose radius changes per pixel, instead of one amount for the whole image.
    • mask = a grayscale guide picture; white = blur hard here, black = stay sharp.
    • distance formula = deriving the radius from position, e.g. "blurrier the farther you are from the focus line" (tilt-shift).
    • smoothstep = a soft ramp between two values, so the blur fades in gradually instead of switching on abruptly.
    • weighted average = summing neighbor colors with falloff weights and dividing — closer pixels count more.
    • cap the radius = limiting the neighbor loop so a huge blur can't stall the GPU.
    • downscale-then-upscale = blurring a shrunk copy for big radii, a cheap way to fake a wide blur.
    Prev← 19 — Metal Performance Shaders (MPS)Next21 — Glass and Refraction →

    21 — Glass and Refraction

    Start here — the plain idea

    Glass in software is a magic trick — there's no actual glass, just clever lying about pixels. "Frosted glass" is really a stack of small cheats layered together: blur the stuff behind it, pump up the color a bit so it doesn't look washed out, add a faint tint, drop in a soft highlight, and make it slightly see-through. Do all five and your brain says "that's glass."

    Refraction is just bending the lookup: instead of reading the pixel straight behind the glass, you read a slightly shifted one, so the background looks warped. Snell's law is only the recipe for how much to shift based on the angle and how dense the glass is. Chromatic aberration is the same shift done three times — red, green, and blue each grabbed from a slightly different spot — because real glass splits colors at its edges.

    "Liquid Glass" is just all of these turned on at once over a rounded shape, with a moving highlight and darker, more reflective edges (that edge effect is called Fresnel — surfaces get shinier the more you look along them). The GPU runs the whole stack per pixel, every frame, which is why it feels alive.

    The techniques behind Apple's frosted glass materials, Liquid Glass, and light refraction effects.

    Frosted Glass Recipe

    Apple's glass = backdrop blur + saturation boost + tint + specular highlight + compositing.

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 frostedGlass(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float blurRadius, half4 tintColor)
{
    // 1. Blur the background
    half4 blurred = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(min(blurRadius, 12.0));

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float weight = exp(-float(x*x + y*y) / (2.0 * blurRadius * blurRadius));
            blurred += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y)) * half(weight);
            totalWeight += weight;
        }
    }
    blurred /= half(totalWeight);

    // 2. Boost saturation (Apple uses ~1.8x)
    half luma = dot(blurred.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    blurred.rgb = mix(half3(luma), blurred.rgb, 1.8h);

    // 3. Apply tint
    blurred.rgb = mix(blurred.rgb, tintColor.rgb, tintColor.a);

    // 4. Add subtle highlight (plusL compositing)
    blurred.rgb = min(blurred.rgb + 0.08h, 1.0h);

    return half4(blurred.rgb, 0.92h);
}

    Refraction (Light Bending)

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 refraction(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float2 center, float radius, float ior)
{
    float2 diff = position - center;
    float dist = length(diff);

    if (dist > radius) return layer.sample(position);

    // Surface normal of the glass sphere
    float2 normal = diff / radius;
    float nz = sqrt(max(1.0 - dot(normal, normal), 0.0));

    // Snell's law refraction
    float eta = 1.0 / ior;  // ior: index of refraction (glass ~1.5)
    float3 incident = float3(0, 0, -1);
    float3 n = float3(normal, nz);
    float cosI = dot(-incident, n);
    float sinT2 = eta * eta * (1.0 - cosI * cosI);
    float3 refracted = eta * incident + (eta * cosI - sqrt(max(1.0 - sinT2, 0.0))) * n;

    // Offset the sample position
    float2 offset = refracted.xy * radius * 0.3;
    return layer.sample(position + offset);
}

    Chromatic Aberration

    Light splits into colors at glass edges:

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 chromaticAberration(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float strength)
{
    float2 center = size * 0.5;
    float2 dir = (position - center) / size;
    float dist = length(dir);

    float2 offset = normalize(dir) * dist * strength;

    half r = layer.sample(position + offset).r;
    half g = layer.sample(position).g;
    half b = layer.sample(position - offset).b;
    half a = layer.sample(position).a;

    return half4(r, g, b, a);
}

    Liquid Glass (iOS 26-Style)

    Combines refraction + blur + specular + Fresnel:

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 liquidGlass(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float2 glassCenter, float2 glassSize, float cornerRadius, float time)
{
    // Rounded rect SDF
    float2 p = abs(position - glassCenter) - glassSize * 0.5 + cornerRadius;
    float d = length(max(p, 0.0)) + min(max(p.x, p.y), 0.0) - cornerRadius;

    if (d > 2.0) return layer.sample(position);

    // Glass surface normal (from SDF gradient)
    float eps = 1.0;
    float dx = length(max(abs(position + float2(eps,0) - glassCenter) - glassSize*0.5 + cornerRadius, 0.0))
             - length(max(abs(position - float2(eps,0) - glassCenter) - glassSize*0.5 + cornerRadius, 0.0));
    float dy = length(max(abs(position + float2(0,eps) - glassCenter) - glassSize*0.5 + cornerRadius, 0.0))
             - length(max(abs(position - float2(0,eps) - glassCenter) - glassSize*0.5 + cornerRadius, 0.0));
    float2 normal = normalize(float2(dx, dy));

    // Refraction offset
    float refractionAmount = smoothstep(2.0, -10.0, d) * 12.0;
    float2 refracted = position + normal * refractionAmount;

    // Blur the refracted sample
    half4 blurred = half4(0);
    for (int y = -4; y <= 4; y++) {
        for (int x = -4; x <= 4; x++) {
            blurred += layer.sample(refracted + float2(x, y) * 1.5);
        }
    }
    blurred /= 81.0h;

    // Saturation boost
    half luma = dot(blurred.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    blurred.rgb = mix(half3(luma), blurred.rgb, 1.6h);

    // Specular highlight (animated light direction)
    float2 lightDir = normalize(float2(cos(time * 0.5), sin(time * 0.3)));
    float specular = pow(max(dot(normal, lightDir), 0.0), 24.0);

    // Fresnel (edges darker/more reflective)
    float fresnel = smoothstep(-8.0, 0.0, d);

    // Compose
    blurred.rgb += half(specular * 0.25);
    blurred.rgb *= half(1.0 - fresnel * 0.25);

    // Edge glow
    float edgeGlow = smoothstep(2.0, 0.0, abs(d)) * 0.15;
    blurred.rgb += half(edgeGlow);

    // Smooth edge alpha
    float alpha = smoothstep(2.0, -1.0, d);
    blurred.a = half(alpha) * 0.9h;

    return blurred;
}

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Frosted glass = blur + saturation boost + tint + highlight
    • [x] Refraction uses surface normals and Snell's law
    • [x] Chromatic aberration splits RGB with position-based offset
    • [x] Fresnel effect: edges appear different from center
    • [x] SDF gradient provides surface normals for any shape
    • [x] Liquid Glass combines all techniques with animated lighting

    The foundation

    • frosted glass = a stack of cheats — blur + saturation boost + tint + highlight + transparency — that together read as "glass."
    • refraction = bending the lookup; you sample a shifted pixel so the background looks warped.
    • index of refraction (IOR) = how dense the glass pretends to be, which sets how strongly the lookup bends (glass ~1.5).
    • Snell's law = the formula for how much to shift, based on the surface angle and the IOR.
    • chromatic aberration = sampling red, green, and blue from slightly different spots, mimicking how glass splits colors at its edges.
    • Fresnel = surfaces look shinier and more reflective at glancing angles, which is why glass edges read brighter.
    • SDF (signed distance field) = a math shape that tells you how far each pixel is from the edge, used here to make rounded glass and derive its surface normals.
    Prev← 20 — Variable BlurNext22 — Lighting and Normal Maps →

    22 — Lighting and Normal Maps

    Start here — the plain idea

    A flat image is flat because nothing on it reacts to light. Lighting fixes that, and the whole thing hinges on one idea: the normal — the direction a surface faces. Lighting is just measuring how much that direction points at the light. Face the light dead-on, you're bright. Turn away, you go dark. That single comparison (a dot product) is the heart of it.

    The Phong model just adds three layers of this. Ambient is the faint light that's everywhere so shadows aren't pitch black. Diffuse is the "how much do I face the light" part — the matte shading. Specular is the shiny hotspot you get when the light bounces off the surface straight into your eye. Stack them and a flat circle suddenly looks like a lit ball.

    The clever part is the normal map. Instead of giving every pixel the same facing direction, you store a different direction per pixel inside an image — packed as RGB, where red/green/blue encode the X/Y/Z of the direction. Now a flat wall can have fake bumps and grooves, because each pixel catches the light as if it were tilted, even though the geometry never changed. You can even cook one up from a height map by looking at how steeply brightness changes from pixel to pixel.

    Adding depth and dimension to flat surfaces using lighting calculations and normal maps.

    Phong Lighting Model

    Three components: ambient + diffuse + specular.

    struct Light {
    float3 position;
    float3 color;
    float intensity;
};

float3 phongLighting(float3 normal, float3 viewDir, float3 lightDir,
                      float3 lightColor, float3 surfaceColor) {
    // Ambient: base illumination
    float3 ambient = surfaceColor * 0.15;

    // Diffuse: how much the surface faces the light
    float diff = max(dot(normal, lightDir), 0.0);
    float3 diffuse = surfaceColor * lightColor * diff;

    // Specular: shiny highlight
    float3 reflectDir = reflect(-lightDir, normal);
    float spec = pow(max(dot(viewDir, reflectDir), 0.0), 32.0);
    float3 specular = lightColor * spec * 0.5;

    return ambient + diffuse + specular;
}

    Normal Maps

    Normal maps store surface detail as RGB colors where R=X, G=Y, B=Z direction:

    fragment half4 normalMapped(
    VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>,
    texture2d<half> colorTex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    texture2d<half> normalTex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(1)</span>,
    constant Light &light <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>)
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);

    half4 color = colorTex.sample(s, in.texCoord);

    // Read normal from map (stored as 0-1, remap to -1 to 1)
    half3 normal = normalTex.sample(s, in.texCoord).rgb * 2.0h - 1.0h;
    normal = normalize(normal);

    float3 lightDir = normalize(light.position - in.worldPosition);
    float3 viewDir = normalize(float3(0, 0, 1) - in.worldPosition);

    float3 lit = phongLighting(float3(normal), viewDir, lightDir,
                                light.color, float3(color.rgb));

    return half4(half3(lit), color.a);
}

    Generating Normals from Height Map

    Create a normal map from a grayscale height texture:

    kernel void heightToNormal(
    texture2d<float, access::read> heightMap <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>,
    texture2d<float, access::write> normalMap <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(1)</span>,
    uint2 gid <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>)
{
    float l = heightMap.read(uint2(max(int(gid.x)-1, 0), gid.y)).r;
    float r = heightMap.read(uint2(min(gid.x+1, heightMap.get_width()-1), gid.y)).r;
    float u = heightMap.read(uint2(gid.x, max(int(gid.y)-1, 0))).r;
    float d = heightMap.read(uint2(gid.x, min(gid.y+1, heightMap.get_height()-1))).r;

    float3 normal = normalize(float3(l - r, u - d, 2.0));
    normalMap.write(float4(normal * 0.5 + 0.5, 1.0), gid);  // Pack to 0-1
}

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Phong = ambient + diffuse (facing light) + specular (shiny highlight)
    • [x] Normal maps store per-pixel surface direction as RGB
    • [x] reflect() and dot() are the key lighting functions
    • [x] Normal maps can be generated from height maps via gradient

    The foundation

    • normal = the direction a surface faces; lighting is just how much that direction points at the light.
    • dot product = the "how much do these two directions agree" measurement that turns facing-the-light into a brightness number.
    • ambient = the faint everywhere-light so shadows aren't pure black.
    • diffuse = matte shading — brighter the more the surface faces the light.
    • specular = the shiny hotspot where the light bounces off into your eye.
    • normal map = an image storing a per-pixel facing direction (packed as RGB), so a flat surface can fake bumps.
    • height-to-normal = deriving those directions from a grayscale height map by measuring how steeply it rises pixel to pixel.
    Prev← 21 — Glass and RefractionNext23 — Animation in Shaders →

    23 — Animation in Shaders

    Start here — the plain idea

    A shader by itself is frozen — it computes the same picture forever. To make it move, you feed it one changing number: time. That's the whole secret. The GPU re-runs your shader every frame, and if you make the output depend on time, the picture changes frame to frame. Animation = "draw, but with a clock ticking in one variable."

    The fun is in shaping that ticking number. Raw time just climbs forever, so you fold it into useful patterns: sin(time) to gently oscillate back and forth, fract(time) to loop 0→1 over and over, step to blink on and off. Easing functions are just curves you bend that 0→1 progress through — ease-in starts slow, bounce overshoots and settles — so motion feels physical instead of robotic.

    Two more tricks round it out. Stagger means offsetting time by position, so each row or item starts a beat later — that ripple-down feel. And morphing blends two shapes by mix-ing their math together as progress goes 0→1, so a circle smoothly melts into a square. All of it is just arithmetic on a single moving number.

    Time-based animation, easing, and morphing — making shaders come alive.

    Passing Time

    // Swift: pass elapsed time to shader
var time = Float(CACurrentMediaTime() - startTime)
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&time, length: 4, index: 0)

    // Metal: use time for animation
fragment half4 animated(VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>, constant float &time <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>) {
    float pulse = sin(time * 3.0) * 0.5 + 0.5;  // Oscillate 0 to 1
    return half4(half(pulse), 0, 0, 1);
}

    Animation Patterns

    Oscillation

    float pulse = sin(time * speed) * 0.5 + 0.5;           // 0 to 1 smoothly
float blink = step(0.5, fract(time * frequency));       // On/off blinking
float triangle = abs(fract(time * speed) * 2.0 - 1.0);  // 0->1->0->1

    Easing in Shaders

    float easeInQuad(float t)    { return t * t; }
float easeOutQuad(float t)   { return t * (2.0 - t); }
float easeInOutQuad(float t) { return t < 0.5 ? 2.0*t*t : -1.0 + (4.0-2.0*t)*t; }
float easeOutBounce(float t) {
    if (t < 1.0/2.75) return 7.5625*t*t;
    else if (t < 2.0/2.75) { t -= 1.5/2.75; return 7.5625*t*t + 0.75; }
    else if (t < 2.5/2.75) { t -= 2.25/2.75; return 7.5625*t*t + 0.9375; }
    else { t -= 2.625/2.75; return 7.5625*t*t + 0.984375; }
}
float easeOutElastic(float t) {
    return pow(2.0, -10.0*t) * sin((t - 0.075) * (2.0*M_PI_F) / 0.3) + 1.0;
}

    Staggered Animation

    // Each row animates with a delay
float staggeredPulse(float2 uv, float time, float staggerDelay) {
    float rowIndex = floor(uv.y * 10.0);
    float localTime = time - rowIndex * staggerDelay;
    return saturate(localTime);  // Clamp to [0, 1]
}

    Morphing Between Shapes

    float morphCircleToSquare(float2 uv, float progress) {
    float2 p = uv * 2.0 - 1.0;

    // Circle SDF
    float circle = length(p) - 0.5;

    // Square SDF
    float2 d = abs(p) - 0.5;
    float square = length(max(d, 0.0)) + min(max(d.x, d.y), 0.0);

    // Interpolate
    float shape = mix(circle, square, progress);
    return 1.0 - smoothstep(-0.01, 0.01, shape);
}

    Looping and Ping-Pong

    float loop = fract(time * speed);              // 0->1, 0->1 (sawtooth)
float pingPong = abs(fract(time * speed) * 2.0 - 1.0);  // 0->1->0 (triangle)
float smoothLoop = smoothstep(0.0, 1.0, pingPong);       // Smooth ping-pong

    SwiftUI Animation Integration

    struct AnimatedShader: View {
    let start = Date()

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { context in
            let t = Float(context.date.timeIntervalSince(start))

            Rectangle()
                .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.animatedEffect(.float(t)))
        }
    }
}

    Checkpoint

    • [x] Pass time from Swift, use sin(time) and fract(time) for animation
    • [x] Easing functions shape the animation curve
    • [x] Stagger = delay based on position (row index, distance, etc.)
    • [x] Morph between SDFs with mix(shape1, shape2, progress)
    • [x] TimelineView(.animation) drives shader animation in SwiftUI

    The foundation

    • time = the one changing number you feed the shader; make output depend on it and the frozen picture starts moving.
    • sin(time) = a smooth back-and-forth oscillation, the workhorse of gentle pulsing motion.
    • fract(time) = the looping part — climbs 0→1, then snaps back to repeat (a sawtooth).
    • easing = bending a 0→1 progress through a curve so motion feels physical (slow start, bounce, elastic) instead of linear.
    • stagger = offsetting time by position so each row or item kicks off a beat later, giving a ripple feel.
    • morphing = mix-ing two shapes' math by a progress value so one smoothly becomes the other.
    • ping-pong = abs(fract(t)*2 - 1), a loop that goes 0→1→0 so the animation reverses instead of jumping.
    Prev← 22 — Lighting and Normal MapsNext24 — Shader Performance →

    24 — Shader Performance

    Start here — the plain idea

    Your shader doesn't run once — it runs for every pixel, millions of times per frame, 120 times a second. So a tiny inefficiency isn't tiny; it's multiplied into the millions. Performance work here is just shaving cost off the thing that gets repeated the most.

    A few facts about the GPU explain almost every rule. It loves doing simple math in parallel, and it has special fast hardware for half-precision numbers — so using half instead of float for colors is basically free speed. It hates branching: when threads hit an if, the GPU often runs both sides and throws one away, so you replace if/else with blending math like mix and step. And reading from a texture is comparatively slow — each lookup costs — so the name of the game is fewer reads (separable passes, sampling between pixels to get two for the price of one).

    The last danger is loops that don't know when to stop. A blur radius driven by user input could spin thousands of times and stall the whole GPU, so you cap it. Everything else — profiling in Xcode, downsampling before heavy effects — is just confirming where the cost actually is and cutting the biggest piece first.

    How to write shaders that run at 120fps on every device. Profile, optimize, and avoid common pitfalls.

    The Golden Rules

    1. Use half instead of float — 2x faster on Apple GPUs for colors and visual effects
    2. Minimize texture reads — each read is expensive; use linear sampling tricks
    3. Avoid branching — GPUs execute BOTH branches; use mix/step instead
    4. Cap loop iterations — unbounded loops can timeout the GPU
    5. Reduce overdraw — don't shade pixels you won't see

    half vs float

    // SLOW
fragment float4 slowShader(VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>, texture2d<float> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>) {
    float4 color = tex.sample(s, in.uv);
    float luminance = dot(color.rgb, float3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    return float4(float3(luminance), color.a);
}

// FAST (2x on Apple GPUs)
fragment half4 fastShader(VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>, texture2d<half> tex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>) {
    half4 color = tex.sample(s, in.uv);
    half luminance = dot(color.rgb, half3(0.2126h, 0.7152h, 0.0722h));
    return half4(half3(luminance), color.a);
}

    Apple GPUs have dedicated half-precision ALUs. For visual effects where you don't need 32-bit precision, half is strictly better.

    Branching

    // SLOW — GPU executes both branches, discards one
if (uv.x > 0.5) {
    color = expensiveEffectA(uv);
} else {
    color = expensiveEffectB(uv);
}

// FAST — math replaces branching
float mask = step(0.5, uv.x);
color = mix(expensiveEffectB(uv), expensiveEffectA(uv), mask);

// EXCEPTION: Early-out is OK when it skips large computation
if (distance > maxRadius) return layer.sample(position);  // Skip blur entirely
// This IS beneficial because ALL threads in the group skip together

    Texture Read Optimization

    // SLOW: Many redundant reads
for (int i = 0; i < 100; i++) {
    sum += texture.sample(s, uv + offset[i]);  // 100 reads!
}

// FASTER: Separable passes (2 x 10 instead of 100)
// Pass 1: 10 horizontal reads
// Pass 2: 10 vertical reads

// FASTEST: Linear sampling trick (sample between pixels)
// 1 bilinear read = 2 nearest reads
sum += texture.sample(linearSampler, uv + 1.5 * texelSize);  // Gets average of pixel 1 and 2

    Loop Optimization

    // DANGEROUS: Unbounded loop can timeout GPU
for (int i = 0; i < int(radius); i++) { ... }
// If radius = 1000, GPU will timeout/crash

// SAFE: Cap the iteration count
int r = min(int(radius), 16);  // Never more than 16 iterations
for (int i = -r; i <= r; i++) { ... }

// FASTER: Unroll small loops
// The compiler does this automatically for small constant counts
// But you can hint: #pragma unroll

    Memory Access Patterns

    // SLOW: Random access pattern
color = texture.read(uint2(randomX, randomY));

// FAST: Sequential access (cache-friendly)
// Threads in a threadgroup should read adjacent pixels
color = texture.read(gid);  // Each thread reads its own position

// FAST: Use threadgroup memory for shared data
threadgroup float4 tile[16][16];
tile[tid.y][tid.x] = texture.read(gid);
threadgroup_barrier(mem_flags::mem_threadgroup);
// Now read from tile instead of texture

    Profiling in Xcode

    GPU Frame Capture

    1. Run your app in Xcode
    2. Click the camera icon in the debug bar (or Debug > Capture GPU Frame)
    3. Select your draw call
    4. See per-pixel shader execution time

    Metal System Trace (Instruments)

    1. Product > Profile (Cmd+I)
    2. Select "Metal System Trace"
    3. Record
    4. Analyze GPU utilization, shader duration, command buffer timing

    Key Metrics

    Metric Target (120fps) Warning
    Frame time < 8.3ms > 8.3ms = frame drops
    GPU utilization < 80% > 90% = thermal throttle risk
    Texture reads/pixel < 20 > 50 = optimize algorithm
    Shader ALU time < 4ms > 6ms = simplify math

    Common Optimizations Checklist

    • [ ] Use half4 return type and half intermediates for colors
    • [ ] Use texture2d<half> instead of texture2d<float> for 8-bit textures
    • [ ] Cap blur radius to max 16-20
    • [ ] Use separable blur (2 passes) instead of 2D kernel
    • [ ] Replace if/else with mix(a, b, step(edge, x))
    • [ ] Early-return for pixels outside effect radius
    • [ ] Use constexpr sampler (compile-time) instead of runtime sampler
    • [ ] Set framebufferOnly = true on MTKView (unless you need readback)
    • [ ] Use .drawingGroup() in SwiftUI to batch rendering
    • [ ] Downsample before heavy effects, then upscale

    Checkpoint

    • [x] half = 2x faster than float on Apple GPUs
    • [x] Replace branching with math (mix, step, smoothstep)
    • [x] Cap loop iterations to prevent GPU timeout
    • [x] Minimize texture reads; use linear sampling tricks
    • [x] Profile with GPU Frame Capture and Metal System Trace
    • [x] Target < 8.3ms frame time for 120fps

    The foundation

    • per-pixel cost = your shader runs millions of times a frame, so every small saving gets multiplied massively.
    • half vs float = Apple GPUs have dedicated half-precision hardware, so half colors run ~2x faster than float for free.
    • branching is expensive = the GPU often runs both sides of an if, so replace it with mix/step math.
    • early-out exception = a return that all threads take together (e.g. skip the blur outside a radius) is genuinely faster.
    • texture reads are slow = minimize lookups with separable passes and linear sampling (one bilinear read = two pixels averaged).
    • cap loops = bound iteration counts so a user-driven radius can't spin forever and stall the GPU.
    • frame budget = 120fps means under 8.3ms per frame; profiling tells you which step is eating it.
    Prev← 23 — Animation in ShadersNext25 — Real-World Apple Effects: Recreating iOS Animations →

    25 — Real-World Apple Effects: Recreating iOS Animations

    Start here — the plain idea

    This is the payoff lesson. Every "wow, how did Apple do that" effect — the blurry nav bar, the menu items that deblur and spring into place, the glassy Send button — turns out to be the same handful of tricks you already know, stacked and tuned just right. Nothing here is new physics; it's recipe-following.

    The recurring pattern is worth naming: blur the background, boost its saturation so it doesn't look gray, add a faint white highlight, give it continuous (smooth) corners, and animate everything with springs instead of linear timing. Sprinkle in a tiny per-item delay (stagger) and a little haptic tap, and it feels like iOS. You'll also see two roads to the same look — the private-API CAFilter/CABackdropLayer route that matches Apple exactly, and the public Metal-shader route you can ship.

    Behind the scenes, the GPU is doing the grunt work every frame: sampling neighbors for blur, bending lookups for refraction, splitting colors at edges. The "magic" is just knowing which dials Apple turns and by how much — saturation around 1.6–1.8x, springs with stiffness 200–400, stagger of 0.03–0.05s. Learn the dial settings and you can rebuild almost any of it.

    Applying everything you've learned to recreate the exact effects Apple uses in iOS.

    Effect 1: Navigation Bar Blur (UIVisualEffectView Equivalent)

    What Apple does: CABackdropLayer + CAFilter(gaussianBlur) + CAFilter(colorSaturate) + compositing filter.

    Private API Version (Exact Match)

    // No shader needed — this uses CAFilter (render server native)

    class AppleBlurView: UIView {
    override class var layerClass: AnyClass {
        NSClassFromString("CABackdropLayer")!
    }

    override init(frame: CGRect) {
        super.init(frame: frame)

        layer.setValue(true, forKey: "windowServerAware")
        layer.setValue(UUID().uuidString, forKey: "groupName")

        let blur = CAFilter(name: "gaussianBlur")
        blur.setValue(20.0, forKey: "inputRadius")
        blur.setValue(true, forKey: "inputNormalizeEdges")

        let saturate = CAFilter(name: "colorSaturate")
        saturate.setValue(1.8, forKey: "inputAmount")

        layer.setValue([blur, saturate], forKey: "filters")

        // Subtle white overlay
        let tint = CALayer()
        tint.frame = bounds
        tint.backgroundColor = UIColor.white.withAlphaComponent(0.1).cgColor
        tint.setValue("plusL", forKey: "compositingFilter")
        layer.addSublayer(tint)

        layer.setValue(true, forKey: "continuousCorners")
    }

    required init?(coder: NSCoder) { fatalError() }
}

    SwiftUI Version (Public API)

    // Progressive blur for scroll header
<span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 navBarBlur(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
                                 float2 size, float blurRadius) {
    // Only blur the top portion
    float navBarHeight = 100.0;
    if (position.y > navBarHeight) return layer.sample(position);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(min(blurRadius, 15.0));

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float weight = exp(-float(x*x + y*y) / (2.0 * blurRadius * blurRadius));
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y)) * half(weight);
            totalWeight += weight;
        }
    }

    half4 blurred = sum / half(totalWeight);

    // Apple's signature saturation boost
    half luma = dot(blurred.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    blurred.rgb = mix(half3(luma), blurred.rgb, 1.8h);

    // Subtle white tint
    blurred.rgb += 0.05h;

    return blurred;
}

    Effect 2: iMessage Send Menu Staggered Deblur

    What Apple does: Each cell starts with high gaussianBlur radius and animates to 0, staggered by index.

    Private API Version

    func animateDeblur(cells: [UIView], fromButton buttonFrame: CGRect) {
    for (index, cell) in cells.enumerated() {
        let delay = Double(index) * 0.035

        // Start blurred
        let blur = CAFilter(name: "gaussianBlur")
        blur.setValue(20.0, forKey: "inputRadius")
        blur.setValue(true, forKey: "inputNormalizeEdges")
        cell.layer.setValue([blur], forKey: "filters")
        cell.alpha = 0

        // Animate deblur
        let blurAnim = CABasicAnimation(keyPath: "filters.gaussianBlur.inputRadius")
        blurAnim.fromValue = 20.0
        blurAnim.toValue = 0.0
        blurAnim.beginTime = CACurrentMediaTime() + delay
        blurAnim.duration = 0.35
        blurAnim.timingFunction = CAMediaTimingFunction(name: .easeOut)
        blurAnim.fillMode = .backwards
        cell.layer.add(blurAnim, forKey: "deblur")

        // Animate position from button origin
        let posAnim = CASpringAnimation(keyPath: "position.y")
        posAnim.fromValue = buttonFrame.midY
        posAnim.toValue = cell.center.y
        posAnim.mass = 1; posAnim.stiffness = 280; posAnim.damping = 24
        posAnim.beginTime = CACurrentMediaTime() + delay
        posAnim.duration = posAnim.settlingDuration
        posAnim.fillMode = .backwards
        cell.layer.add(posAnim, forKey: "position")

        // Fade in
        UIView.animate(withDuration: 0.25, delay: delay) {
            cell.alpha = 1
        }
    }
}

    SwiftUI Version

    struct StaggeredDeblurList: View {
    @State private var isShowing = false

    var body: some View {
        VStack(spacing: 0) {
            ForEach(Array(items.enumerated()), id: \.element.id) { index, item in
                MenuRow(item: item)
                    .blur(radius: isShowing ? 0 : 20)
                    .opacity(isShowing ? 1 : 0)
                    .offset(y: isShowing ? 0 : -50)
                    .animation(
                        .spring(mass: 1, stiffness: 280, damping: 24)
                        .delay(Double(index) * 0.035),
                        value: isShowing
                    )
            }
        }
    }
}

    Effect 3: Scroll Edge Blur (Messages Send Menu)

    What Apple does: calculatePercentEdgeEffected computes proximity to scroll edges, applies proportional blur + opacity.

    Metal Shader Version

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 scrollEdgeBlur(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float edgeDistance)
{
    float topEdge = edgeDistance;
    float bottomEdge = size.y - edgeDistance;

    float topFade = smoothstep(0, topEdge, position.y);
    float bottomFade = smoothstep(size.y, bottomEdge, position.y);
    float edgeFactor = 1.0 - min(topFade, bottomFade);

    if (edgeFactor < 0.01) return layer.sample(position);

    // Blur proportional to edge proximity
    float radius = edgeFactor * 8.0;
    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(radius);

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float weight = exp(-float(x*x + y*y) / (2.0 * radius * radius));
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y)) * half(weight);
            totalWeight += weight;
        }
    }

    half4 blurred = sum / half(totalWeight);
    blurred.a *= half(1.0 - edgeFactor * 0.6);  // Also fade opacity

    return blurred;
}

    Effect 4: Glass Material Button

    What Apple does: CKGlassSendButton with CABackdropLayer + plusL compositing + blur animate.

    class GlassButton: UIControl {
    private let backdropView: UIView
    private let highlightLayer = CALayer()
    private let label = UILabel()

    override init(frame: CGRect) {
        let backdropClass = NSClassFromString("_UIBackdropView") as! UIView.Type
        let settings = (NSClassFromString("_UIBackdropViewSettings") as! NSObject.Type)
            .perform(NSSelectorFromString("settingsForStyle:"), with: 2060)!
            .takeUnretainedValue()
        settings.setValue(20.0, forKey: "blurRadius")
        settings.setValue(1.8, forKey: "saturationDeltaFactor")

        backdropView = backdropClass.perform(NSSelectorFromString("alloc"))!
            .takeUnretainedValue()
            .perform(NSSelectorFromString("initWithSettings:"), with: settings)!
            .takeUnretainedValue() as! UIView

        super.init(frame: frame)

        addSubview(backdropView)
        backdropView.frame = bounds
        backdropView.layer.cornerRadius = bounds.height / 2
        backdropView.layer.setValue(true, forKey: "continuousCorners")
        backdropView.clipsToBounds = true

        // Plus-L highlight
        highlightLayer.frame = bounds
        highlightLayer.backgroundColor = UIColor.white.withAlphaComponent(0.12).cgColor
        highlightLayer.setValue("plusL", forKey: "compositingFilter")
        backdropView.layer.addSublayer(highlightLayer)

        label.textColor = .white
        label.textAlignment = .center
        addSubview(label)
    }

    // Press animation
    override var isHighlighted: Bool {
        didSet {
            let scale: CGFloat = isHighlighted ? 0.92 : 1.0
            let spring = CASpringAnimation(keyPath: "transform.scale")
            spring.toValue = scale
            spring.mass = 1; spring.stiffness = 400; spring.damping = 30
            spring.duration = spring.settlingDuration
            layer.add(spring, forKey: "press")
            layer.setValue(scale, forKeyPath: "transform.scale")

            // Haptic
            if isHighlighted {
                UIImpactFeedbackGenerator(style: .light).impactOccurred()
            }
        }
    }

    required init?(coder: NSCoder) { fatalError() }
}

    Effect 5: Liquid Glass Refraction

    What Apple uses internally: custom Metal shaders in ChatKit's default.metallib.

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 liquidGlass(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float time)
{
    float2 uv = position / size;
    float2 center = float2(0.5, 0.5);

    // SDF for rounded rectangle (the glass shape)
    float2 p = abs(uv - center) - float2(0.3, 0.15);
    float d = length(max(p, 0.0)) + min(max(p.x, p.y), 0.0) - 0.05;

    if (d > 0.02) return layer.sample(position);  // Outside glass

    // Refraction distortion (light bends at glass edges)
    float2 normal = normalize(float2(
        length(max(abs(uv + float2(0.001, 0) - center) - float2(0.3, 0.15), 0.0)) -
        length(max(abs(uv - float2(0.001, 0) - center) - float2(0.3, 0.15), 0.0)),
        length(max(abs(uv + float2(0, 0.001) - center) - float2(0.3, 0.15), 0.0)) -
        length(max(abs(uv - float2(0, 0.001) - center) - float2(0.3, 0.15), 0.0))
    ));

    float refractionStrength = smoothstep(0.02, -0.05, d) * 15.0;
    float2 refractedPos = position + normal * refractionStrength;

    // Blur the refracted sample
    half4 sum = half4(0);
    for (int y = -3; y <= 3; y++) {
        for (int x = -3; x <= 3; x++) {
            sum += layer.sample(refractedPos + float2(x, y) * 2.0);
        }
    }
    half4 blurred = sum / 49.0h;

    // Boost saturation (Apple's signature)
    half luma = dot(blurred.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    blurred.rgb = mix(half3(luma), blurred.rgb, 1.6h);

    // Specular highlight (light reflection on glass surface)
    float specular = pow(max(dot(normal, normalize(float2(-0.5, -0.7))), 0.0), 16.0);
    blurred.rgb += half(specular * 0.3);

    // Edge darkening (Fresnel)
    float fresnel = smoothstep(-0.05, 0.0, d);
    blurred.rgb *= half(1.0 - fresnel * 0.3);

    // Chromatic aberration at edges
    if (d > -0.02) {
        float abAmount = smoothstep(-0.02, 0.0, d) * 3.0;
        half r = layer.sample(refractedPos + normal * abAmount).r;
        half b = layer.sample(refractedPos - normal * abAmount).b;
        blurred.r = r;
        blurred.b = b;
    }

    return blurred;
}

    Effect 6: Photo Scrubber Blur (Photos App)

    Thumbnails blur as they move away from the center selection.

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 scrubberBlur(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 center, float focusRadius, float maxBlur)
{
    float dist = abs(position.x - center.x);

    if (dist < focusRadius) return layer.sample(position);

    float blurAmount = smoothstep(focusRadius, focusRadius + 100.0, dist) * maxBlur;
    int r = int(min(blurAmount, 10.0));

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float count = 0;

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y));
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }

    half4 result = sum / half(count);
    result.a *= half(1.0 - smoothstep(focusRadius + 50.0, focusRadius + 200.0, dist) * 0.4);

    return result;
}

    The Complete Apple Animation Recipe

    When recreating any Apple animation, follow this order:

    1. Spring physics — mass=1, stiffness=200-400, damping=18-30
    2. CAFilter blur — for deblur/blur transitions (private API)
    3. Continuous corners — layer.cornerCurve = .continuous
    4. Saturated backdrop — blur + saturation boost (1.6-1.8x)
    5. plusL compositing — for light highlights on glass
    6. Staggered timing — 0.03-0.05s delay per item
    7. Haptic punctuation — .light on press, .medium on complete
    8. Premultiplied alpha — always premultiply

    This is the formula behind nearly every polished Apple UI animation.

    Checkpoint

    Before completing this series, you should be able to: - [x] Recreate navigation bar blur using CAFilter/CABackdropLayer - [x] Build staggered deblur animations (iMessage Send Menu style) - [x] Implement scroll edge blur effects - [x] Create glass material buttons with proper compositing - [x] Write liquid glass refraction shaders - [x] Apply the complete Apple animation recipe to your own UI

    The foundation

    • the Apple recipe = blur + saturation boost + faint highlight + continuous corners + spring timing, stacked together.
    • CABackdropLayer / CAFilter = the private-API route that runs in the render server and matches Apple's look exactly.
    • public Metal route = the same effects rebuilt as <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> shaders you can actually ship.
    • saturation boost (1.6-1.8x) = pumping color back into a blurred backdrop so it doesn't look washed-out gray.
    • plusL compositing = an additive blend that lays a soft light highlight onto glass.
    • spring physics = mass/stiffness/damping (≈1 / 200-400 / 18-30) that make motion settle naturally instead of linearly.
    • stagger (0.03-0.05s/item) = a small per-item delay that makes a group animate in sequence, like the iMessage menu.
    • continuous corners = the smooth, non-circular rounding (cornerCurve = .continuous) that gives Apple shapes their look.
    Prev← 24 — Shader PerformanceNextCard Shader Reflections — PBR Metallic Card →

    Card Shader Reflections — PBR Metallic Card

    Start here — the plain idea

    Picture one of those shiny metal credit cards — silver, gold, platinum — and how the light slides across the surface as you tilt it. That's the effect here: a flat rectangle that looks like polished metal, with reflections that move as you scroll.

    Nothing is actually 3D. The "metal" is a lie painted by a fragment shader — a tiny function that runs once per pixel and decides what color that pixel should be. The trick has three parts: (1) a real physics formula for how metal reflects light at different angles (that's why gold looks warm head-on but shifts at a glance), (2) a fake "brushed grain" texture made from noise so it isn't mirror-smooth, and (3) a pretend environment of light blobs that the surface reflects. Tie those to the scroll angle and the card comes alive.

    Think of it as a stage magician's mirror: there's no real room behind it, just a clever painting that updates as you move.

    Physically-based metallic card rendering with Fresnel conductor reflections, environment mapping, normal-mapped micro-detail, and dynamic lighting — all driven by scroll gesture.

    How It Works

    Architecture

    ScrollView (horizontal)
  └─ Card[] (each has a Skia Canvas)
       └─ <Shader source={cardShader} uniforms={{ rotate, resolution, cardType }} />
    • scrollX → interpolated to rotation in range [-π, π]
    • Each card is also rotated in 3D via CSS transforms (perspective 1200, rotateY, base 18° tilt)
    • The shader renders the metallic surface; the card geometry is just a flat rect

    Uniforms

    Uniform Type Description
    rotate float Card rotation angle in radians (-π to π), from scroll offset
    resolution vec2 Card pixel dimensions
    cardType float Material selector: 0=Silver, 1=Gold, 2=Platinum, 3=Metal

    Core Shader Techniques

    1. Fresnel Conductor Equation (Real Metal Physics)

    This is NOT the cheap Schlick approximation. It uses the full Fresnel equations for conductors (metals), which have complex refractive indices (n + ik):

    vec3 fresnelConductor(float cosTheta, vec3 reflectivity, vec3 edgeTint) {
    vec3 r = clamp(reflectivity, vec3(0.0), vec3(0.99));
    vec3 r_sqrt = sqrt(r);
    // Derive n (refractive index) from reflectivity + edge tint
    vec3 n = mix((1.0 + r_sqrt) / (1.0 - r_sqrt), (1.0 - r) / (1.0 + r), edgeTint);
    // Derive k² (extinction coefficient) from n and reflectivity
    vec3 k2 = ((n + 1.0) * (n + 1.0) * r - (n - 1.0) * (n - 1.0)) / (1.0 - r);
    k2 = max(k2, vec3(0.0));
    vec3 k = sqrt(k2);
    // Full Fresnel: average of s-polarized and p-polarized reflectance
    vec3 rs_num = pow2(n) + pow2(k) - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + pow2(vec3(cosTheta));
    vec3 rs_den = pow2(n) + pow2(k) + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + pow2(vec3(cosTheta));
    vec3 rs = rs_num / rs_den;
    vec3 rp_num = (pow2(n) + pow2(k)) * pow2(vec3(cosTheta)) - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + vec3(1.0);
    vec3 rp_den = (pow2(n) + pow2(k)) * pow2(vec3(cosTheta)) + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + vec3(1.0);
    vec3 rp = rp_num / rp_den;
    return clamp(0.5 * (rs + rp), vec3(0.0), vec3(1.0));
}

    Why this matters: Real metals reflect different wavelengths at different angles. Gold looks yellow head-on but shifts warm at grazing angles. This equation captures that per-channel. Schlick approximation cannot.

    2. Perlin Noise Normal Mapping (Brushed Metal Grain)

    Generates a normal map procedurally from layered noise:

    float calculateMetallicDetail(vec2 uv, float cardType) {
    float fine   = noise(uv * 1600.0) * 0.35;   // fine grain
    float medium = noise(uv * 800.0)  * 0.3;     // medium grain
    float rough  = noise(uv * 400.0)  * 0.2;     // large grain
    // Directional grain (rotated 15°) for brushed look
    vec2 rotatedUV = vec2(
        uv.x * cos(PI * 0.15) - uv.y * sin(PI * 0.15),
        uv.x * sin(PI * 0.15) + uv.y * cos(PI * 0.15)
    );
    float directional = noise(rotatedUV * 1000.0) * 0.4;
    return (fine + medium + rough + directional) * 0.4 * intensity;
}

vec3 calculateNormal(vec2 uv, float normalStrength, float cardType) {
    vec2 eps = vec2(1.0) / resolution;
    float c = calculateMetallicDetail(uv, cardType);
    float r = calculateMetallicDetail(uv + vec2(eps.x, 0.0), cardType);
    float t = calculateMetallicDetail(uv + vec2(0.0, eps.y), cardType);
    return normalize(vec3(
        (r - c) * normalStrength * 2.0,
        (t - c) * normalStrength * 2.0,
        1.0
    ));
}

    Technique: Finite-difference normal map from height field. Sample noise at 3 points (center, right, up), compute derivatives → surface normal.

    3. Environment Mapping
