    Fake environment map using spherical UV mapping + procedural light blobs:

    vec3 envMap(vec3 r, float roughness, float type) {
    vec2 envUV = vec2(atan(r.z, r.x) / (2.0 * PI) + 0.5, acos(r.y) / PI);
    // Two light positions in env map
    float intensity1 = pow(1.0 - distance(envUV, vec2(0.3, 0.4)) * 1.8, 6.0);
    float intensity2 = pow(1.0 - distance(envUV, vec2(0.7, 0.6)) * 1.8, 6.0);
    vec3 envColor = baseColor + highlight1 * intensity1 * 1.6 + highlight2 * intensity2 * 1.6;
    // Grazing angle color shift
    float colorShift = pow(1.0 - r.y, 4.0) * 0.8;
    return mix(envColor, mixColor, roughness);
}

    4. Dynamic Lighting

    Light position rotates with the card but reversed (so light appears fixed in world space):

    vec3 calculateDynamicLight(vec2 uv, float rotationAngle) {
    float c = cos(-rotationAngle);  // negative = light stays fixed
    float s = sin(-rotationAngle);
    vec3 rotatedLight = vec3(
        baseLight.x * c - baseLight.z * s,
        baseLight.y,
        baseLight.x * s + baseLight.z * c
    );
    return normalize(rotatedLight);
}

    5. Final Composition

    finalColor = mix(baseColor, envColor, fresnel)
           * diffuse lighting
           + specular highlights
           * metallic grain pattern
           + motion-based contrast
           + dynamic vignette
           + color shift from rotation

    Material Properties

    Property Silver Gold Platinum Metal
    Reflectivity 0.92, 0.92, 0.92 0.92, 0.88, 0.78 0.99, 0.98, 0.97 0.96, 0.96, 0.96
    Roughness 0.32 0.32 0.30 0.32
    Normal Strength 0.20 0.13 0.14 0.16
    Micro Detail 1.6 1.0 1.0 1.4

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Uniforms {
    float rotate;
    float2 resolution;
    float cardType;
};

// --- Utility ---
float2 pow2(float2 x) { return x * x; }
float3 pow2(float3 x) { return x * x; }

float hash(float2 p) {
    float3 p3 = fract(float3(p.xyx) * float3(443.897, 441.423, 437.195));
    p3 += dot(p3, p3.yzx + 19.19);
    return fract((p3.x + p3.y) * p3.z);
}

float noise(float2 p) {
    float2 i = floor(p);
    float2 f = fract(p);
    f = f * f * (3.0 - 2.0 * f);
    float a = hash(i);
    float b = hash(i + float2(1.0, 0.0));
    float c = hash(i + float2(0.0, 1.0));
    float d = hash(i + float2(1.0, 1.0));
    return mix(mix(a, b, f.x), mix(c, d, f.x), f.y);
}

// --- Fresnel Conductor ---
float3 fresnelConductor(float cosTheta, float3 reflectivity, float3 edgeTint) {
    float3 r = clamp(reflectivity, float3(0.0), float3(0.99));
    float3 r_sqrt = sqrt(r);
    float3 n = mix((1.0 + r_sqrt) / (1.0 - r_sqrt),
                   (1.0 - r) / (1.0 + r), edgeTint);
    float3 k2 = ((n + 1.0) * (n + 1.0) * r - (n - 1.0) * (n - 1.0)) / (1.0 - r);
    k2 = max(k2, float3(0.0));
    float3 k = sqrt(k2);

    float ct2 = cosTheta * cosTheta;
    float3 n2k2 = n * n + k2;
    float3 rs = (n2k2 - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + ct2)
              / (n2k2 + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + ct2);
    float3 rp = (n2k2 * ct2 - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + 1.0)
              / (n2k2 * ct2 + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + 1.0);
    return clamp(0.5 * (rs + rp), float3(0.0), float3(1.0));
}

// --- Metallic Detail ---
float metallicDetail(float2 uv, float cardType, float2 res) {
    float fine   = noise(uv * 1600.0) * 0.35;
    float medium = noise(uv * 800.0)  * 0.3;
    float rough  = noise(uv * 400.0)  * 0.2;
    // Directional grain at 15°
    float angle = M_PI_F * 0.15;
    float2 rotUV = float2(uv.x * cos(angle) - uv.y * sin(angle),
                          uv.x * sin(angle) + uv.y * cos(angle));
    float dir = noise(rotUV * 1000.0) * 0.4;
    return (fine + medium + rough + dir) * 0.4;
}

float3 calcNormal(float2 uv, float strength, float cardType, float2 res) {
    float2 eps = 1.0 / res;
    float c = metallicDetail(uv, cardType, res);
    float r = metallicDetail(uv + float2(eps.x, 0), cardType, res);
    float t = metallicDetail(uv + float2(0, eps.y), cardType, res);
    return normalize(float3((r - c) * strength * 2.0,
                            (t - c) * strength * 2.0,
                            1.0));
}

// --- Fragment Shader ---
// Use as: .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.cardShader(...))
// Or in MTKView fragment function:

fragment float4 cardFragment(
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>
) {
    float2 uv = position.xy / u.resolution;
    float3 viewDir = normalize(float3(uv * 2.0 - 1.0, 1.0));

    // Material selection (simplified — expand per cardType)
    float3 reflectivity = float3(0.92); // silver default
    float3 edgeTint = float3(0.94);
    float3 baseColor = float3(0.15);
    float roughness = 0.32;

    // Dynamic light (rotates opposite to card)
    float c = cos(-u.rotate), s = sin(-u.rotate);
    float3 lightDir = normalize(float3(-3.0 * c - 2.5 * s, -0.8, -3.0 * s + 2.5 * c));

    // Normal from procedural grain
    float3 normal = calcNormal(uv, 0.2, u.cardType, u.resolution);

    // Fresnel
    float NdotV = max(dot(normal, viewDir), 0.0);
    float3 F = fresnelConductor(NdotV, reflectivity, edgeTint);

    // Environment reflection
    float3 reflectDir = reflect(-viewDir, normal);
    float2 envUV = float2(atan2(reflectDir.z, reflectDir.x) / (2.0 * M_PI_F) + 0.5,
                          acos(reflectDir.y) / M_PI_F);
    float i1 = pow(max(1.0 - distance(envUV, float2(0.3, 0.4)) * 1.8, 0.0), 6.0);
    float i2 = pow(max(1.0 - distance(envUV, float2(0.7, 0.6)) * 1.8, 0.0), 6.0);
    float3 envColor = baseColor + float3(0.9) * i1 * 1.6 + float3(0.85) * i2 * 1.6;

    // Specular
    float3 halfDir = normalize(lightDir + viewDir);
    float NdotH = max(dot(normal, halfDir), 0.0);
    float NdotL = max(dot(normal, lightDir), 0.0);
    float specPower = (1.0 - roughness) * 156.0 + 16.0;
    float spec = pow(NdotH, specPower);

    // Compose
    float3 color = mix(baseColor, envColor, F * 0.8);
    color = color * (0.5 + 0.7 * pow(NdotL, 1.4)) + baseColor * spec * 1.2;

    // Metallic grain
    float grain = metallicDetail(uv, u.cardType, u.resolution);
    color *= (1.0 + grain * 0.35);

    // Vignette
    float2 vc = float2(0.5 + sin(u.rotate) * 0.15, 0.5 + cos(u.rotate * 0.7) * 0.1);
    float vignette = 1.0 - length((uv - vc) * 1.5);
    color *= mix(0.85, 1.0, vignette);

    return float4(color, 1.0);
}

    SwiftUI Integration (iOS 17+)

    // Option A: .colorEffect shader modifier
ContentView()
    .colorEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.cardShader(
            .float(rotation),          // rotate
            .float2(width, height),    // resolution
            .float(cardType)           // cardType
        )
    )

// Option B: MTKView for full control (recommended for this complexity)
struct MetalCardView: UIViewRepresentable {
    let rotation: Float
    let cardType: Int
    func makeUIView(context: Context) -> MTKView { ... }
}

    The foundation

    • Fresnel conductor = the real-metal formula for how much light reflects at a given angle, computed per color channel so gold can shift warm at grazing angles.
    • Normal mapping = faking tiny surface bumps by sampling noise and measuring its slope, so flat geometry catches light like brushed metal.
    • Environment map = a pretend surrounding world (here, a couple of glowing light blobs) that the surface "reflects" instead of a real room.
    • Finite-difference normal = compute the bump direction by comparing a point to its right and up neighbors — slope = direction the surface faces.
    • Dynamic light = the light rotates opposite to the card so it appears pinned in place while the card turns.
    • Uniforms = the few values Swift hands the shader each frame (rotation, resolution, which metal) — think of them as the dials on the effect.

    GitHub Terrain — WebGPU 3D Contribution Graph

    Start here — the plain idea

    You know the green GitHub contribution grid? This effect takes that flat grid and pushes each square up into a little 3D block — busier days become taller towers — so the whole thing looks like a tiny landscape you can tilt and zoom. Then it can morph smoothly back down to the flat 2D grid.

    This is the first lesson with the full pipeline, so here's the key picture. A vertex shader runs once per corner point and decides where each block's corners land on screen (it builds the box, tilts it into the isometric view, and figures out lighting and shadows). A fragment shader then runs once per pixel and decides the final color — adding shading, shadows cast by taller neighbors, warm peaks, cool valleys, and a little film grain.

    The core trick is height-as-data: contribution counts become block heights, and almost every nice touch (shadows, ambient occlusion, peak glow) comes from each block asking "how tall are my neighbors?" by looking them up in a height grid.

    Full vertex + fragment shader pipeline in WGSL that renders GitHub contribution data as an interactive 3D terrain with isometric projection, shadow casting, ambient occlusion, rim lighting, fresnel, atmospheric haze, and film grain.

    How It Works

    Architecture

    Contribution data (365+ blocks)
  → Buffer bindings (colors, positions, heights per block)
  → Vertex shader: extrude blocks to 3D, isometric projection, lighting calc
  → Fragment shader: shadow blending, post-processing, film grain
  → react-native-wgpu Canvas

    Uniforms

    Uniform Type Description
    aspectRatio f32 Canvas aspect ratio
    time f32 Animation time
    blockCount f32 Number of contribution blocks
    progress f32 0 = 3D isometric, 1 = flat 2D grid (morphing)
    dataProgress f32 Blend between two data sets (A → B)
    zoomScale f32 Pinch-to-zoom multiplier

    Storage Buffers

    Buffer Content
    blockColors array<u32> — contribution level (0-4, 6=card) per block
    blockPositions array<vec4f> — grid col/row position
    blockHeights array<f32> — extrusion height per block
    heightGrid array<f32> — height lookup for shadow/AO neighbor queries
    *B variants Second dataset for animated data transitions

    Vertex Shader Deep Dive

    1. Block Geometry (6 faces × 6 verts each = 36 verts/block)

    Each block is a box with 6 faces. Per-face vertex positions computed in a switch:

    switch(faceIndex) {
    case 0u: { // TOP — cone-shaped for organic look
        let ux = qv.x - 0.5;
        let uz = qv.y - 0.5;
        let tr = length(vec2f(ux, uz)) * 2.0;
        let cone = 1.0 - tr * 0.35;           // edges dip slightly
        localPos = vec3f(ux * csX, hh * max(cone, 0.0), uz * csZ);
        faceNormal = normalize(vec3f(-ux * 0.8, 1.0, -uz * 0.8)); // tilted normal
    }
    case 1u: { /* BOTTOM */ }
    case 2u: { /* FRONT  */ }
    case 3u: { /* BACK   */ }
    case 4u: { /* RIGHT  */ }
    case 5u: { /* LEFT   */ }
}

    Key insight: The top face isn't flat — it uses a cone function so tall blocks have slightly domed tops, creating a more organic terrain feel.

    2. Isometric ↔ Flat Morphing

    All geometry smoothly transitions between 3D isometric and flat 2D:

    let isoAngleY = mix(ISO_ANGLE_Y, FLAT_ANGLE_Y, progress);  // 0.5 → 0
let isoAngleX = mix(ISO_ANGLE_X, FLAT_ANGLE_X, progress);  // 0.9 → ~π/2
// Cell size morphs from 3D footprint to 2D footprint
var csX = mix(CELL_FOOTPRINT_ISO, CELL_FOOTPRINT_FLAT, progress);

    3. Grid-Based Shadow Casting (Ray March)

    Traces rays from each block toward the light source through the height grid:

    fn gridShadow(myH: f32, col: f32, row: f32) -> f32 {
    let lightDir = normalize(vec3f(0.38, 0.84, 0.36));
    var g = vec2f(lightDir.x, lightDir.z);
    g = g / length(g);

    for (var s: i32 = 1; s < 10; s = s + 1) {   // march 10 steps
        let nc = bc + i32(round(g.x * f32(s)));
        let nr = br + i32(round(g.y * f32(s)));
        let nh = heightAt(nc, nr);
        if (nh > myH + 0.06) {                    // neighbor is taller
            let t = smoothstep(myH + 0.06, myH + 0.36, nh);
            let fall = 1.0 - f32(s) * 0.05;       // shadow fades with distance
            sh *= mix(1.0, 0.78, t * 0.55 * max(fall, 0.5));
        }
    }
    return max(sh, 0.58);  // never fully black
}

    4. Valley Ambient Occlusion

    Blocks surrounded by taller neighbors get darkened:

    fn valleyOcclusion(myH: f32, col: f32, row: f32) -> f32 {
    for dc in -1..1, dr in -1..1:
        maxNeighborHeight = max(maxNeighborHeight, heightAt(bc+dc, br+dr));
    let diff = max(0.0, maxNeighborHeight - myH);
    return smoothstep(0.0, 0.5, diff);
}

    5. Per-Vertex Outputs

    The vertex shader computes and passes to fragment:

    Varying Purpose
    color Block color (green gradient by contribution level)
    shade Diffuse lighting (Lambertian)
    isBase 1.0 if empty cell (level 0)
    isTop 1.0 if top face
    heightFrac 0=bottom, 1=top of block
    topRim Distance from center of top face (for edge darkening)
    shadowCast Pre-computed shadow from taller neighbors
    peakLift Block height (for peak glow)
    rimLight Fresnel-like rim from view angle
    valleyAO Ambient occlusion in valleys
    fresnel View-dependent reflectance

    Fragment Shader Deep Dive

    The fragment shader applies 8 post-processing effects:

    // 1. Tone mapping — warm highlights on lit faces
let tone = mix(toneLo, toneHi, pow(shade, 0.78));

// 2. Top face canopy boost
lit = lit * mix(canopy, vec3f(1.0), progress);

// 3. Peak highlight — tall blocks glow
lit = lit * mix(1.0, peakShade, depthFactor * isTop);

// 4. Top rim darkening — edges of top face slightly darker
lit = lit * mix(rimDark, 1.0, progress);

// 5. Contact shadows — base of blocks darkened
let contact = mix(0.88, 1.0, smoothstep(0.0, 0.7, heightFrac));

// 6. Cast shadows from grid ray march
lit = lit * mix(castSoft, 1.0, progress);

// 7. Color temperature — warm on peaks, cool in valleys
let warmShift = vec3f(1.04, 1.01, 0.96);
let coolShift = vec3f(0.97, 0.99, 1.03);
let tempShift = mix(coolShift, warmShift, heightFrac);

// 8. Film grain — organic texture
let grain = hash(fragCoord.xy) - 0.5;
col = col + grain * 0.06;

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    Vertex Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Uniforms {
    float aspectRatio;
    float time;
    float progress;
    float dataProgress;
    float zoomScale;
};

struct VertexOut {
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>;
    float3 color;
    float shade;
    float isTop;
    float heightFrac;
    float topRim;
    float shadowCast;
    float rimLight;
    float valleyAO;
    float fresnel;
};

float heightAt(device const float* grid, int c, int r, int cols, int rows) {
    if (c < 0 || c >= cols || r < 0 || r >= rows) return 0.0;
    return grid[c * rows + r];
}

float gridShadow(float myH, float col, float row,
                 device const float* grid, int cols, int rows) {
    float3 lightDir = normalize(float3(0.38, 0.84, 0.36));
    float2 g = normalize(float2(lightDir.x, lightDir.z));
    int bc = int(floor(col + 0.5));
    int br = int(floor(row + 0.5));
    float sh = 1.0;

    for (int s = 1; s < 10; s++) {
        int nc = bc + int(round(g.x * float(s)));
        int nr = br + int(round(g.y * float(s)));
        float nh = heightAt(grid, nc, nr, cols, rows);
        if (nh > myH + 0.06) {
            float t = smoothstep(myH + 0.06, myH + 0.36, nh);
            float fall = 1.0 - float(s) * 0.05;
            sh *= mix(1.0f, 0.78f, t * 0.55 * max(fall, 0.5f));
        }
    }
    return max(sh, 0.58);
}

vertex VertexOut terrainVertex(
    uint vertexID <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">vertex_id</span>,
    uint instanceID <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">instance_id</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    device const packed_float3* positions <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>,
    device const float* heights <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(2)</span>,
    device const uint* colors <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(3)</span>,
    device const float* heightGrid <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(4)</span>
) {
    VertexOut out;

    uint faceIndex = vertexID / 6;
    uint faceVertex = vertexID % 6;
    float3 blockPos = positions[instanceID];
    float blockHeight = heights[instanceID];

    float blockSize = 0.009;
    float maxHeight = 8.6;
    float h = max(blockHeight * maxHeight, 0.026);
    float height = mix(h, 0.06, u.progress);
    float hh = height * 0.5;

    // Quad vertices (two triangles)
    float2 quadVerts[6] = {
        {0,0}, {1,0}, {0,1}, {0,1}, {1,0}, {1,1}
    };
    float2 qv = quadVerts[faceVertex];

    float csX = mix(0.009, 0.007, u.progress); // cell footprints
    float3 localPos;
    float3 faceNormal;

    // Top face with cone shape
    if (faceIndex == 0) {
        float ux = qv.x - 0.5;
        float uz = qv.y - 0.5;
        float cone = 1.0 - length(float2(ux, uz)) * 2.0 * 0.35;
        localPos = float3(ux * csX, hh * max(cone, 0.0), uz * csX);
        faceNormal = normalize(float3(-ux * 0.8, 1.0, -uz * 0.8));
    }
    // ... other faces (front, back, left, right, bottom)

    // World position
    float3 worldPos = blockPos * blockSize + localPos * blockSize;
    worldPos.y += hh * blockSize;

    // Isometric rotation
    float isoAngleY = mix(0.5, 0.0, u.progress);
    float isoAngleX = mix(0.9, 1.5, u.progress);
    float cy = cos(isoAngleY), sy = sin(isoAngleY);
    float cx = cos(isoAngleX), sx = sin(isoAngleX);

    float ry_x = worldPos.x * cy - worldPos.z * sy;
    float ry_z = worldPos.x * sy + worldPos.z * cy;
    float rx_y = worldPos.y * cx - ry_z * sx;
    float rx_z = worldPos.y * sx + ry_z * cx;

    float scale = u.zoomScale * 3.0; // viewport fitting
    out.position = float4(ry_x * scale / u.aspectRatio, rx_y * scale, rx_z * 0.014 + 0.5, 1.0);

    // Lighting
    float3 light = normalize(float3(0.38, 0.84, 0.36));
    float diffuse = max(dot(faceNormal, light), 0.0);
    out.shade = 0.26 + 0.74 * pow(diffuse, 0.62);
    out.shadowCast = gridShadow(blockHeight, blockPos.x, blockPos.z,
                                heightGrid, GRID_COLS, GRID_ROWS);
    // ... compute remaining varyings

    return out;
}

    Fragment Shader

    fragment float4 terrainFragment(VertexOut in <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stage_in</span>) {
    float3 bg = float3(0.976, 0.965, 0.945);

    // Tone mapping
    float3 toneLo = float3(0.965, 0.963, 0.958);
    float3 toneHi = float3(1.0, 0.998, 0.993);
    float3 tone = mix(toneLo, toneHi, pow(in.shade, 0.78));
    float3 lit = in.color * tone * (0.94 + 0.06 * in.shade);

    // Contact shadows at block base
    float contact = mix(0.88f, 1.0f, smoothstep(0.0f, 0.7f, in.heightFrac));
    lit *= contact;

    // Cast shadows
    lit *= mix(1.0f, in.shadowCast, 0.58f);

    // Rim light
    lit *= (1.0 + in.rimLight * 0.25);

    // Valley AO
    lit *= (1.0 - in.valleyAO * 0.32);

    // Color temperature by height
    float3 warm = float3(1.04, 1.01, 0.96);
    float3 cool = float3(0.97, 0.99, 1.03);
    lit *= mix(cool, warm, in.heightFrac) * 0.35 + 0.65;

    // Film grain
    float2 p = fract(in.position.xy * float2(0.1031, 0.103));
    p += dot(p, p.yx + 19.19);
    float grain = fract((p.x + p.y) * p.x) - 0.5;
    lit += grain * 0.06;

    return float4(lit, 1.0);
}

    Swift Integration

    This requires a full Metal render pipeline — not a SwiftUI shader modifier:

    let pipelineDescriptor = MTLRenderPipelineDescriptor()
pipelineDescriptor.vertexFunction = library.makeFunction(name: "terrainVertex")
pipelineDescriptor.fragmentFunction = library.makeFunction(name: "terrainFragment")
// Bind contribution data as buffer(1)..buffer(4)
// Animate `progress` uniform 0→1 for iso↔flat morph

    The foundation

    • Vertex shader = runs per corner point; here it extrudes each block into a 3D box and projects it to screen.
    • Fragment shader = runs per pixel; here it does all the post-processing color work (tone, shadows, grain).
    • Height grid = a lookup table of every block's height, so any block can ask "how tall are my neighbors?"
    • Grid shadow (ray march) = step from a block toward the light; if a taller block is in the way, darken — that's a cast shadow.
    • Valley ambient occlusion = blocks ringed by taller neighbors get darkened, mimicking how light can't reach a pit.
    • Isometric ↔ flat morph = a single progress value lerps angles and cell sizes to slide between 3D and 2D.
    • Varyings = per-vertex values (color, shade, rim light…) the vertex shader computes and the fragment shader receives, smoothly interpolated across each face.

    • 01-card-shader-reflections — Fragment-only PBR shader
    • 03-sphere-waves — CPU particle math
    • enzomangano-demos-reference — Full repo reference

    Sphere Waves — Fibonacci Sphere with Perspective Projection

    Start here — the plain idea

    Picture 2000 glowing dots arranged on the surface of an invisible ball, breathing in and out in slow waves while the whole thing slowly shifts color. That's the effect: a shimmering sphere made of particles, with closer dots drawn bigger so it feels genuinely 3D.

    The core trick is the Fibonacci sphere — a clever bit of math (using the golden ratio) that sprinkles points so evenly over a ball that they never clump. From there everything is layered on with simple math: three sine waves at different speeds push the dots in and out to make organic, never-repeating motion; a perspective formula shrinks far dots and grows near ones; and the hue cycles over time.

    Notice this lesson computes positions per particle, not per pixel — sometimes the CPU (or a compute shader) figures out where 2000 things go, and the GPU just draws them. Think of it as choreographing 2000 dancers rather than painting a wall.

    2000 particles distributed on a Fibonacci sphere with triple-wave displacement, perspective projection, and pinch-to-zoom. CPU-computed via Skia Path on worklet thread.

    How It Works

    The Math: Fibonacci Sphere Distribution

    The Fibonacci sphere places N points nearly uniformly on a sphere surface:

    for (let i = 0; i < N; i++) {
    const a = i / (N * 0.5) - 1.0;              // a ∈ [-1, 1] (latitude)
    const px = cos(i * magicalMul + iTime) * sqrt(1.0 - a * a);
    const py = cos(i * magicalMul + iTime + 11) * sqrt(1.0 - a * a);
    // a = vertical position, px/py = horizontal circle at that latitude
    // sqrt(1 - a²) = radius of circle at latitude a
}

    magicalMul is the golden ratio angle (~2.4 radians). Changing it creates entirely different 3D structures (tap to randomize).

    Triple-Wave Z Displacement

    Three sine waves at different frequencies create organic depth motion:

    const wave1 = sin(i * 0.1  + iTime * 0.7)  * 80;   // slow, large
const wave2 = cos(i * 0.05 + iTime * 0.3)  * 40;   // slower, medium
const wave3 = sin(i * 0.15 + iTime * 1.2)  * 20;   // fast, small
const z = wave1 + wave2 + wave3;

    Why 3 waves: Single wave = boring uniform oscillation. Triple wave creates complex, organic patterns because the frequencies are incommensurate (don't repeat at the same time).

    Perspective Projection

    Real perspective — closer particles are larger:

    const scale = distance / (distance + z);
const x = centerX + px * WIDTH * 0.4 * scale;
const y = centerY + a  * WIDTH * 0.4 * scale;

    distance is controlled by pinch gesture (default 300). Pinching in → extreme fisheye; pinching out → flattens to orthographic.

    Pulsing Radius

    Each particle pulses based on position + time:

    const pulse = sin(i * 0.2 + iTime * 2.0) * 0.3 + 1.0;
const timePulse = sin(iTime * 0.8) * 0.2 + 1.0;
const intensity = (1.0 - abs(py)) / (N * 0.01);
const baseRadius = max(0.5, min(intensity * 20 * scale, 10));
const radius = baseRadius * pulse * timePulse;

    Animated HSL Gradient

    Colors cycle through hue over time:

    const hueShift = (time * 40) % 360;
colors = [
    `hsl(${(340 + hueShift) % 360}, 90%, 70%)`,
    `hsl(${(280 + hueShift) % 360}, 85%, 75%)`,
    `hsl(${(220 + hueShift) % 360}, 95%, 80%)`,
    `hsl(${(160 + hueShift) % 360}, 88%, 72%)`,
    `hsl(${( 60 + hueShift) % 360}, 92%, 78%)`,
];

    Applied as a LinearGradient fill on the Skia Path containing all 2000 circles.

    Interactions

    Gesture Effect
    Tap right New random magicalMul → entirely new structure
    Tap left Undo (history stack)
    Pinch Adjust perspective distance (300 default, spring back on release)

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    Approach A: Compute Shader (GPU particles)

    Best performance — compute all 2000 particles on GPU:

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Particle {
    float2 position;
    float radius;
    float4 color;
};

struct Uniforms {
    float time;
    float magicalMul;
    float distance;
    float2 resolution;
    float hueShift;
};

// HSL to RGB
float3 hsl2rgb(float h, float s, float l) {
    float c = (1.0 - abs(2.0 * l - 1.0)) * s;
    float x = c * (1.0 - abs(fmod(h / 60.0, 2.0) - 1.0));
    float m = l - c * 0.5;
    float3 rgb;
    if      (h < 60)  rgb = float3(c, x, 0);
    else if (h < 120) rgb = float3(x, c, 0);
    else if (h < 180) rgb = float3(0, c, x);
    else if (h < 240) rgb = float3(0, x, c);
    else if (h < 300) rgb = float3(x, 0, c);
    else               rgb = float3(c, 0, x);
    return rgb + m;
}

kernel void fibonacciSphere(
    device Particle* particles <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>,
    uint id <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>
) {
    const uint N = 2000;
    if (id >= N) return;

    float i = float(id);
    float a = i / (float(N) * 0.5) - 1.0;
    float px = cos(i * u.magicalMul + u.time) * sqrt(1.0 - a * a);
    float py = cos(i * u.magicalMul + u.time + 11.0) * sqrt(1.0 - a * a);

    // Triple wave displacement
    float wave1 = sin(i * 0.1  + u.time * 0.7)  * 80.0;
    float wave2 = cos(i * 0.05 + u.time * 0.3)  * 40.0;
    float wave3 = sin(i * 0.15 + u.time * 1.2)  * 20.0;
    float z = wave1 + wave2 + wave3;

    // Perspective projection
    float scale = u.distance / (u.distance + z);
    float2 center = u.resolution * 0.5;
    float x = center.x + px * u.resolution.x * 0.4 * scale;
    float y = center.y + a  * u.resolution.x * 0.4 * scale;

    // Pulsing radius
    float pulse = sin(i * 0.2 + u.time * 2.0) * 0.3 + 1.0;
    float timePulse = sin(u.time * 0.8) * 0.2 + 1.0;
    float intensity = (1.0 - abs(py)) / (float(N) * 0.01);
    float baseRadius = clamp(intensity * 20.0 * scale, 0.5, 10.0);
    float radius = baseRadius * pulse * timePulse;

    // Color from position in gradient
    float t = float(id) / float(N);
    float hue = fmod(340.0 + u.hueShift + t * 280.0, 360.0);
    float3 rgb = hsl2rgb(hue, 0.9, 0.74);

    particles[id].position = float2(x, y);
    particles[id].radius = radius;
    particles[id].color = float4(rgb, 1.0);
}

    Then render particles with instanced draw or a fragment shader that reads the particle buffer.

    Approach B: SwiftUI Canvas (CPU, simpler)

    For prototyping or if particle count is manageable:

    import SwiftUI

struct FibonacciSphereView: View {
    @State private var time: Double = 0
    @State private var magicalMul: Double = 2.4
    @State private var distance: Double = 300

    let N = 2000
    let timer = Timer.publish(every: 1/60, on: .main, in: .common).autoconnect()

    var body: some View {
        Canvas { context, size in
            let cx = size.width / 2
            let cy = size.height / 2
            let w = size.width

            for i in 0..<N {
                let fi = Double(i)
                let a = fi / (Double(N) * 0.5) - 1.0
                let px = cos(fi * magicalMul + time) * sqrt(1.0 - a * a)
                let py = cos(fi * magicalMul + time + 11) * sqrt(1.0 - a * a)

                // Triple wave
                let z = sin(fi * 0.1 + time * 0.7) * 80
                      + cos(fi * 0.05 + time * 0.3) * 40
                      + sin(fi * 0.15 + time * 1.2) * 20

                // Perspective
                let scale = distance / (distance + z)
                let x = cx + px * w * 0.4 * scale
                let y = cy + a * w * 0.4 * scale

                // Radius
                let pulse = sin(fi * 0.2 + time * 2.0) * 0.3 + 1.0
                let intensity = (1.0 - abs(py)) / (Double(N) * 0.01)
                let radius = max(0.5, min(intensity * 20 * scale, 10)) * pulse

                // Color
                let hue = (340 + time * 40 + Double(i) / Double(N) * 280)
                    .truncatingRemainder(dividingBy: 360) / 360

                context.fill(
                    Path(ellipseIn: CGRect(x: x - radius, y: y - radius,
                                          width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2)),
                    with: .color(Color(hue: hue, saturation: 0.9, brightness: 0.74))
                )
            }
        }
        .background(.black)
        .onReceive(timer) { _ in time += 0.016 }
        .gesture(
            MagnifyGesture().onChanged { value in
                distance = 300 * Double(value.magnification)
            }.onEnded { _ in
                withAnimation(.spring(duration: 0.5)) { distance = 300 }
            }
        )
        .onTapGesture { magicalMul = Double.random(in: 0...100) }
    }
}

    Approach C: Metal Fragment Shader (no geometry, SDF circles)

    Render all particles in a single fragment shader using SDF circles:

    fragment float4 sphereWavesFragment(
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>
) {
    float2 fragCoord = position.xy;
    float3 col = float3(0);
    float alpha = 0;

    for (int i = 0; i < 2000; i++) {
        float fi = float(i);
        // ... same fibonacci + wave + perspective math ...
        float2 center = float2(x, y);
        float d = length(fragCoord - center) - radius;

        if (d < 0) {
            float3 particleColor = hsl2rgb(hue, 0.9, 0.74);
            float a = smoothstep(0.0, -1.5, d); // soft edge
            col = mix(col, particleColor, a);
            alpha = max(alpha, a);
        }
    }

    return float4(col, alpha);
}

    Warning: 2000 iterations per fragment is expensive. Use compute shader approach for production.

    The foundation

    • Fibonacci sphere = golden-ratio spacing that scatters N points almost perfectly evenly over a ball.
    • magicalMul = the golden-ratio angle; nudge it and the whole 3D structure changes shape.
    • Triple-wave displacement = three sine waves at different speeds added together, so motion looks organic and never repeats.
    • Perspective projection = scale = distance / (distance + z); near dots get bigger, far dots shrink.
    • Pulsing radius = each dot's size wobbles via its own sine of position and time, for a breathing feel.
    • Per-particle vs per-pixel = here you compute 2000 positions (on CPU or compute shader) and then draw them, instead of a rule per pixel.
    • Compute shader (kernel) = the GPU path that crunches all 2000 particles in parallel into a buffer the renderer reads.

    Metaball — Blur + Color Matrix Threshold Trick

    Start here — the plain idea

    Metaballs are those blobs that behave like droplets of mercury — drag two near each other and they bulge, stretch, and merge into one gooey shape, then snap apart. It looks like fluid physics, but it's a cheap illusion.

    The trick is almost silly: draw two plain circles, blur them into soft fuzzy clouds, then crank the contrast so hard that everything fuzzy becomes either fully solid or fully gone. Where two blurred edges overlap, their faintness adds up and crosses the "solid" line — so a smooth bridge appears between them. Pull them apart and the overlap fades below the line, and they separate. Blur + a hard threshold = organic merge, no physics required.

    The "proper" version skips the blur and uses a metaball field — each pixel sums up radius² / distance² from every blob and lights up where the total crosses a threshold. Same idea, exact control. Think of each blob as a heat source; you paint solid wherever it's hot enough.

    Two draggable blobs that merge organically using zero shader code — just Gaussian blur + alpha channel threshold via color matrix. The simplest way to get liquid/metaball effects.

    How It Works

    The Trick

    The entire effect is just two compositing operations:

    1. Draw two overlapping circles
2. Apply heavy Gaussian blur (radius 30)
3. Apply color matrix that cranks alpha contrast

    When two blurred circles overlap, their alpha values add up. The color matrix then thresholds the alpha — anything above a certain value becomes opaque, everything below becomes transparent. This creates the organic "merging blob" shape.

    The Color Matrix

    <ColorMatrix matrix={[
    1, 0, 0, 0, 0,     // R: pass through
    0, 1, 0, 0, 0,     // G: pass through
    0, 0, 1, 0, 0,     // B: pass through
    0, 0, 0, 60, -30,  // A: output = input * 60 - 30
]} />

    The alpha row is the key: alpha_out = alpha_in * 60 - 30

    Input alpha Output alpha Result
    0.0 -30 → 0 Fully transparent
    0.3 -12 → 0 Still transparent
    0.5 0 → 0 Threshold edge
    0.51 0.6 → clamped Starts appearing
    0.6 6 → 1 Fully opaque
    1.0 30 → 1 Fully opaque

    The steep ramp (multiply by 60) creates a sharp cutoff around alpha ~0.5. Where two blurred circles overlap, their alphas sum past this threshold, creating the smooth merge.

    Geometry + Gesture

    // Two circles, both paths combined into one
const path = useDerivedValue(() => {
    const circles = Skia.Path.Make();
    circles.addCircle(firstCx.value, firstCy.value, RADIUS);   // 80px
    circles.addCircle(secondCx.value, secondCy.value, RADIUS);
    circles.simplify();  // union the paths
    return circles;
});

// First circle: drag freely
// Second circle: drag, then spring back to center on release
onEnd: () => {
    secondCx.value = withSpring(width / 2);
    secondCy.value = withSpring(height / 2);
}

    Color

    Sweep gradient from the origin creates a cyan→blue→cyan color band:

    <SweepGradient c={vec(0, 0)} colors={['cyan', 'blue', 'cyan']} />

    Compositing Stack

    Canvas
  └─ Group (layer = Paint with Blur + ColorMatrix)
       └─ Path (two circles, simplified/unioned)
            └─ SweepGradient fill
  └─ Touchable.Circle (invisible, for gesture hit testing)
  └─ Touchable.Circle (invisible, for gesture hit testing)

    The Group with a layer prop means: render children into an offscreen buffer, apply the Paint effects (blur + color matrix), then composite back.

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    Approach A: CIFilter Chain (Simplest — no Metal code)

    The most direct translation uses Core Image, which is what this technique maps to:

    import CoreImage

func metaballEffect(circles: [(CGPoint, CGFloat)], size: CGSize) -> CIImage {
    let ctx = CIContext()

    // 1. Draw circles into a CIImage
    var combined = CIImage.empty()
    for (center, radius) in circles {
        let circle = CIFilter.radialGradient()!
        circle.center = center
        circle.radius0 = Float(radius * 0.8)
        circle.radius1 = Float(radius)
        circle.color0 = CIColor(red: 0, green: 1, blue: 1, alpha: 1)
        circle.color1 = CIColor(red: 0, green: 1, blue: 1, alpha: 0)

        if let output = circle.outputImage {
            combined = output.composited(over: combined)
        }
    }

    // 2. Gaussian blur
    let blur = CIFilter.gaussianBlur()!
    blur.inputImage = combined
    blur.radius = 30

    // 3. Alpha threshold via color matrix
    let matrix = CIFilter.colorMatrix()!
    matrix.inputImage = blur.outputImage
    matrix.rVector = CIVector(x: 1, y: 0, z: 0, w: 0)
    matrix.gVector = CIVector(x: 0, y: 1, z: 0, w: 0)
    matrix.bVector = CIVector(x: 0, y: 0, z: 1, w: 0)
    matrix.aVector = CIVector(x: 0, y: 0, z: 0, w: 60) // alpha *= 60
    matrix.biasVector = CIVector(x: 0, y: 0, z: 0, w: -30) // alpha -= 30

    return matrix.outputImage!
}

    Approach B: SwiftUI Canvas + Blur (Quick & Dirty)

    struct MetaballView: View {
    @State private var pos1 = CGPoint(x: 200, y: 400)
    @State private var pos2 = CGPoint(x: 200, y: 400)
    let radius: CGFloat = 80

    var body: some View {
        Canvas { context, size in
            // Draw circle 1
            var path = Path()
            path.addEllipse(in: CGRect(
                x: pos1.x - radius, y: pos1.y - radius,
                width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2))
            // Draw circle 2
            path.addEllipse(in: CGRect(
                x: pos2.x - radius, y: pos2.y - radius,
                width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2))

            context.fill(path, with: .color(.cyan))
        }
        .blur(radius: 30)                    // Step 2: heavy blur
        .contrast(20)                         // Step 3: threshold!
        .saturation(2)                        // Boost color
        .gesture(DragGesture().onChanged { pos1 = $0.location })
    }
}

    The .contrast() trick: In SwiftUI, high contrast on a blurred shape achieves the same alpha thresholding as the color matrix. contrast(20) ≈ the 60/-30 color matrix.

    Approach C: Metal Fragment Shader (Full control)

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Uniforms {
    float2 resolution;
    float2 circle1;   // center of circle 1
    float2 circle2;   // center of circle 2
    float radius;
};

fragment float4 metaballFragment(
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>
) {
    float2 uv = position.xy;

    // Soft circle SDF (smooth falloff instead of hard edge)
    float d1 = length(uv - u.circle1);
    float d2 = length(uv - u.circle2);

    // Metaball field: sum of inverse-square falloffs
    float field = (u.radius * u.radius) / (d1 * d1 + 1.0)
                + (u.radius * u.radius) / (d2 * d2 + 1.0);

    // Threshold
    float threshold = 1.0;
    float edge = smoothstep(threshold - 0.1, threshold + 0.1, field);

    // Color: sweep gradient based on angle from origin
    float angle = atan2(uv.y, uv.x);
    float t = angle / (2.0 * M_PI_F) + 0.5;
    float3 col = mix(float3(0, 1, 1), float3(0, 0, 1),
                     sin(t * M_PI_F * 2.0) * 0.5 + 0.5);

    return float4(col * edge, edge);
}

    This is the "proper" metaball: Instead of blur+threshold hack, use the actual metaball field equation (sum of r²/d²). Gives you exact control over the merge radius and falloff.

    SwiftUI Integration

    // For Approach B (simplest):
MetaballView()

// For Approach C (Metal):
TimelineView(.animation) { _ in
    MetalMetaballView(circle1: pos1, circle2: pos2)
        .gesture(DragGesture().onChanged { pos1 = $0.location })
}

    When to Use Which Approach

    Approach Pros Cons
    CIFilter chain Most direct translation, flexible Needs CIContext, not real-time friendly
    SwiftUI blur+contrast 3 lines of code, works now Less control, visual artifacts at edges
    Metal SDF Exact control, GPU-accelerated, scalable to N blobs More code, needs MTKView

    The foundation

    • Metaball = blobs that merge and split like liquid droplets, faked without any real physics.
    • Blur + threshold trick = blur two circles into soft clouds, then a hard cutoff turns overlap into a smooth bridge.
    • Color matrix (alpha row) = alpha_out = alpha_in * 60 - 30, a steep ramp that snaps fuzzy edges to solid-or-gone around ~0.5.
    • .contrast() shortcut = in SwiftUI, high contrast on a blurred shape does the same thresholding in one line.
    • Metaball field = the "proper" version: each pixel sums radius² / distance² from every blob and lights up past a threshold.
    • Offscreen layer = render the blobs into a separate buffer first, apply blur + threshold, then composite back.

    Fractal Glass — Masked Blur Refraction

    Start here — the plain idea

    Imagine a bright circle sitting behind a panel of textured bathroom glass. Where the glass covers it, the circle goes soft and frosted; outside the glass it stays crisp. Drag the circle around and it sharpens and blurs as it passes the edge of the panel.

    The whole effect is one neat pattern: draw the same thing twice. One copy is sharp, clipped to show only outside the glass. The other copy is blurred, clipped to show only inside the glass. Lay them together and you get a window of frostiness over a sharp world.

    The "fractal" part is just the glass texture — a grid of little gradient tiles that vary the blur's opacity across the panel, so it looks like real uneven, hand-poured glass instead of a flat smudge. Think of it as a stencil that decides how much frost shows where.

    A draggable colored circle viewed through a frosted glass panel. The glass region shows a blurred version of the circle while outside the glass the circle is sharp. A 4x4 grid of gradient tiles creates the irregular "fractal" glass pattern.

    How It Works

    Architecture

    Canvas (full screen)
  ├─ Group (clip = glass rect, INVERTED)
  │    └─ Circle (sharp, outside glass)
  │
  └─ Group (clip = glass rect)
       └─ Mask (mode = alpha)
            ├─ mask: FractalGlassMask (4×4 gradient grid)
            └─ child: Circle + Blur(25)

    Two copies of the circle: 1. Outside glass: Clipped with invertClip — renders everywhere EXCEPT the glass rect 2. Inside glass: Clipped TO the glass rect, blurred, alpha-masked by gradient grid

    The Fractal Glass Mask

    A 4×4 grid of rectangles, each filled with a LinearGradient from white → 45% black:

    // Creates 16 gradient tiles (4 cols × 4 rows)
for (col = 0; col < 4; col++) {
    for (row = 0; row < 4; row++) {
        const tileX = glassX + tileWidth * col;
        const tileY = glassY + tileHeight * row;
        <Rect x={tileX} y={tileY} width={tileWidth} height={tileHeight}>
            <LinearGradient
                colors={['white', 'rgba(0,0,0,0.45)']}
                start={vec(tileX, tileY)}
                end={vec(tileX + tileWidth - 5, tileY + tileHeight - 5)}
            />
        </Rect>
    }
}

    This creates varying alpha across the glass — some regions are fully transparent (white = alpha 1 in mask), some are partially (0.45 alpha). This makes the glass look non-uniform, like real textured glass.

    Circle + Blur Inside Glass

    <Mask mode="alpha" mask={<FractalGlassMask ... />}>
    <Circle cx={cx} cy={cy} r={100} color={circleColor}
            clip={rect(x, y, width, height)}>
        <Blur blur={25} mode={'decal'} />
    </Circle>
</Mask>

    The decal blur mode prevents the blur from bleeding outside the clip region.

    Interaction

    • Pan gesture: Drags the circle position
    • Theme toggle: Switches circle color (orange ↔ blue) and background (white ↔ black) with withTiming transitions

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    Approach A: SwiftUI + Material (Simplest)

    struct FractalGlassView: View {
    @State private var circlePos = CGPoint(x: 180, y: 270)
    @State private var isDark = false

    let glassFrame = CGRect(x: 40, y: 250, width: 310, height: 300)

    var body: some View {
        ZStack {
            // Background
            Color(isDark ? .black : .white)

            // Circle OUTSIDE glass (sharp)
            Circle()
                .fill(isDark ? Color.cyan : Color.orange)
                .frame(width: 200, height: 200)
                .position(circlePos)
                .mask {
                    Rectangle().fill(.white)
                        .overlay {
                            RoundedRectangle(cornerRadius: 0)
                                .frame(width: glassFrame.width, height: glassFrame.height)
                                .position(x: glassFrame.midX, y: glassFrame.midY)
                                .blendMode(.destinationOut)
                        }
                        .compositingGroup()
                }

            // Glass panel with blurred circle inside
            Circle()
                .fill(isDark ? Color.cyan : Color.orange)
                .frame(width: 200, height: 200)
                .position(circlePos)
                .blur(radius: 25)
                .mask { fractalGlassMask }
                .clipShape(Rectangle()
                    .offset(x: glassFrame.minX, y: glassFrame.minY)
                    .size(width: glassFrame.width, height: glassFrame.height))
        }
        .gesture(DragGesture().onChanged { circlePos = $0.location })
    }

    var fractalGlassMask: some View {
        Canvas { context, size in
            let cols = 4, rows = 4
            let tileW = glassFrame.width / CGFloat(cols)
            let tileH = glassFrame.height / CGFloat(rows)

            for col in 0..<cols {
                for row in 0..<rows {
                    let x = glassFrame.minX + tileW * CGFloat(col)
                    let y = glassFrame.minY + tileH * CGFloat(row)
                    let rect = CGRect(x: x, y: y, width: tileW, height: tileH)

                    context.fill(Path(rect),
                        with: .linearGradient(
                            Gradient(colors: [.white, .white.opacity(0.55)]),
                            startPoint: CGPoint(x: x, y: y),
                            endPoint: CGPoint(x: x + tileW - 5, y: y + tileH - 5)))
                }
            }
        }
    }
}

    Approach B: Metal Fragment Shader

    For full GPU control over the glass effect:

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Uniforms {
    float2 resolution;
    float2 circleCenter;
    float circleRadius;
    float4 glassRect;     // x, y, width, height
    float3 circleColor;
    int tilesPerAxis;     // 4
};

// Fractal glass mask: 4×4 grid of gradients
float glassMask(float2 uv, float4 rect, int tiles) {
    // Check if inside glass rect
    float2 localUV = (uv - rect.xy) / rect.zw;
    if (localUV.x < 0 || localUV.x > 1 || localUV.y < 0 || localUV.y > 1)
        return 0.0;

    // Which tile?
    float2 tileUV = fract(localUV * float(tiles));

    // Diagonal gradient per tile: white (1.0) → 0.55
    float gradient = mix(1.0, 0.55, (tileUV.x + tileUV.y) * 0.5);
    return gradient;
}

// Soft circle SDF
float circleSDF(float2 uv, float2 center, float radius) {
    return length(uv - center) - radius;
}

// Box SDF for glass rect
float boxSDF(float2 uv, float4 rect) {
    float2 center = rect.xy + rect.zw * 0.5;
    float2 halfSize = rect.zw * 0.5;
    float2 d = abs(uv - center) - halfSize;
    return length(max(d, 0.0));
}

fragment float4 fractalGlassFragment(
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    texture2d<float> blurredTex <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>  // pre-blurred circle
) {
    float2 uv = position.xy;
    float2 normUV = uv / u.resolution;

    float circleDist = circleSDF(uv, u.circleCenter, u.circleRadius);
    bool insideGlass = (uv.x >= u.glassRect.x &&
                        uv.x <= u.glassRect.x + u.glassRect.z &&
                        uv.y >= u.glassRect.y &&
                        uv.y <= u.glassRect.y + u.glassRect.w);

    float3 color;

    if (insideGlass) {
        // Sample blurred texture
        constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);
        float3 blurred = blurredTex.sample(s, normUV).rgb;

        // Apply fractal glass mask
        float mask = glassMask(uv, u.glassRect, u.tilesPerAxis);
        color = blurred * mask;
    } else {
        // Sharp circle outside glass
        float edge = smoothstep(1.0, -1.0, circleDist);
        color = u.circleColor * edge;
    }

    return float4(color, 1.0);
}

    Note: The blur requires a two-pass approach — render the circle to a texture, blur it (Gaussian blur shader or MPSImageGaussianBlur), then sample the blurred texture in the main pass.

    Approach C: CIFilter (Production-ready)

    func fractalGlassEffect(
    circleImage: CIImage,
    glassRect: CGRect,
    tiles: Int = 4
) -> CIImage {
    // 1. Blur the circle image
    let blurred = circleImage.applyingFilter("CIGaussianBlur", parameters: [
        kCIInputRadiusKey: 25
    ])

    // 2. Create fractal mask
    let maskImage = createFractalMask(rect: glassRect, tiles: tiles)

    // 3. Blend: blurred inside mask, sharp outside
    let result = circleImage.applyingFilter("CIBlendWithMask", parameters: [
        kCIInputBackgroundImageKey: blurred,
        kCIInputMaskImageKey: maskImage
    ])

    return result
}

    The Key Insight

    Draw the same content twice — once sharp (clipped outside), once blurred (clipped inside). This pattern works for any refraction/frosted glass effect. The mask controls the glass "texture."

    The foundation

    • Draw-twice pattern = render the content sharp (clipped outside the glass) and blurred (clipped inside) — the core trick for any frosted/refraction effect.
    • Clip / invert clip = restrict drawing to a region, or to everything except that region.
    • Fractal glass mask = a 4×4 grid of gradient tiles that varies opacity across the panel so the glass looks uneven and real.
    • Alpha mask = a grayscale image that controls how much of the blurred layer shows through, pixel by pixel.
    • decal blur mode = keeps the blur from bleeding past the clip edge.
    • Two-pass blur = render the circle to a texture, blur that texture, then sample it — how the GPU version gets its frost.

    • 04-metaball — Another blur-based technique
    • 06-blur-circles — Noise-driven motion with clip masking
    • enzomangano-demos-reference — Full repo reference

    Source: enzomanuelmangano/demos/fractal-glass

    Blur Circles — Noise-Driven Organic Motion

    Start here — the plain idea

    Two colorful circles drift around the screen like they're floating in water, and where one passes over the other you see a soft, blurry lens-like patch. Calm, hypnotic, never quite repeating.

    Two tricks are stacked here. First, the motion: instead of random jitter (too twitchy) or sine waves (too repetitive), each circle's position is steered by simplex noise — smooth, wandering numbers that feel organic, like a leaf on a stream. Feed time into the noise and the circles meander naturally forever. Second, the lens look: draw circle one sharp everywhere, then draw a blurred copy of it again but clipped to only show inside circle two. So circle two becomes a moving window that frosts whatever it covers.

    Think of noise as a "natural-looking randomness" dial, and the clip as a roaming magnifying glass that softens instead of magnifies.

    Two circles moving on simplex noise paths — one sharp with a sweep gradient, one blurred — clipped against each other to create a lens/distortion illusion.

    How It Works

    Noise-Driven Position

    Each circle's position is driven by 2D simplex noise, not random:

    // Constants
const FREQUENCY = 1800;   // noise scale (higher = slower movement)
const A = 30;             // amplitude in pixels
const RADIUS = 80;

// Circle 1: normal speed
const x = noise2D(clock / frequency, 0);
const y = noise2D(0, clock / frequency);
cx = amplitude * x + center.x;
cy = amplitude * y + center.y;

// Circle 2: double frequency, triple amplitude
const x2 = secondNoise2D(clock / (frequency * 2), 0);
const y2 = secondNoise2D(0, clock / (frequency * 2));
cx2 = (amplitude * 3) * x2 + center.x;
cy2 = (amplitude * 3) * y2 + center.y;

    Why simplex noise: Random motion is jittery. Sine waves are repetitive. Simplex noise creates smooth, organic, non-repeating paths — like floating in water.

    Two different noise generators (createNoise2D() called twice) produce independent, uncorrelated paths.

    Compositing Stack

    Canvas
  ├─ Circle 1 (sharp, sweep gradient fill, blur mask on edge)
  │    └─ SweepGradient(cyan → magenta → yellow → cyan)
  │    └─ BlurMask(blur: 10, style: normal)
  │
  ├─ Group (clip = Circle 2 path, rounded rect)
  │    └─ Circle 1 AGAIN (clipped to Circle 2's shape)
  │         └─ Same SweepGradient
  │         └─ Blur(blur: 10)
  │
  └─ Circle 2 (blurred outline, acts as clip shape)

    The illusion: Where Circle 2 overlaps Circle 1, you see a blurred version of Circle 1's gradient. Outside the overlap, Circle 1 is sharp. This creates a lens/magnifying glass effect.

    Animation Loop

    clock.value = withRepeat(
    withTiming(20000, { duration: 20000 }),  // 20 seconds to reach 20000
    -1,    // infinite repeat
    true,  // reverse (ping-pong)
);

    The clock ping-pongs between 0 and 20000, which feeds into the noise function. Since noise is continuous, the motion smoothly reverses.

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    Approach A: SwiftUI Canvas + Simplex Noise

    import SwiftUI

// Simplex noise (simplified 2D)
struct SimplexNoise {
    private let perm: [Int]

    init(seed: UInt64 = 0) {
        var p = Array(0..<256)
        var rng = RandomNumberGenerator(seed: seed)
        p.shuffle(using: &rng)
        perm = p + p // double for wrapping
    }

    func noise(_ x: Double, _ y: Double) -> Double {
        // Standard 2D simplex implementation
        // ... (use a Swift simplex noise library like GameplayKit's GKNoise)
        let skew = (x + y) * 0.3660254  // (sqrt(3)-1)/2
        let i = Int(floor(x + skew))
        let j = Int(floor(y + skew))
        // ... full simplex implementation
        return value // -1 to 1
    }
}

struct BlurCirclesView: View {
    @State private var time: Double = 0
    let noise1 = SimplexNoise(seed: 42)
    let noise2 = SimplexNoise(seed: 99)
    let timer = Timer.publish(every: 1/60, on: .main, in: .common).autoconnect()

    let radius: CGFloat = 80
    let frequency: Double = 1800
    let amplitude: CGFloat = 30

    var body: some View {
        let cx1 = UIScreen.main.bounds.width / 2
            + amplitude * noise1.noise(time / frequency, 0)
        let cy1 = UIScreen.main.bounds.height / 2
            + amplitude * noise1.noise(0, time / frequency)
        let cx2 = UIScreen.main.bounds.width / 2
            + (amplitude * 3) * noise2.noise(time / (frequency * 2), 0)
        let cy2 = UIScreen.main.bounds.height / 2
            + (amplitude * 3) * noise2.noise(0, time / (frequency * 2))

        ZStack {
            // Sharp circle with angular gradient
            Circle()
                .fill(
                    AngularGradient(colors: [.cyan, .purple, .yellow, .cyan],
                                    center: .center)
                )
                .frame(width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2)
                .position(x: cx1, y: cy1)

            // Blurred version, clipped to circle 2's shape
            Circle()
                .fill(
                    AngularGradient(colors: [.cyan, .purple, .yellow, .cyan],
                                    center: .center)
                )
                .frame(width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2)
                .position(x: cx1, y: cy1)
                .blur(radius: 10)
                .clipShape(Circle()
                    .offset(x: cx2 - UIScreen.main.bounds.width / 2,
                            y: cy2 - UIScreen.main.bounds.height / 2)
                    .size(width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2))
        }
        .background(.white)
        .onReceive(timer) { _ in time += 16 } // ~16ms per frame
    }
}

    Approach B: Metal Fragment Shader

    Render the entire effect in a single fragment pass:

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Uniforms {
    float2 resolution;
    float2 circle1;     // noise-driven center
    float2 circle2;     // noise-driven center
    float radius;
    float time;
};

// Simple value noise for the sweep gradient
float3 sweepGradient(float2 uv, float2 center) {
    float angle = atan2(uv.y - center.y, uv.x - center.x);
    float t = angle / (2.0 * M_PI_F) + 0.5;  // 0→1

    // cyan → magenta → yellow → cyan
    float3 cyan    = float3(0, 1, 1);
    float3 magenta = float3(1, 0, 1);
    float3 yellow  = float3(1, 1, 0);

    if (t < 0.33) return mix(cyan, magenta, t / 0.33);
    if (t < 0.66) return mix(magenta, yellow, (t - 0.33) / 0.33);
    return mix(yellow, cyan, (t - 0.66) / 0.34);
}

fragment float4 blurCirclesFragment(
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    texture2d<float> blurredCircle1 <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">texture(0)</span>  // pre-blurred
) {
    float2 uv = position.xy;

    float d1 = length(uv - u.circle1) - u.radius;
    float d2 = length(uv - u.circle2) - u.radius;

    // Sharp circle 1
    float alpha1 = smoothstep(1.0, -1.0, d1);
    float3 sharpColor = sweepGradient(uv, u.circle1) * alpha1;

    // Blurred circle 1, visible only inside circle 2
    bool insideCircle2 = d2 < 0;
    float3 finalColor = sharpColor;

    if (insideCircle2) {
        constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);
        float2 texUV = uv / u.resolution;
        float3 blurred = blurredCircle1.sample(s, texUV).rgb;
        float blurAlpha = smoothstep(1.0, -1.0, d2);
        finalColor = mix(sharpColor, blurred, blurAlpha);
    }

    return float4(finalColor, 1.0);
}

    GameplayKit Noise (Built-in)

    For production, use Apple's built-in noise:

    import GameplayKit

let noiseSource = GKPerlinNoiseSource(
    frequency: 1.0 / 1800.0,
    octaveCount: 4,
    persistence: 0.5,
    lacunarity: 2.0,
    seed: 42
)
let noise = GKNoise(noiseSource)

// Sample at time-based position
let x = noise.value(atPosition: vector_float2(Float(time), 0))
let y = noise.value(atPosition: vector_float2(0, Float(time)))

    Key Takeaway

    Simplex noise + clip masking = organic lens effects. The same technique works for: - Floating particles - Ambient background animations - Liquid/water surface simulation - Organic UI transitions

    The foundation

    • Simplex noise = smooth, wandering randomness; better than jittery random or repetitive sin for organic motion.
    • Noise-driven position = feed time into noise to steer each circle, so it drifts naturally and never quite repeats.
    • Two noise generators = two independent seeds give two uncorrelated paths so the circles don't move in sync.
    • Frequency / amplitude = how slow the drift is and how far it ranges from center.
    • Clip masking = draw a blurred copy of circle one but only show it inside circle two — a moving frosted window.
    • Ping-pong clock = a timer that runs forward then reverses; because noise is continuous, motion reverses smoothly.
    • Sweep gradient = color that cycles by angle around the circle's center, giving the rainbow rim.

    Spiral — Logarithmic Spiral with Spring Physics

    Start here — the plain idea

    Picture about 1800 dots swirling out from the center in a galaxy-like spiral, small and sharp near the middle, bigger and softer toward the edges. Tap and the whole spiral winds or unwinds with a springy settle.

    The heart of it is one famous formula: the logarithmic spiral, r = a · e^(k·θ) — the same shape as a nautilus shell or a swirling galaxy. Each dot gets a number; that number sets how far out it sits and what angle it lands at. The clever bit is that a single shared angle value feeds every dot's position, so nudging that one number re-choreographs all 1800 dots at once — that's why one tap morphs the entire pattern smoothly.

    Depth is faked with two cheap tricks: dots farther out are drawn larger, and a blur softens the edges, so your eye reads it as a 3D swirl receding into space. Think of it as one dial (angle) wired to a thousand dancers.

    ~1800 circles arranged on a logarithmic spiral that responds to touch with spring physics. Sweep gradient + blur mask for depth.

    How It Works

    The Math: Logarithmic Spiral

    const logarithmicSpiral = ({ angle, index }) => {
    const a = index / 4;           // distance from center grows with index
    const k = 0.005;               // spiral tightness
    return {
        x: a * Math.exp(k * angle) * Math.cos(angle * index),
        y: a * Math.exp(k * angle) * Math.sin(angle * index),
    };
};

    The classic logarithmic (equiangular) spiral: r = a * e^(kθ)

    • a = index / 4 → each successive circle is farther from center
    • k = 0.005 → very gentle exponential growth
    • angle * index → each circle is at a different angular position, creating the spiral pattern
    • exp(k * angle) → overall scale of the spiral changes with the angle parameter

    Key insight: The angle shared value controls the entire spiral shape. Changing it smoothly morphs the pattern because cos(angle * index) and sin(angle * index) redistribute all points simultaneously.

    Particle Count

    const spiralCircleCount = Math.floor(windowHeight * 1.8);
// On iPhone 15 Pro: ~1800 circles

    Spring Animation

    const TimingConfig = { duration: 3000, dampingRatio: 1 }; // critically damped

// On tap/interaction:
angle.value = withSpring(newAngle, TimingConfig);

    Each of the ~1800 circle coordinates is a makeMutable shared value that updates via useAnimatedReaction when the angle changes. This means all circles animate simultaneously with the spring.

    Rendering

    Canvas
  └─ Path (all circles combined)
       ├─ SweepGradient (color)
       └─ BlurMask (soft edges for depth)

    All circles are added to a single Skia Path and filled with a sweep gradient. The BlurMask softens edges for a depth-of-field effect.

    Distance-Based Sizing

    Circles farther from center are larger (they have higher index). Combined with the blur mask, this creates a sense of depth — center circles are small and sharp, outer circles are larger and softer.

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    Approach A: SwiftUI Canvas (Direct)

    import SwiftUI

struct SpiralView: View {
    @State private var angle: Double = .pi / 2
    let circleCount = 1800
    let k = 0.005

    var body: some View {
        Canvas { context, size in
            let cx = size.width / 2
            let cy = size.height / 2
            let maxDist = sqrt(cx * cx + cy * cy)

            for i in 0..<circleCount {
                let a = Double(i) / 4.0
                let r = a * exp(k * angle)
                let x = cx + r * cos(angle * Double(i))
                let y = cy + r * sin(angle * Double(i))

                // Distance-based radius (larger at edges)
                let dist = sqrt((x - cx) * (x - cx) + (y - cy) * (y - cy))
                let radius = max(1.0, min(dist / maxDist * 6, 8))

                // Color from sweep gradient
                let hue = atan2(y - cy, x - cx) / (2 * .pi) + 0.5

                context.fill(
                    Path(ellipseIn: CGRect(x: x - radius, y: y - radius,
                                          width: radius * 2, height: radius * 2)),
                    with: .color(Color(hue: hue, saturation: 0.8, brightness: 0.9))
                )
            }
        }
        .background(.black)
        .onTapGesture {
            withAnimation(.spring(duration: 3, bounce: 0)) {
                angle += .pi / 4
            }
        }
    }
}

    Approach B: Metal Compute Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Particle {
    float2 position;
    float radius;
};

struct Uniforms {
    float angle;
    float2 center;
    float k;          // spiral tightness (0.005)
    uint count;
};

kernel void logarithmicSpiral(
    device Particle* particles <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(1)</span>,
    uint id <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">thread_position_in_grid</span>
) {
    if (id >= u.count) return;

    float i = float(id);
    float a = i / 4.0;
    float r = a * exp(u.k * u.angle);

    float x = u.center.x + r * cos(u.angle * i);
    float y = u.center.y + r * sin(u.angle * i);

    // Distance-based radius
    float dist = length(float2(x, y) - u.center);
    float maxDist = length(u.center);
    float radius = clamp(dist / maxDist * 6.0, 1.0, 8.0);

    particles[id].position = float2(x, y);
    particles[id].radius = radius;
}

    Then render particles with instanced drawing or a fragment shader SDF approach.

    Approach C: Metal Fragment Shader (SDF)

    fragment float4 spiralFragment(
    float4 position <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">position</span>,
    constant Uniforms &u <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">buffer(0)</span>
) {
    float2 uv = position.xy;
    float3 col = float3(0);

    for (uint i = 0; i < u.count; i++) {
        float fi = float(i);
        float a = fi / 4.0;
        float r = a * exp(u.k * u.angle);
        float2 center = u.center + float2(
            r * cos(u.angle * fi),
            r * sin(u.angle * fi)
        );

        float dist = length(uv - center);
        float maxDist = length(u.center);
        float particleR = clamp(length(center - u.center) / maxDist * 6.0, 1.0, 8.0);

        float sdf = dist - particleR;
        if (sdf < 1.0) {
            float alpha = smoothstep(1.0, -0.5, sdf);
            // Sweep gradient color
            float hue = atan2(center.y - u.center.y, center.x - u.center.x)
                       / (2.0 * M_PI_F) + 0.5;
            float3 particleCol = hsl2rgb(hue * 360.0, 0.8, 0.9);
            col = mix(col, particleCol, alpha);
        }
    }

    return float4(col, 1.0);
}

    Performance note: 1800 particles per fragment is very heavy. Use compute shader + instanced rendering for production. The fragment SDF approach is only viable for <200 particles.

    Variations

    The logarithmic spiral formula is easy to modify:

    Change Effect
    Increase k Spiral expands faster
    cos(angle * i * 2) Double spiral (two arms)
    Add sin(time + i * 0.1) to radius Breathing/pulsing
    Replace exp(k*angle) with angle Archimedean spiral (uniform spacing)
    Add z = sin(i * 0.05 + time) + perspective 3D spiral

    The foundation

    • Logarithmic spiral = r = a · e^(k·θ), the nautilus/galaxy curve where each turn grows by a constant factor.
    • k (tightness) = how fast the spiral expands; bigger k = looser, more open arms.
    • Shared angle = one value feeds every dot's position, so changing it re-choreographs all 1800 at once.
    • Spring physics = the angle animates with a critically damped spring, so taps settle smoothly without overshoot.
    • Distance-based sizing = dots farther from center are drawn larger, faking depth.
    • Blur mask = softens dot edges for a depth-of-field feel — sharp center, soft rim.
    • Compute vs fragment = with ~1800 dots, compute the positions once (compute shader) rather than looping all dots per pixel.

    Related

    • 03-sphere-waves — Similar particle math (Fibonacci vs logarithmic)
    • 06-blur-circles — Noise-driven organic motion
    • enzomangano-demos-reference — Full repo reference

    Source: enzomanuelmangano/demos/spiral

    Prev← Blur Circles — Noise-Driven Organic MotionNextBottom Ambient Light — SwiftUI `layerEffect` Glow →

    Bottom Ambient Light — SwiftUI layerEffect Glow

    Start here — the plain idea

    You know that soft glow that creeps up from the bottom of the screen when a notification slides in, or behind media controls? That's the effect here. There's no glow image and no particle engine — it's all faked with math on a plain white rectangle.

    Think of it like an artist who never paints a lamp, only paints where the lamp's light lands. The shader runs once per pixel and asks "how close am I to the bottom edge, and how much light should pile up here?" Near the bottom it answers "a lot," and the answer fades the higher you go. Stack a few of those fading glows together, jiggle them with some slow waves so they breathe, sprinkle in a few drifting specks, and your eye reads the whole thing as warm ambient light.

    The reason it looks alive instead of like a flat gradient is the "decay" — light falls off sharply and smoothly, exactly like real light in a dark room. Everything else is just making that falloff wobble in believable ways.

    A soft, animated ambient light rising from the bottom edge of the screen — built as a SwiftUI layerEffect powered by a single <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> Metal shader. No image assets: every wisp, mote, and bloom is generated procedurally from math. Great for notification reveals, media surfaces, or controller/game UI underglow.

    How It Works

    Architecture

    The whole effect is one shader applied to a plain white rectangle. SwiftUI's job is tiny — drive time and a reveal value into the shader; Metal does all the shape, noise, and motion. This is the Metal-in-SwiftUI layerEffect path, and exactly the "creative pipeline" idea from WWDC26: Compose Advanced Graphics Effects.

    struct BottomAmbientLightView: View {
    var tint: Color = .cyan
    var progress: CGFloat = 1

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { timeline in            // drives time, every frame
            GeometryReader { geometry in
                Rectangle()
                    .fill(.white)                          // the "source layer" = a mask
                    .layerEffect(
                        ShaderLibrary.bottomAmbientLighting(
                            .float2(Float(geometry.size.width), Float(geometry.size.height)),
                            .float(timeline.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
                                .truncatingRemainder(dividingBy: 100_000)),   // wrapped time
                            .float(Float(progress)),
                            .color(tint)
                        ),
                        maxSampleOffset: .zero            // shader only reads its own pixel
                    )
            }
        }
    }
}

    Three things worth noticing:

    • The white Rectangle is a stand-in mask. The shader reads layer.sample(position).a to confine the glow to the view's bounds; the rectangle's color is ignored. (compositing)
    • TimelineView(.animation) is the time source. Shaders are stateless, so animation has to be fed in as a uniform — the same pattern as animation in shaders.
    • Wrapped time (truncatingRemainder(dividingBy: 100_000)) keeps the float small so sin/fract don't lose precision after the app has run for hours.
    • maxSampleOffset: .zero tells SwiftUI the shader never samples neighboring pixels — it only ever calls layer.sample(position) — which lets the system optimize.

    The shader signature

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> half4 bottomAmbientLighting(
    float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
    float2 size, float time, float progress, half4 tint
) { ... }

    <span class="xref-missing" title="note not in this site">stitchable</span> exposes it to ShaderLibrary; SwiftUI::Layer is the rasterized source; it returns a premultiplied half4 (color already scaled by alpha). The extra parameters after layer are the uniforms passed from Swift, in order.

    Stage 1 — coordinates & vertical falloff

    float2 uv = position / max(size, float2(1.0));   // 0..1 across the view
float bottomDistance = 1.0 - uv.y;               // 0 at bottom, 1 at top
float deepBloom  = exp(-bottomDistance * 4.4);   // bright near the bottom, decays fast
float upperMist  = exp(-bottomDistance * 3.0) * 0.2;
float verticalFade = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.0, 0.62, bottomDistance);

    Exponential falloff (exp(-d·k)) is the core of the glow — it's intense at the edge and fades smoothly upward, far more natural than a linear gradient. This is the shaping-function thinking from math for shaders.

    Stage 2 — wide overlapping sources (no single spotlight)

    float2 leftCenter   = float2(0.08 + horizontalFlow, 1.08);   // centers BELOW the screen (y>1)
float2 middleCenter = float2(0.52 - horizontalFlow*0.45, 1.12);
float2 rightCenter  = float2(0.94 + horizontalFlow*0.35, 1.06);
float sourceGlow = leftGlow*0.7 + middleGlow*0.82 + rightGlow*0.64;  // soft circles, summed

    Three soft circles (1 - smoothstep(0, r, distance)) placed just off-screen below the bottom edge, gently drifting via horizontalFlow sines. Summing several wide, offset glows avoids a tell-tale single "flame/spotlight" shape — a key trick for believable ambient light. Soft circles + distance fields are the metaball/distance toolkit.

    Stage 3 — life: mist, breathing, ribbons

    float breathing = 0.92 + sin(time * 0.64) * 0.08;            // slow global pulse
float risingBands = 0.5 + sin(bottomDistance*15.0 - time*2.15
    + sin(wispWarp*9.0 + time*0.36) * 1.1) * 0.5;            // sin-of-sin = domain warp
float wisps = risingBands * exp(-bottomDistance * 3.2);

    Layered sines at incommensurate frequencies (0.64, 2.15, 0.36…) make it feel alive and never visibly loop — the summed-sine idea from your interaction-math noise & patterns. Feeding one sin into another's phase is cheap domain warping, the same technique in distortion effects.

    Stage 4 — the organic upward reveal

    float revealHeight = mix(0.0, 0.86, revealProgress);
float revealWarp   = sin(uv.x*5.0 - time*1.4)*0.055 + sin(uv.x*13.0 + time*0.86)*0.025;
float organicReveal = 1.0 - smoothstep(revealHeight - 0.14, revealHeight + 0.1,
                                       bottomDistance + revealWarp);

    Instead of revealing a rectangular mask, progress moves a wavy boundary upward: the smoothstep edge is perturbed by revealWarp, so the light grows in with an organic, liquid front. progress is the gesture/animation handle the Swift side animates.

    Stage 5 — particles: light pockets & motes (per-pixel loops)

    for (ushort i = 0; i < 6; i++) {                 // 6 drifting "light pockets"
    float pr = fract(time*speed + hash(seed));   // each loops on its own clock
    float pocketY = mix(1.14, -0.16, pr);        // rises from below to above
    lightPockets += exp(-dot(p, p) * 1.8) * fade;// gaussian blob, fades in/out
}
// ... and 18 tiny "motes" with aspect-corrected soft circles

    Each particle is hashed (fract(sin(seed)*43758.5453) — the classic GPU hash) for position/speed variation, rises on its own fract(time·speed) clock, and fades in/out at the ends of its life. Loops-as-particles is a standard fragment-shader pattern (fragment shaders); moteSpace = float2(uv.x*aspectRatio, uv.y) keeps circles round on non-square screens.

    Stage 6 — compose & output (premultiplied alpha)

    half volumeAlpha = half(saturate(volume * 0.48));
half sourceMask  = layer.sample(position).a;     // confine to the view's bounds
volumeAlpha *= tint.a * sourceMask;
half alpha = saturate(volumeAlpha + moteAlpha);
return half4(tint.rgb * volumeAlpha + half3(1.0) * moteAlpha, alpha);  // premultiplied

    The glow takes the tint color; the motes are white. The output is premultiplied (rgb already scaled by alpha), which is what layerEffect expects — get this wrong and you get dark fringes (blending & compositing).

    The Swift orchestration

    The reveal is animated entirely on the SwiftUI side by ramping progress, with a staged sequence and accessibility respected:

    withAnimation(.easeOut(duration: 1.15)) { lightReveal = 1 }       // grow the light in
try? await Task.sleep(for: .milliseconds(280))
withAnimation(.spring(duration: 0.78, bounce: 0.16)) { isContentVisible = true }

    It checks @Environment(\.accessibilityReduceMotion) and jumps straight to the final state when reduced — the same courtesy as good shader hygiene and our accessibility notes. The demo's chrome uses glassEffect (Liquid Glass).

    Techniques catalog (what to steal)

    Technique Where it's taught
    Exponential falloff exp(-d·k) 05-math-for-shaders
    Soft circles via smoothstep(distance) 05-math-for-shaders, 04-metaball
    Summed/incommensurate sines, domain warp 15-noise-and-patterns, 14-distortion-effects
    fract(sin(x)*43758.5) hash for variation 15-noise-and-patterns
    Loops as particles 06-fragment-shaders
    Aspect correction 04-coordinates-and-color
    layerEffect + SwiftUI::Layer + time uniform 18-metal-in-swiftui, 23-animation-in-shaders
    Premultiplied-alpha output 16-blending-and-compositing

    Why it's clever

    No textures, no image assets, no particle system objects — just one stitchable shader on a white rectangle, with all the organic shape coming from exponential falloffs, summed sines, and hashed loops. The SwiftUI side stays tiny (drive time + progress), which makes the effect trivially reusable and tintable. It's a perfect study piece for how far you can get with the shaping-function toolkit alone.

    The foundation

    • layerEffect = a SwiftUI modifier that hands your view's pixels to a Metal shader, which gets to repaint each one.
    • exponential falloff (exp(-d·k)) = the "light fades as you move away" curve — bright at the edge, fading fast and smooth, the way real glow does.
    • soft circle = a fuzzy-edged blob made with smoothstep on distance; sum a few placed just off-screen and you get ambient light with no single hot-spot.
    • uniform = a value (time, progress, tint) you pass from Swift into the shader; it's the same for every pixel this frame.
    • wrapped time = keeping the clock value small (truncatingRemainder) so sin/fract stay precise even after hours of running.
    • domain warp = feeding one wave into another wave's input, so the motion looks organic instead of a clean repeating ripple.
    • premultiplied alpha = color values already multiplied by their transparency; it's the format layerEffect expects, and getting it wrong gives dark fringes.

    Related

    • The SwiftUI shader entry point: 18-metal-in-swiftui
    • WWDC26 on this exact pattern: Compose Advanced Graphics Effects
