    Liquid Glass — the Math Behind It

    Start here — the plain idea

    Liquid Glass is that iOS 26 look where a panel sits over your content like a real slab of glass — the stuff behind it bends at the edges, catches a faint rainbow fringe, blurs a little, and the rim glows. Apple's real shader is secret, but the recipe is no mystery.

    Here's the whole trick in one sentence: for every pixel, figure out one number — how far am I from the edge of the glass shape — and derive everything from it. That number comes from a Signed Distance Field (SDF), think of it as a built-in ruler that tells each pixel its distance to the nearest edge (negative if it's inside the glass).

    Once you have that ruler, real glass behaviors fall out cheaply. To fake light bending, you just read the background from a slightly shifted spot, and shift it more near the edges (where a lens bends most). Read red, green, and blue from slightly different shifts and you get the prism fringe. Brighten where the distance is near zero and you get the glowing rim. Blur the background and it's frosted. One good number, reused four ways.

    Apple's Liquid Glass (iOS 26+) looks like a real pane of glass floating over your content: the background bends at its edges, picks up a faint prismatic fringe, blurs softly, and catches a bright rim of light. Apple's exact shader is private, but the look is a well-understood stack of graphics-math tricks you can recreate. This breaks down the recipe.

    The five ingredients

    1. Shape      → a rounded-rect Signed Distance Field (SDF)
2. Refraction → offset the background sample along the SDF's gradient (fake Snell's law)
3. Dispersion → sample R/G/B at slightly different offsets (chromatic aberration), edges only
4. Fresnel    → brighten the rim (glass reflects most at grazing angles)
5. Blur       → soften what shows through (frosted glass)

    Everything keys off one number per pixel: the signed distance to the glass shape.

    1. The shape: a rounded-rect SDF

    A signed distance field returns, for any point, the distance to the shape's surface — negative inside, zero on the edge, positive outside. For a rounded rectangle (half-size b, corner radius r):

    float sdRoundedRect(float2 p, float2 b, float r) {
    float2 q = abs(p) - b + r;
    return min(max(q.x, q.y), 0.0) + length(max(q, 0.0)) - r;
}

    This is the same SDF toolkit as 04-metaball and the shaping functions — one cheap formula gives you the shape and a smooth notion of "how close am I to the edge," which every other step needs.

    2. Refraction: bend the background

    Real glass bends light by Snell's law. In a shader you fake it: instead of bending light, you move where you read the background from. The direction to push is the gradient of the SDF (which points straight out of the surface — the normal); the amount is largest at the edges, where a lens bends most:

    float d = sdRoundedRect(p, b, r);
float2 n = normalize(gradient(d));            // SDF gradient = surface normal
float edge = exp(d / falloff);                // d<0 inside; ~1 at rim, ~0 in the center
float2 uv2 = uv + n * strength * edge;        // read the background from a shifted spot
half4 color = background.sample(uv2);

    You compute the gradient with a tiny finite difference (sample the SDF a hair to each side and subtract) — the same "derivative = direction" idea from the derivative chain. The flat center barely shifts; the curved rim shifts a lot — exactly how a real lens behaves, and why straight lines in the background bow as they pass under the edge.

    3. Dispersion: edge chromatic aberration

    A prism splits white light because each color bends by a slightly different amount. Reproduce it by sampling the three channels at slightly different offsets, and only near the edge (where bending is strong):

    float r = background.sample(uv + n * off * 1.10).r;
float g = background.sample(uv + n * off       ).g;
float b = background.sample(uv + n * off * 0.90).b;
half3 color = half3(r, g, b);

    That faint rainbow fringe on the rim is what sells "this is glass" rather than "this is a blur."

    4. Fresnel: the bright rim

    Glass reflects very little when you look straight through it, but almost like a mirror at grazing angles — the Fresnel effect. On a flat panel there's no view angle to use, so the look is approximated as an edge highlight: brightness that spikes where |d| is near zero.

    float rim = exp(d / (falloff * 0.35)) * rimStrength;   // bright hairline at the edge
color += rim;

    5. Blur

    Frosted glass scatters light, so the background that shows through is blurred — the multi-tap averaging from 13-blur-effects. In SwiftUI you often get this for free by putting the shader over a .blur/material layer, then doing refraction on top.

    Putting it together (SwiftUI)

    This is a layerEffect over whatever is behind it — same plumbing as Metal in SwiftUI and the WWDC26 compose graphics effects session. The shader receives the rasterized background as SwiftUI::Layer and the panel size; everything above happens per pixel.

    Try it

    The demo above is this exact pipeline in 2D: a rounded-rect SDF over a grid background, with sliders for refraction strength, corner radius, and edge falloff, plus a chromatic-aberration toggle. Watch the grid lines bow at the rim and the colors fringe.

    Why it's a great study piece

    Liquid Glass is one SDF reused four ways — shape, refraction direction, dispersion amount, and rim light all fall out of the single distance value. That economy (compute one good number, derive everything from it) is the same lesson as the bottom ambient light and the whole model-it-as-a-function mindset.

    The foundation

    • SDF (signed distance field) = a per-pixel ruler: distance to the shape's edge, negative inside, zero on the edge, positive outside.
    • SDF gradient = the direction pointing straight out of the surface (the normal), found by sampling the SDF a hair to each side — it's which way to push the refraction.
    • refraction (faked) = bending light by reading the background from a shifted spot instead, shifted most near the edges, so straight lines bow.
    • chromatic aberration / dispersion = sampling R, G, B at slightly different offsets so the rim gets a prism-like rainbow fringe.
    • Fresnel = glass reflecting more at grazing angles; on a flat panel it's approximated as a bright hairline where the distance is near zero.
    • blur = softening the background that shows through, the frosted-glass part — multi-tap averaging from the blur lesson.

    Related

    • SDFs & shaping: 04-metaball, 05-math-for-shaders

    Apple-Intelligence / Siri Glow — Flowing Edge Light

    Start here — the plain idea

    This is the colorful light that runs around the border of the screen when Siri or Apple Intelligence is listening — the modern replacement for the old Siri orb. Like the other glow effects, it's drawn entirely with math, no images.

    The recipe is just three moves. First, a mask: each pixel measures how far it is from the nearest screen edge, and only pixels near the border get to glow (bright at the edge, fading to nothing toward the center). Second, color that travels: instead of a fixed hue, the color sweeps around the perimeter over time, with a couple of overlapping waves layered in so it shimmers and never looks mechanical. Third, bloom: paint that color onto black and let overlapping light add up, so it reads as soft luminous glow.

    Once you notice the pattern — distance mask + flowing color + additive bloom — you've basically got the whole family of Apple glow effects. Move the mask to the bottom edge and it's the ambient light from the previous lesson; wrap it around the border and it's this.

    When Siri / Apple Intelligence is listening, a soft multi-colour light flows around the edges of the screen. It's the spiritual successor to the old Siri orb. The math is surprisingly approachable — a screen-edge mask, a hue that flows around the perimeter over time, and additive bloom. (Apple's exact version is private; this is how you recreate the feel.)

    The three ingredients

    1. Mask   → distance to the nearest screen edge → a glow that hugs the border
2. Colour → a hue that flows around the perimeter, animated over time + a little noise
3. Bloom  → add it onto black so overlapping light brightens (additive)

    1. The edge mask

    The glow lives near the border, so the key quantity is how far each pixel is from the nearest edge. In normalized uv (0–1), that's just:

    float edgeDist = min(min(uv.x, 1.0 - uv.x), min(uv.y, 1.0 - uv.y));
float glow = exp(-edgeDist / thickness);     // 1 at the edge, fading inward

    exp(-d/thickness) is the same exponential falloff as the ambient light and shaping functions — bright at the border, smoothly gone by the center. (For real screens you'd build this from a rounded-rect SDF, like Liquid Glass, so the glow rounds the corners.)

    2. Colour that flows around the edge

    The signature move is colour traveling around the perimeter. Parameterize position around the border by the angle from center, then make the hue a function of that angle plus time:

    float ang = atan2(uv.y - 0.5, uv.x - 0.5) / (2.0 * M_PI_F) + 0.5;   // 0..1 around
float flow = 0.12 * sin(ang * 18.0 + time * 1.3)
           + 0.08 * sin(ang * 7.0  - time * 0.8);                    // summed sines = shimmer
float hue = fract(ang + time * speed + flow);
half3 color = hsv2rgb(half3(hue, 0.85, 1.0));

    The ang + time*speed term sweeps the rainbow around the edge; the summed sines (incommensurate frequencies, 15-noise-and-patterns) add the living, non-mechanical shimmer — exactly the "gently alive, never looping" trick from your interaction-math oscillation & noise notes. Apple's real version reads more like a mesh gradient (a few moving colour anchors) than a pure hue sweep, but the principle — a small set of flowing colours mapped around the border — is the same.

    3. Bloom (additive)

    Output the colour multiplied by the glow mask onto black, so where light overlaps it adds and brightens — additive blending (16-blending-and-compositing):

    return half4(color * glow, glow);            // premultiplied; add over dark UI

    A second, wider, dimmer pass (larger thickness, lower intensity) gives the soft outer halo that real bloom has.

    Putting it together

    It's a full-screen layerEffect (or an overlay view) behind/around your content, driven by TimelineView(.animation) for time — same delivery as Metal in SwiftUI and animation in shaders. Fade the whole thing in/out with a progress uniform when Siri starts/stops, just like the ambient light's reveal.

    Try it

    The demo above is this recipe: edge-distance mask × flowing hue × bloom, with thickness, flow-speed, and intensity sliders. Crank the speed and watch the colour chase around the border.

    Why it's a great study piece

    It's the same three-part pattern as the ambient light (mask → flowing colour → bloom), just shaped to the screen border instead of the bottom. Once you see that "Apple glow effects = a distance mask + flowing colour + additive bloom," you can build the family — listening states, focus rings, notification reveals — from one mental template.

    The foundation

    • edge mask = each pixel's distance to the nearest screen edge, turned into "how much glow lives here" — bright at the border, gone by the center.
    • exponential falloff (exp(-d/thickness)) = the smooth fade from the edge inward; bigger thickness = a wider glow band.
    • flowing hue = mapping color to angle-around-the-border plus time, so the rainbow sweeps around the perimeter.
    • summed sines = a couple of overlapping waves at different frequencies added in, giving living shimmer that never visibly loops.
    • additive bloom = painting color onto black so overlapping light adds and brightens, the way real glow stacks.
    • progress uniform = a Swift-driven value to fade the whole effect in/out when Siri starts and stops listening.
